Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
8 min
Khiyar al-Ayb: The Option of Defect and Its Rulings in Hanafi Fiqh
Defines khiyar al-ayb, lists types of defects and conditions for recourse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions of khiyar al-ayb, apply the rule that the buyer may only keep at full price or return (no deduction), and distinguish between defects that require recurrence and those that do not, such as madness.

Core Idea
The buyer's option upon discovering a pre-existing defect is to keep at full price or return—no proportional deduction.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq lays out the foundational rulings of khiyar al-ayb: what constitutes a defect, when the option arises, and what remedies the buyer has. It also delves into specific defects like ibaq, theft, and madness, with nuanced conditions for minors and the special permanence-based rule for madness.

Guided Path
1
Define 'defect' and its categories
Identify the Hanafi definition of a defect ('ayb') as a departure from the original sound nature that reduces value per merchant custom. Note the two types: apparent (like blindness) and hidden (like cough).
The standard for identifying a defect is the custom of merchants ('urf al-tujjar).
2
List the conditions for khiyar al-ayb
Confirm that the defect existed with the seller, the buyer was unaware at sale/delivery, no action indicating satisfaction after knowledge, and no disclaimer by the seller.
All four conditions must be met for the option to exist.
3
State the primary remedy and its exceptions
The buyer may either keep the defective item at the full price or return it for a full refund. Note the two exceptions where proportional deduction is allowed: seller causes defect after sale but before delivery, or buyer acquires a new defect and seeks compensation for the original one.
The hadith 'Whoever cheats us is not from us' prohibits concealing defects.
4
Analyze specific defects in minors (ibaq, urinating in bed, theft)
Understand that for a discerning minor (mumayyiz), these acts are defects only if they occur during minority and recur in the same life stage (both in minority or both after puberty). If the stage changes, it is a different defect and no return.
Ibaq before puberty is due to play; after puberty, due to malice—different underlying cause.
5
Examine the special case of madness
Madness is a permanent defect of the inner self. The correct Hanafi view is that it need not recur at the buyer; the buyer may return even if the slave had madness at the seller during minority and later manifests after puberty at the buyer.
This is due to the hadith 'Whoever becomes mad never recovers' and the principle that the cause (corruption of the inner faculty) is the same regardless of age.

باب خيار العيب
أي في بيان أحكامه
من وجد بالمبيع عيبا أخذه بكل الثمن أورده وما أوجب نقصان الثمن عند ينقض به الثمن التجار عيب كالإباق والبول في الفراش عيب أي فهو عيب: أي كل شيء الأثمان، باب خيار العيب من إضافة الشيء إلى سببه، وهو نقص خلا عنه أصل الفطرة السليمة، وهو نوعان: ظاهري كالعمى والماء في العين، وباطني كالسعال وانقطاع الحيض شهرين فصاعداً، والإباق ونحوها، والمراد بالعيب ، كان عند البائع ولم يره المشتري عند البيع ولا عند القبض، ولم يوجد من المشتري ما يدل على الرضاء به بعد العلم بالعيب، ولم يكن البائع شرط البراءة منه خاصاً أو عاماً. (مسكين، فتح) أخذه بكل الثمن أو ردّه أي إن وجد بالمبيع عيباً ينقص به الثمن وكان عند البائع وقبضه من غير أن يعلم به و لم يوجد منه ما يدل على الرضاء بالعيب فهو مخير إن شاء أخذ المبيع المعيب بكل الثمن أو رده على البائع؛ لأن السلامة مطلق العقد يقتضي فكانت السلامة كالمشروطة في العقد صريحا، فعند فواتها يتخير كفوات من العيب، الوصف المرغوب المشروط في العقد، ولا ينقص من الثمن شيئاً؛ لأن الأوصاف لا يقابلها شيء من إلا إذا صارت الأوصاف مقصودة بالإتلاف بأن حدث العيب بفعل البائع بعد البيع قبل القبض حيث يسقط من الثمن بحصته إذا اختار الأخذ أو حدث عند المشتري عيب آخر حيث يكون له الرجوع بنقصان العيب على البائع كما سيجيء، ولكون السلامة كالمشروطة لا يحل كتمان العيب في بيع أو ثمن؛ لأن الغش حرام، قال عليه الصلاة والسلام: «من غشنا فليس منا». رمز الحقائق: ٢٠/٢] (فتح) أو رده على البائع، ومؤونة الرد على المشتري. (ع) ف) وما أوجب نقصان الثمن: وإن لم تنقص العين ولا المنفعة كالظفر الأسود الصحيح القوي على العمل؛ لأن الضرر اللاحق بسبب العيب هو النقصان في المالية، فيرجع في معرفة النقصان إلى أهله وهم التجار وأرباب الصنائع، ولابد أن يكون العيب غير مختلف عندهم سواء أخبر الجميع بأنه عيب أو البعض فقط، ويكتفى بقول عدلين منهم رمز الحقائق: ٢١/٢] (فتح) عند التجار بضم التاء مع التشديد وبكسرها مع التخفيف جمع تاجر أو عند أرباب الصنائع إن كان المبيع من المصنوعات. (ف) كالإباق هو فرار العبد والجارية من المولى من غير ظلمه، فإن كان بظلمه سمي هروباً، فعلى هذا الإباق عيب والهرب ليس بعيب. (ف)] مطلقاً سواء كان الفرار من المولى أو ممن في يده بإجارة أو إعارة أو وديعة وإن كان فيما دون السفر، أما إذا غصبه ،رجل، فأبق منه إلى منزل ،مولاه فليس بعيب كما إذا أبق من المشتري إلى البائع و لم يختف عنده، وكانا في قرية واحدة في الثور، والأحسن أنه عيب. (ط، عيني) من .والسرقة والجنون والبخر .والسرقة: أي إذا وجدت هذه الأشياء من صغير غير عاقل لا تكون عيباً، وفي الصغير العاقل ذكراً كان أو أنثى وهو الذي يأكل وحده ويشرب وحده تكون عيباً، فلو فر الصغير الذي لا يعقل فهو ضال لا آبق، وهذه عيوب في الصغير العاقل ما لم يبلغ فأما إذا بلغ فليس ذلك الماضي بعيب حتى يعاوده بعد البلوغ في يد البائع، ثم يبيعه ويعاوده في يد المشتري، ومعنى هذا: أنه إذا ظهرت هذه العيوب عند البائع في صغره، ثم حدثت عند المشتري في صغره فهو عيب وله الرد بذلك، وإذا وجدت هذه الأشياء في صغره فباعه، فوجدت عند المشتري بعد البلوغ لم يرده؛ لأن الإباق قبل البلوغ لحب اللعب، والبول في الفراش قبله لضعف في المثانة والسرقة قبله لقلة المبالاة، والإباق والسرقة بعد البلوغ يكونان لخبث في الباطن. والبول بعده تكون لداء في الباطن وسوء اختيار وهو عيب آخر، فعند اتحاد الحالة بأن يثبت الإباق وغيره عند بائعه ثم مشتريه كلاهما في صغره أو كبره، له الردّ؛ لاتحاد الحالة، وعند الاختلاف ليس له الرد؛ لكونه عيباً آخر كعبد حمّ عند بائعه، ثم حمّ عند المشتري، فإن كان هذه الحمى الثانية غير النوع الأول لا يثبت حق الرد، وإن كان من نوعه يثبت حق الرد والسرقة لا تختلف بين أن يكون من المولى أو من غيره إلا إذا سرق المأكول للأكل، فليس بعيب، ولو سرق للبيع فهو عيب، ولو سرق شيئاً يسيراً وهو ما دون الدرهم لا يكون عيباً، ولو نقب البيت يكون عيباً وإن لم يأخذ شيئاً. (عيني، فتح) والجنون [لأنه فساد في لباطن ولا يختلف صغراً وكبراً. (عيني)] وهو لا يختلف باختلاف السنين حتى لو وجد عند البائع في صغره، وعاد عند المشتري بعد الكبر يرده؛ لأنه لا يشترط فيه اتحاد الحالتين؛ لأن السبب فيهما واحد، وهو فساد الباطن، فالصغر والكبر فيه سواء، وتكلم المشايخ في قدره، قال بعضهم: أقله ساعة، وقال بعضهم: إن كان أكثر من يوم وليلة فهو عيب وقال بعضهم: الجنون المطبق بفتح الموحدة عيب، وغيره ليس بعيب، وخير الأمور أوسطها يعني أكثر من يوم وليلة، وإذا ثبت وجوده عند البائع هل يشترط أن يوجد ثانياً عند المشتري؟ اختلف المشايخ فيه قال بعضهم لا يشترط؛ لأن الجنون عيب لازم أبداً؛ لقوله عليه: "من جنّ لم يفق أبداً" يعني لم يزل عنه صفة الجنون، فللمشتري حق الرد ولو لم يعاود عنده. وقال بعضهم: يشترط ولا يكون له حق الرد ما لم يوجد ثانياً عند المشتري كما في الإباق ونظائره، إلا أن الفرق بينهما أن في الجنون لا يشترط اتحاد الحالة أي الصغر والكبر، والقول الأول هو الصحيح؛ لأن الله تعالى قادر على إزالة الجنون بآثاره فلا يرده ما لم يعاود عنده وبه قال الجمهور والجنون اختلال القوة التي بها إدراك الكليات. والعقل هو القوة المذكورة، ومعدنه القلب، وشعاعه في الدماغ. (عيني، فتح) والبخر بالخاء المعجمة وبالتحريك نتن الفم، وعبارة القاموس تفيد أن البخر أعم من نتن ريح الفم والأنف والإبط. ثم اعلم أن البخر الذي هو عيب هو الناشي من تغير المعدة، دون ما يكون من تباعد ما بين الأسنان فإن ذلك يزول بتنظيفها، وأما البحر بالجيم هو انتفاخ تحت السرة، فعيب في الغلام والجارية. (فتح) ساعة
Chapter of the Option of Defect. Explanation of its rulings. Whoever finds a defect in the sold item takes it with the full price or returns it. And what necessitates a decrease in price among merchants is a defect, such as running away and urinating in bed. Defect: meaning everything that reduces the price. Chapter of the Option of Defect is an addition of a thing to its cause. It is a deficiency absent from the original sound nature, and it is of two types: apparent like blindness and water in the eye, and hidden like coughing, cessation of menses for two months or more, running away and the like. The intended meaning of defect is that it existed with the seller, the buyer did not see it at the time of sale or at the time of taking possession, and there was nothing from the buyer indicating satisfaction with it after knowledge of the defect, and the seller did not stipulate immunity from it specifically or generally. (Miskeen, Fath) He takes it with the full price or returns it: meaning if he finds in the sold item a defect that diminishes the price, and it existed with the seller, and he took possession without knowing it, and there was nothing from him indicating satisfaction with the defect, then he has the choice: if he wishes he takes the defective sold item with the full price or returns it to the seller. Because the contract absolutely implies safety, so safety is like explicitly stipulated in the contract. Thus when it is lost, he has a choice like when a desired attribute stipulated in the contract is lost. And he does not deduct anything from the price because attributes are not compensated by anything except when the attributes are intended through destruction, such as if the defect occurred by the seller's action after the sale but before taking possession, then a proportional amount is deducted from the price if he chooses to take it, or if another defect occurs with the buyer, he may recourse for the decrease due to the defect upon the seller as will come. And because safety is like stipulated, it is not permissible to conceal a defect in a sale or price because cheating is forbidden. He (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever cheats us is not from us.' (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 20/2) (Fath) Or he returns it to the seller, and the expense of returning is upon the buyer. (Ayni, Fath) And what necessitates a decrease in price: even if the essence or benefit does not diminish, like a black nail that is healthy and strong for work, because the harm due to the defect is the decrease in value. So recourse is to its people, i.e., the merchants and craftsmen. And the defect must not be disputed among them, whether all inform that it is a defect or only some, and the statement of two just persons among them is sufficient. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 21/2) (Fath) Among merchants (plural of tajir) or among craftsmen if the sold item is a manufactured good. (Fath) Like running away (ibaq): it is the escape of a male or female slave from the master without oppression. If it is due to oppression, it is called flight (hurub). Thus ibaq is a defect and hurub is not a defect. (Fath) Absolutely, whether the escape is from the master or from one in whose possession he is by lease, loan, or trust, even if it is less than a journey. But if a man usurps him, and he escapes from him to his master's house, then it is not a defect, just as if he escapes from the buyer to the seller without hiding, and they are in the same village in Thawr? The better view is that it is a defect. (Fath) And urinating in bed: because these actions cause a decrease in value according to merchants, and they are defects in a discerning minor. (Tahawi, Ayni) From. And theft, madness, and halitosis (bakh). Theft: if these things occur from a non-rational minor, they are not defects. In a rational minor, male or female, who eats and drinks alone, they are defects. If a non-rational minor runs away, he is a stray (dal) not a runaway (abiq). These are defects in a rational minor until he reaches puberty. Once he reaches puberty, that past action is not a defect unless it recurs after puberty in the seller's hand, then he sells it and it recurs in the buyer's hand. Meaning: if these defects appeared with the seller during his minority, then occurred with the buyer during his minority, it is a defect and he has the right to return due to it. If these things occurred during his minority, then he sold it, and they were found with the buyer after puberty, he cannot return it because ibaq before puberty is due to love of play, urinating in bed before it is due to bladder weakness, theft before it is due to carelessness. Ibaq and theft after puberty are due to internal malice. Urinating after it is due to an internal disease and poor choice, which is another defect. So when the situation is uniform, i.e., ibaq etc. is confirmed with his seller then his buyer, both during his minority or both after puberty, he has the right to return due to uniformity. When there is difference, he has no right to return because it is another defect, like a slave who had fever with his seller then fever with the buyer; if this second fever is not of the first type, the right to return is not established; if it is of the same type, it is established. Theft does not differ between stealing from the master or from others, except if he steals food to eat, it is not a defect. If he steals to sell, it is a defect. If he steals a little, less than a dirham, it is not a defect. If he breaks into a house, it is a defect even if he does not take anything. (Ayni, Fath) Madness: because it is a corruption of the inner self and does not differ between minority and majority. (Ayni) It does not differ by age, so if it was found with the seller during his minority and recurred with the buyer after majority, he returns it because uniformity of the two situations is not required; the cause is one, i.e., inner corruption, so minority and majority are the same in it. The scholars discussed its extent. Some said: its minimum is an hour. Some said: if it is more than a day and night, it is a defect. Some said: continuous madness is a defect, and other is not a defect. The best of matters is the middle, i.e., more than a day and night. If its existence at the seller is confirmed, is it necessary that it occurs again at the buyer? The scholars differed: some said it is not necessary because madness is a permanent defect, due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever becomes mad never recovers,' meaning the attribute of madness never leaves him, so the buyer has the right to return even if it does not recur with him. Some said it is necessary, and he has no right to return unless it occurs again at the buyer like ibaq and its analogues, except the difference between them is that in madness uniformity of situation (minority and majority) is not required. The first opinion is the correct one because Allah is able to remove madness, so he does not return unless it recurs with him, and the majority said this. Madness is the derangement of the faculty by which generalities are perceived. Intellect is that faculty, its seat is the heart, and its ray is in the brain. (Ayni, Fath) And halitosis (bakh) with mu'jam kha and fath, it is stench of the mouth. The expression of al-Qamus indicates that bakh is more general than stench of the mouth, nose, and armpit. Then know that bakh which is a defect is that arising from stomach disorder, not that from gaps between teeth, for that goes away with cleaning. As for 'bahr' with jim, it is a swelling under the navel, a defect in a boy or girl. (Fath) Sa'ah.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary on the chapter of Khiyar al-Ayb (Option of Defect) from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It first defines the nature of a defect (ayb): a deficiency that departs from the original sound creation, of two types—apparent (like blindness or water in the eye) and hidden (like cough, amenorrhea for two months or more, running away, etc.). The defect must have existed with the seller, not known to the buyer at the time of sale or delivery, and the buyer must not have acted in a way indicating acceptance after knowledge, and the seller must not have disclaimed liability. The core ruling: the buyer may either take the defective item at the full price or return it; he cannot deduct a portion of the price. This is because the contract of sale inherently implies freedom from defects (salamah), so it is treated as if it were a stipulation. If that implied condition is absent, the buyer has an option, analogous to when an explicitly stipulated desirable attribute is missing. The only exceptions are when the seller caused the defect after the sale but before delivery, in which case a proportional deduction is allowed, or when another defect occurs with the buyer—then he may seek compensation for the original defect. Concealing a defect is forbidden due to the hadith: 'Whoever cheats us is not from us.' The passage then explains that the standard for recognizing a defect is the custom of merchants or craftsmen; their declaration (by two just witnesses) is taken. Specific defects are discussed: ibaq (running away) is a defect unless it is due to oppression (then it is hurub, not a defect). For minors, actions like ibaq, urinating in bed, theft, and madness are considered defects only if the minor is discerning (mumayyiz) and the actions occur during minority. If they occur only during minority and not after puberty, they are not defects because the motive differs (play vs. malice). The answer for madness is different: it is a defect regardless of age because it stems from internal corruption, and the buyer may return even if it does not recur if it was present at the seller, according to the correct opinion. The duration of madness: the best view is that it must last more than a day and night to be a defect. Halitosis (bakh) is a defect if due to stomach disorder, not merely unclean teeth; swelling under the navel (bahr) is a defect.

Key Terms
العيب (al-'ayb): A deficiency in the sold item that departs from the original sound nature (fitrah salimah), causing a decrease in value according to merchants. It may be apparent (like blindness) or hidden (like cough).
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option granted to the buyer upon discovering a pre-existing defect in the purchased item; he may either keep it at the full price or return it to the seller.
الإباق (al-ibaq): The escape of a slave from the master without oppression. Considered a defect if it occurs without the master's injustice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what are the buyer's remedies upon discovering a pre-existing defect in a purchased item?
A) The buyer may demand a proportional refund and keep the item.
B) The buyer may either return the item for a full refund or keep it at the full price.
C) The buyer must keep the item and has no recourse.
D) The buyer may only return the item if the seller knew of the defect.

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-ayb grants the buyer a binary choice (keep at full price or return) when a pre-existing defect is discovered; no proportional deduction, and madness is treated as a permanent defect not requiring recurrence.

Theft as a Defect in Slaves: Conditions and Recurrence
Conditions for theft as a defect in slave sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which theft in a slave constitutes a defect allowing return, and apply the rule of recurrence across stages of life.

Core Idea
Theft is a defect only if committed by a rational minor or adult, with recurrence in the same stage of life, and if the stolen amount is at least a dirham.

In Hanafi sales law, the right to return a slave due to a defect requires careful analysis of the defect's nature and timing. The classic text of Kanz al-Daqaiq, expanded by later commentators, establishes that theft (saraqa) is a defect only under specific conditions related to the slave's rationality, age, recurrence, and the value and purpose of the theft. This lesson unpacks these conditions so that you can apply them correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The text establishes conditions under which theft in a slave is considered a defect (ayb) giving the buyer the right to return (khiyar al-ayb). Your task is to extract these conditions and apply them.
2
Distinguish rationality
Theft is a defect only for a rational minor (saghir aqil) and for adults; it is not a defect for a non-rational minor. Identify the criterion for rationality: the ability to eat and drink independently.
Non-rational minors are considered lost (dall), not absconding (abiq).
3
Apply the recurrence condition
For the defect to be actionable, it must occur in the same stage of life (childhood or adulthood) in both the seller's and buyer's possession. Different stages break the link because the underlying cause changes (e.g., play vs. wickedness).
If theft appears after puberty, it is a new defect unless it recurs after puberty in the buyer's possession.
4
Account for value and purpose
Stealing less than a dirham is not a defect. Stealing food for eating is not a defect, but stealing for sale is. Breaking into a house is a defect even without taking anything.
These are specific exceptions to the general rule.
5
Synthesize the ruling
Combine all conditions: the theft must be from a rational minor or adult, recur in the same stage of life, involve at least a dirham (except burglary), and if food, be for sale. Apply these to a hypothetical scenario to determine the buyer's right to return.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسرقة: أي إذا وجدت هذه الأشياء من صغير غير عاقل لا تكون عيباً، وفي الصغير العاقل ذكراً كان أو أنثى وهو الذي يأكل وحده ويشرب وحده تكون عيباً، فلو فر الصغير الذي لا يعقل فهو ضال لا آبق، وهذه عيوب في الصغير العاقل ما لم يبلغ فأما إذا بلغ فليس ذلك الماضي بعيب حتى يعاوده بعد البلوغ في يد البائع، ثم يبيعه ويعاوده في يد المشتري، ومعنى هذا: أنه إذا ظهرت هذه العيوب عند البائع في صغره، ثم حدثت عند المشتري في صغره فهو عيب وله الرد بذلك، وإذا وجدت هذه الأشياء في صغره فباعه، فوجدت عند المشتري بعد البلوغ لم يرده؛ لأن الإباق قبل البلوغ لحب اللعب، والبول في الفراش قبله لضعف في المثانة والسرقة قبله لقلة المبالاة، والإباق والسرقة بعد البلوغ يكونان لخبث في الباطن. والبول بعده تكون لداء في الباطن وسوء اختيار وهو عيب آخر، فعند اتحاد الحالة بأن يثبت الإباق وغيره عند بائعه ثم مشتريه كلاهما في صغره أو كبره، له الردّ؛ لاتحاد الحالة، وعند الاختلاف ليس له الرد؛ لكونه عيباً آخر كعبد حمّ عند بائعه، ثم حمّ عند المشتري، فإن كان هذه الحمى الثانية غير النوع الأول لا يثبت حق الرد، وإن كان من نوعه يثبت حق الرد والسرقة لا تختلف بين أن يكون من المولى أو من غيره إلا إذا سرق المأكول للأكل، فليس بعيب، ولو سرق للبيع فهو عيب، ولو سرق شيئاً يسيراً وهو ما دون الدرهم لا يكون عيباً، ولو نقب البيت يكون عيباً وإن لم يأخذ شيئاً. (عيني، فتح)
And theft: meaning if these things are found from a minor without reason, they are not a defect. And in the minor with reason, male or female, who eats alone and drinks alone, they are a defect. If a minor without reason flees, he is just lost, not an absconder. These defects in the minor with reason until he reaches puberty are such that if he reaches puberty, that past is not a defect until it recurs after puberty in the seller's possession, then he sells him and it recurs in the buyer's possession. Meaning: if these defects appeared at the seller's during his childhood, then occurred at the buyer's during his childhood, it is a defect and he has the right to return. If these things were found in his childhood, then he sold him, and it was found at the buyer's after puberty, he does not return him; because absconding before puberty is due to love of play, bedwetting before it due to weakness of the bladder, theft before it due to carelessness; absconding and theft after puberty are due to inner wickedness. Bedwetting after it is due to an internal disease and bad choice, and it is another defect. So when the state is unified - that absconding etc. is confirmed at both seller and buyer both in childhood or both in adulthood - he has the right to return due to unified state. When there is a difference, he does not have the right to return because it is another defect, like a slave with fever at the seller then fever at the buyer: if the second fever is not of the first type, the right to return is not established; if it is of the same type, the right to return is established. And theft does not differ whether from the master or from others, except if he steals food for eating, it is not a defect. If he steals for sale, it is a defect. If he steals something insignificant, less than a dirham, it is not a defect. If he breaks into a house, it is a defect even if he takes nothing. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on theft as a defect in a slave for the purpose of returning the sale. The commentator expands on the matn stating that if these actions (theft, absconding, bedwetting) are found from a minor who is not rational (ghayr aqil), they are not defects. For a rational minor (saqir aqil), they are defects. The key condition is that the defect must recur in the buyer's possession in the same stage of life (childhood or adulthood) as it occurred in the seller's possession for the right of return to exist. If the stages differ, the right of return is lost because the underlying cause is different: pre-puberty it is due to play or carelessness, post-puberty it is due to inner wickedness. Theft is further detailed: it does not matter from whom it was stolen, but if stolen food is for eating it is not a defect, while if stolen for sale it is. Stealing less than a dirham is not a defect, but breaking into a house is a defect even if nothing is taken. The citations (Ayni, Fath) indicate these are from the commentaries al-Binayah and Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
عيب (ayb): A defect in a slave that gives the buyer the option to return the sale
صغير عاقل (saghir aqil): A minor who can eat and drink alone and is considered rational
سرقة (saraqa): Theft; one of the defects discussed in the passage
رد (radd): Return of the sold item due to a defect; buyer's right

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under which condition is theft in a slave NOT considered a defect?
A) When the slave is a rational minor
B) When the stolen amount is less than a dirham
C) When the slave steals food for sale
D) When the slave breaks into a house

Key Takeaway
A theft is a defect only if the slave is rational, the theft recurs in the same stage of life, the stolen amount is at least a dirham, and if food, it is stolen for sale not eating.

Insanity as a Defect: Recurrence Requirement
Ruling on insanity as a defect and recurrence requirement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on insanity as a defect in sales and explain why recurrence at the buyer's is not required.

Core Idea
Insanity is a continuous defect — once proven at the seller, the buyer may return the slave even without recurrence.

In Hanafi sales law, the buyer has the right to return a defective good (khiyar al-ayb). Insanity (junun) is a recognized defect, but the mashayikh disagreed on its definition and whether it must recur at the buyer's to justify return. This passage presents the ikhtilaf and gives the preferred position, supported by a hadith and a rational distinction from absconding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defect and its nature
Note that insanity is defined as 'dysfunction of the power of perception of universals' and is considered a corruption of the interior that does not differ by age.
The definition is important to understand why unity of state (childhood vs adulthood) is not required.
2
Determine the minimum duration for insanity to be a defect
The mashayikh gave different opinions: an hour, more than a day and night, or only continuous (mutbiq). The preferred view is 'more than a day and night' – the middle path.
The text states 'خير الأمور أوسطها يعني أكثر من يوم وليلة'
3
Understand the ikhtilaf on recurrence
The key dispute: is it necessary for the insanity to recur at the buyer's for him to have the right of return? Some said yes (like absconding), others said no.
The two opinions are clearly presented; the text favours the first (no recurrence required).
4
Analyze the dalil (hadith) and its legal implication
The first opinion cites the hadith 'Whoever becomes insane never recovers' – meaning insanity is a permanent attribute. Therefore, once confirmed at the seller, it is considered continuous; recurrence is not needed to prove the defect.
This hadith is a dalil: it proves that insanity is a lasting condition, not a temporary one.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi position
The text declares the first opinion (no recurrence) as 'sahih' and adds that Allah can remove insanity, so recurrence would be required if it were not continuous. The majority agrees.
The preferred position: insanity is a defect, and the buyer can return the slave even if it does not recur at his place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجنون [لأنه فساد في لباطن ولا يختلف صغراً وكبراً. (عيني)] وهو لا يختلف باختلاف السنين حتى لو وجد عند البائع في صغره، وعاد عند المشتري بعد الكبر يرده؛ لأنه لا يشترط فيه اتحاد الحالتين؛ لأن السبب فيهما واحد، وهو فساد الباطن، فالصغر والكبر فيه سواء، وتكلم المشايخ في قدره، قال بعضهم: أقله ساعة، وقال بعضهم: إن كان أكثر من يوم وليلة فهو عيب وقال بعضهم: الجنون المطبق بفتح الموحدة عيب، وغيره ليس بعيب، وخير الأمور أوسطها يعني أكثر من يوم وليلة، وإذا ثبت وجوده عند البائع هل يشترط أن يوجد ثانياً عند المشتري؟ اختلف المشايخ فيه قال بعضهم لا يشترط؛ لأن الجنون عيب لازم أبداً؛ لقوله عليه: 'من جنّ لم يفق أبداً' يعني لم يزل عنه صفة الجنون، فللمشتري حق الرد ولو لم يعاود عنده. وقال بعضهم: يشترط ولا يكون له حق الرد ما لم يوجد ثانياً عند المشتري كما في الإباق ونظائره، إلا أن الفرق بينهما أن في الجنون لا يشترم رط اتحاد الحالة أي الصغر والكبر، والقول الأول هو الصحيح؛ لأن الله تعالى قادر على إزالة الجنون بآثاره فلا يرده ما لم يعاود عنده وبه قال الجمهور والجنون اختلال القوة التي بها إدراك الكليات. والعقل هو القوة المذكورة، ومعدنه القلب، وشعاعه في الدماغ. (عيني، فتح)
And insanity: because it is corruption in the interior and does not differ by smallness or bigness. (Ayni) It does not differ by age, so even if it was found at the seller during his childhood, and recurred at the buyer after adulthood, he returns him; because unity of state is not required; the cause is one, corruption of interior. So childhood and adulthood are the same. The masters spoke about its extent: some said its minimum is an hour; some said if it is more than a day and night it is a defect; some said continuous insanity (al-mutbiq) is a defect, other is not. The best is the middle, meaning more than a day and night. If its existence at the seller is confirmed, is it required that it occurs a second time at the buyer? The masters differed: some said it is not required because insanity is a necessary defect forever, due to his saying: 'Whoever becomes insane never recovers' meaning the attribute of insanity does not leave him, so the buyer has the right to return even if it did not recur with him. Some said it is required and he has no right to return unless it occurs a second time at the buyer, as in absconding and its likes, except the difference between them is that in insanity unity of state (childhood and adulthood) is not required. And the first statement is correct because Allah is able to remove insanity by its effects, so he does not return it unless it recurs with him, and the majority agreed. And insanity is the dysfunction of the power by which universals are perceived. And the intellect is the mentioned power, its origin is the heart, and its ray is in the brain. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage discusses insanity as a defect. It states that insanity is corruption of the interior and does not vary with age. Therefore, if a slave was insane at the seller's during childhood and becomes insane again at the buyer's after adulthood, the buyer can return him because the cause (inner corruption) is the same. The mashayikh differed on the minimum duration: some said an hour, some said more than a day and night, and the preferred view is more than a day and night (continuous insanity). A key ikhtilaf is whether recurrence at the buyer's is necessary. Some say no, based on the hadith 'Whoever becomes insane never recovers', meaning the defect is permanent; the buyer can return even without recurrence. Others say yes, required as in other defects. The text sides with the first view, reasoning that Allah can remove insanity so the defect must recur. It concludes with a definition: insanity is dysfunction of the faculty that perceives universals (al-kulliyat), with the heart as its seat and the brain as its instrument.

Key Terms
جنون (junun): Dysfunction of the power of perception of universals; a defect in slaves
مطبق (mutbiq): Continuous or permanent insanity

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view, is recurrence of insanity at the buyer's required for the buyer to return the slave?
A) Yes, recurrence is required.
B) No, recurrence is not required.
C) Only if the insanity lasted less than a day.
D) Only if the buyer requested it.

Key Takeaway
Insanity is considered a continuous defect in Hanafi fiqh; once proven at the seller, the buyer has the right to return the slave even if it does not recur.

Bad Breath (Bakhar) as a Defect in Sales
Definition and cause of bad breath as a defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between chronic bad breath (a legal defect) and temporary bad breath (not a defect) in the context of sale contracts, and identify the condition that makes it a defect.

Core Idea
Only chronic bad breath from stomach disorder is a defect; temporary causes like poor hygiene do not qualify.

In sales law, the buyer has the right to return a purchased item if a defect is discovered. But what counts as a defect? This lesson zooms in on one specific condition: bad breath (al-bakhar). The text clarifies that not all bad breath is a defect—only that with a persistent internal cause. This distinction protects both buyer and seller.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its general meaning
The word 'bakhar' (البخر) can refer to any stench from the mouth, nose, or armpit, as per al-Qamūs. Recognize its broader linguistic scope before narrowing to the legal context.
The key is that bakhar is more general than just mouth stench.
2
Extract the legal definition of the defect
The text explicitly states that the bakhar which is a 'ayb (defect) is the one 'al-nāshi' min taghayyur al-mi‘da'—arising from a stomach disorder. This is the core distinction.
The cause must be internal and chronic, not external.
3
Contrast with non‑defect causes
Note that bad breath caused by gaps between teeth (tabā‘ud mā bayn al-asnān) is not a defect because it is removable by cleaning. This opposite case reinforces the condition.
Temporary conditions that can be cured are not defects.
4
Distinguish the similar term 'bahr'
The text warns against confusing 'bakhar' with 'bahr' (with jīm), which means a swelling under the navel. Both are defects, but they are separate conditions.
Spelling and pronunciation matter in fiqh terminology.
5
Apply the rule to concrete scenarios
Given a case of bad breath in a purchased slave, determine if it originates from stomach disorder (defect) or from external factors (not a defect).
Always inquire into the cause before ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبخر بالخاء المعجمة وبالتحريك نتن الفم، وعبارة القاموس تفيد أن البخر أعم من نتن ريح الفم والأنف والإبط. ثم اعلم أن البخر الذي هو عيب هو الناشي من تغير المعدة، دون ما يكون من تباعد ما بين الأسنان فإن ذلك يزول بتنظيفها، وأما البحر بالجيم هو انتفاخ تحت السرة، فعيب في الغلام والجارية. (فتح)
And bakhar: with the khā' and with fatḥa, is stench of the mouth. The expression of al-Qāmūs indicates that bakhar is more general than stench of the air of the mouth, nose, and armpit. Then know that the bakhar that is a defect is that arising from a change in the stomach, not that from farness between the teeth, for that goes away by cleaning them. As for 'bahr' with the jīm, it is a swelling under the navel, a defect in both male and female slaves. (Fath)
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage defines bad breath (bakhar) as stench of the mouth, but notes that it can also refer to stench from nose or armpit. However, for it to be a defect in the context of sales, it must originate from a stomach disorder. Bad breath caused by gaps between teeth is not a defect because it can be cured by cleaning. The text also distinguishes 'bahr' (with a jim), which is a swelling under the navel, and that is considered a defect in both male and female slaves. This clarifies what conditions are considered permanent or medically significant defects giving the buyer a right to return.

Key Terms
البخر (al-bakhar): Bad breath that is a defect, specifically arising from a stomach disorder.
البحر (al-bahr): Swelling under the navel, considered a defect irrespective of gender.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

In Hanafi sales law, chronic bad breath from stomach disorder is a defect; temporary or removable causes are not.

Defects in Slaves: Dafar, Fornication, and Disbelief
Rulings on defects allowing return of slaves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which defects give the right to return a slave in a sale and distinguish the Hanafi positions on each, including the exception for fornication in females and the dispute over disbelief.

Core Idea
Dafar, fornication, and child of fornication are defects in female slaves; disbelief is a defect in both, but finding a Muslim slave when bought as a disbeliever is not a defect.

In this lesson, we examine three specific defects that may arise in slave transactions: armpit stench (dafar), fornication and its child, and disbelief. Each has nuanced rulings in the Hanafi school as to whether the buyer can return the slave. We will also explore the inter-madhhab dispute with the Shafi'i school regarding disbelief as a defect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defect
First, identify the specific defect being discussed: dafar (armpit stench), fornication, child of fornication, or disbelief.
Note that lack of menstruation is also mentioned but not elaborated.
2
Determine the affected slave
Check whether the defect applies only to female slaves, only to male slaves, or to both. For example, dafar and fornication are defects in female slaves, while disbelief applies to both.
Male slaves are generally not returned for these unless the defect impairs service.
3
Evaluate recurrence requirement
For most defects, recurrence (at the buyer's possession) is required to exercise the right of return. However, fornication in a female slave is an exception: recurrence is not required per Imam Muhammad's narration.
For male slaves, fornication is a defect only if habitual (more than twice).
4
Analyze the disbelief dispute
The Hanafi position: if a slave is bought as a disbeliever but found to be Muslim, it is not a defect because religious matters are not considered in transactions. The Shafi'i school disagrees, allowing return.
Hanafi proof: 'matters of religion are not considered in transactions.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والدفر: بفتح الدال المهملة وفتح الفاء وسكونها تتن الإبط. (عيني)] قال في "الجمهرة": الدفر هو النتن مطلقا، يقال رجل دافر، وامرأة دافرة، وأما الذفر بالذال المعجمة، فهو حدة الرائحة من طيب أو نتن، وربما خص به الطيب، فقيل: مسك أذفر، ويقال في نتن ريح الأنف أيضاً وصرح في البزازية" بأن نتن ريح الأنف عيب. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٢/٢] (فتح) وولده: [ أي ولد الزنا، وهذه الأربعة تكون عيبا في الأمة دون الغلام؛ لأنه يخل بالمقصود منها، وهو الاستفراش وطلب الولد. (عيني)] أي وكون المبيع ولد الزنا، فحذف المضاف والمضاف إليه. في الأمة: متعلق بالأربعة المذكورة؛ لأنه قد يراد منها الاستفراش. وهذه المعاني تمنع منه، بخلاف الغلام؛ لأنه للاستخدام، وهذه الأشياء لا تخل به، إلا أن يكون البخر والدفر فاحشا بحيث يمنع القرب من المولى، أو يكون الزنا عادة له بأن يتكرر منه أكثر من مرتين؛ لأن اتباع النساء مخل بالخدمة، واليسر عيب وهو الذي يعمل بشماله ولا يعمل بيمينه. والعيوب كلها لا بد لها من المعاودة عند المشتري حتى يرد بها إلا الزنا في الجارية، فإنه روي عن محمد في الأمالي : لو اشترى جارية بالغة، وقد كانت زنت عند البائع، فللمشتري أن يردها وإن لم يزن عنده؛ للحوق العار بالأولاد، وعند الشافعي الزنا في الغلام عيب كالسرقة رمز الحقائق: ٢٢/٢] (فتح) والكفر فيهما: [ أي الكفر عيب في الأمة والغلام (عيني)] أي الكفر بأقسامه، وكذا الرفض والاعتزال عيب في الغلام والجارية؛ لأن طبع المسلم يتنفر عن صحبة الكافر، فلو اشترى عبداً على أنه كافر، فوجده مسلماً لا لأنه زوال للعيب، وقال الشافعي يرده لفوات الوصف المرغوب فيه؛ لأن استعباد الكافر وإذلاله مطلوب للمسلم. رمز الحقائق: ۲۳/۲] ولنا أن هذا الأمر راجع إلى الديانة، ولا عبرة به في المعاملات. (فتح، عناية)
And dafar, and fornication, and its child in the female slave, and disbelief in both, and lack of menstruation. And dafar: with fatḥa of dāl and fatḥa of fā and its sukun, is stench of the armpit. (Ayni) Al-Jamhara said: dafar is stench absolutely; a man is dāfir, a woman is dāfira. As for dhafar with dhāl, it is sharpness of smell, either perfume or stench; sometimes it is specified for perfume; it is said musk adhfar. And it is also said for stench of the nose. And in al-Bazzāziyya it is stated that stench of the nose is a defect. [Ramz al-Ḥaqāʾiq: 22/2] (Fath) And its child: [i.e. the child of fornication; these four are defects in the female slave but not the male, because they impair the purpose of her, which is bed companionship and seeking children. (Ayni)] i.e. the sold item being a child of fornication, so the mudaf and mudaf ilayh are omitted. 'In the female slave' is connected to the mentioned four, because she may be intended for bed companionship. These meanings prevent that, unlike the male slave because he is for service, and these things do not impair it, except if the bad breath and dafar are excessive to the point of preventing closeness to the master, or if fornication becomes a habit for him repeating more than twice; because chasing women impairs service. And al-yasar is a defect, which is one who works with his left hand and does not work with his right. All defects require recurrence at the buyer to return by them, except fornication in the female slave; for it is related from Muhammad in al-Amālī: if someone buys an adult female slave, and she had fornicated at the seller, the buyer may return her even if she did not fornicate with him, due to the shame attaching to children. And according to al-Shāfiʿī, fornication in the male slave is a defect like theft. [Ramz al-Ḥaqāʾiq: 22/2] (Fath) And disbelief in both: [i.e. disbelief is a defect in the female and male slave. (Ayni)] i.e. disbelief in all its forms; likewise rafd and iʿtizāl are defects in male and female slaves, because the nature of a Muslim is averse to accompanying a disbeliever. So if one buys a slave assuming he is a disbeliever, and finds him Muslim, it is not a defect (rather removal of defect). Al-Shāfiʿī said he returns him because of loss of the desirable description; because enslaving and humiliating a disbeliever is sought by a Muslim. [Ramz al-Ḥaqāʾiq: 23/2] Our argument: this matter is related to religion, and it is not considered in transactions. (Fath, ʿInāya)
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage covers several defects: dafar (armpit odor), the child of fornication, and disbelief. For dafar, it is stench (any kind), and it is a defect. For the child of fornication, it is a defect in a female slave because it impairs the purpose of companionship and childbearing. For male slaves, these are generally not defects unless they are excessive. Fornication itself is a defect in a female slave even without recurrence, based on a narration from Imam Muhammad. Disbelief is a defect in both genders because a Muslim's nature is averse to it. However, if a slave is bought as a disbeliever and found to be Muslim, it is not a defect but the removal of a defect, so the buyer cannot return him according to the Hanafi school, contrary to al-Shāfiʿī. The Hanafi position is that such religious matters are not considered in transactions.

Key Terms
الدفر (ad-dafar): Stench, specifically of the armpit; a defect
الزنا (az-zina): Fornication; a defect in female slaves, and in males if habitual
الكفر (al-kufr): Disbelief; a defect in both male and female slaves

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, always
B) Yes, if he fornicated more than twice at the seller's place
C) No, never
D) Yes, but only if he is a male slave

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between defects that impair the purpose of the slave and matters of religion; only the former give a right of return.

Lack of Menstruation as a Defect: Age Limits and Cause
Primary ruling and age dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which lack of menstruation is considered a defect in a female slave and identify the age of menopause according to Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Lack of menstruation is a defect only if the woman is of menstruating age and the cessation is pathological.

In sales of female slaves, a buyer may return the item if a hidden defect is discovered. This lesson examines when the lack of menstruation qualifies as such a defect, focusing on the age of the woman and the necessity of a pathological cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the buyer's right to return the female slave due to lack of menstruation as a defect. The ruling is that the return is valid (sahih) if the conditions are met.
2
Locate the implicit dalil
The passage states that continuous cessation in an adult woman is a sign of disease. This is the proof that it is a defect.
The dalil is the sign of disease, indicating a hidden defect.
3
Trace the age dispute
The age of menopause determines when cessation is natural. Abu Hanifa says 17 years, the Sahibayn say 15.
This affects whether the lack is a defect or natural menopause.
4
Note the condition of awareness
If the buyer knew the woman was menopausal, lack is not a defect. If he bought expecting menstruation and she is menopausal, he can return.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعدم الحيض: لأن ارتفاع الدم مستمرا في البالغة علامة الداء والمعتبر في انقطاعه أقصى غاية البلوغ، وهو سبع عشرة سنة عند أبي حنيفة، وخمس عشرة عندهما والحاصل أن عدم الحيض لا يكون عيباً عند عدم الإمكان؛ لكونها صغيرة أو آئسة إذا كان عالماً ،بأيامها، أما إذا اشتراها على أنها تحيض، فإذا هي آئسة له الرد، ويعرف الانقطاع بقول الأمة؛ لأنه لا يعرفه غيرها، ويستحلف البائع مع ذلك إن كان بعد القبض، فترد بنكوله، وإن كان قبله فكذلك يستحلف في الصحيح. وعن أبي يوسف يرد بلا يمين البائع، ولو ادعى انقطاعه في مدة قصيرة لا تسمع دعواه، وفي المدة المديدة تسمع، وأقلها ثلاثة أشهر عند أبي يوسف وأربعة أشهر وعشر عند محمد، وسنتان عند أبي حنيفة وزفر، ولا تسمع دعوى انقطاعه إلا بذكر سببه من داء أو حبل؛ لأنه إذا كان السبب غير الداء والحبل لا يكون عيبا بأن كانت ممتدة الطهر، والمرجع في الحبل قول النساء وفي الداء قول الأطباء أي عدلين منهم، وقال الشيخ أبو المعين النسفي في "شرح الجامع الكبير": وإن كان العيب خفياً لا يطلع عليه إلا الأطباء، يثبت بقول عدل منهم، وكذلك إذا =
And lack of menstruation: because continuous cessation of blood in an adult woman is a sign of disease. The considered period for its cessation is the ultimate end of puberty, which is seventeen years according to Abu Hanifa, and fifteen according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The gist is that lack of menstruation is not a defect when it is impossible due to her being young or menopausal, if [the buyer] was aware of her days. But if he bought her on the understanding that she menstruates, and she turns out to be menopausal, he has the right to return. The cessation is known by the statement of the female slave, because no one else knows it. The seller is also made to take an oath if after possession, so she is returned by his refusal (nukul). If before possession, similarly he is made to take an oath in the correct view. From Abu Yusuf: she is returned without the seller's oath. If he claims cessation in a short period, his claim is not heard; in a long period it is heard. The minimum is three months according to Abu Yusuf, four months and ten according to Muhammad, and two years according to Abu Hanifa and Zufar. A claim of cessation is not heard unless he mentions its cause, disease or pregnancy. Because if the cause is other than disease or pregnancy, it is not a defect, as in the case of a woman with a long period of purity. The reference in pregnancy is the word of women; in disease, the word of physicians, i.e. two just ones. And Shaykh Abu al-Maʿīn al-Nasafī said in Sharḥ al-Jāmiʿ al-Kabīr: if the defect is hidden, not known except to physicians, it is established by the statement of one just physician, and similarly if =
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage discusses lack of menstruation as a defect. It explains that the cessation of menstruation in a woman of adult age is a sign of disease, making it a defect. However, it depends on age: if a woman is young (before puberty) or has reached menopause (i.e., reached an age where menstruation normally stops), it is not a defect. The age of menopause is disputed: Abu Hanifa says 17 years, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say 15. If a buyer purchases a female slave assuming she menstruates and she is actually menopausal, he can return her. The proof of cessation is the woman's own statement, and the seller is put on oath. There is a dispute about the minimum period for which cessation is considered permanent: Abu Hanifa and Zufar say 2 years, Muhammad says 4 months 10 days, Abu Yusuf says 3 months. Also, the claim of cessation must be accompanied by a cause (disease or pregnancy) to be considered a defect. The reference for pregnancy is women's statements, and for disease, two just physicians. Finally, quoting al-Nasafī, if the defect is hidden and only known to physicians, the testimony of one just physician is sufficient.

Key Terms
عدم الحيض (adam al-hayd): Lack of menstruation; a defect only if abnormal for age and caused by disease or pregnancy
آيسة (ā'isa): Menopausal woman; one who has passed the age of menstruation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the age after which a woman is considered menopausal and lack of menstruation is not a defect?
A) 15 years
B) 17 years
C) 13 years
D) 20 years

Key Takeaway
Lack of menstruation is a defect only in adult women of menstruating age, and the age of menopause is 17 in Abu Hanifa’s view, 15 in the view of the Sahibayn.

Proof of Cessation: Oaths, Minimum Periods, and Evidence
Procedural rules for cessation claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules on the evidence required to prove cessation of menstruation, including the role of oaths, minimum periods, and testimony of physicians and women.

Core Idea
A claim of cessation requires a minimum duration, the seller's oath, and a pathological cause; proof relies on the slave's word and expert testimony.

When a buyer claims that a purchased female slave does not menstruate, the seller may contest. This lesson explains how cessation is legally established: through the slave's own statement, the seller's oath, and the minimum periods for considering cessation permanent. It also covers the requirement to mention a cause and the evidence of physicians and women.

Guided Path
1
Understand how cessation is known
The cessation is known by the statement of the female slave herself, because she alone knows her menstruation. The seller is also put on oath.
The oath is required after possession; before possession, the seller is still made to take an oath in the correct view.
2
Understand the oath and nukul
If the seller refuses to take the oath (nukul), the slave is returned to the buyer without further proof. This is the standard ruling, though Abu Yusuf has a different view that no oath is needed.
Oath is a key procedural element.
3
Understand the minimum periods dispute
A claim of cessation is only heard if the period is 'long'. The minimum is 3 months (Abu Yusuf), 4 months 10 days (Muhammad), or 2 years (Abu Hanifa & Zufar).
These are intra-Hanafi differences.
4
Understand the requirement of mentioning cause
A claim of cessation is not accepted unless the buyer mentions a cause: disease or pregnancy. If the cause is something else (e.g., naturally long purity), it is not a defect.
Cause must be pathological.
5
Understand evidence from physicians and women
For pregnancy, the word of women is accepted. For disease, the statement of two just physicians is required. Al-Nasafi reports that for hidden defects only known to physicians, one just physician suffices.

Four Defects in Slave-Girls and the Gender Distinction
Lists four defects in female slaves and discusses gender distinction with Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four defects specific to female slaves and explain the Hanafi rationale for distinguishing between male and female slaves, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument on zina as a defect in male slaves.

Core Idea
A defect is defined by the purpose of purchase: for slave-girls, istifrash; for male slaves, service.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq lists four defects that give a buyer the right to return a slave-girl. The Hanafi school ties these defects to the primary purpose of purchasing a female slave—sexual relations and offspring—and explains why the same defects are not automatically defects in a male slave. The text also addresses a Shafi'i disagreement about zina in male slaves. By understanding these distinctions, you will see how the madhhab builds rulings on the objective of the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four defects listed for the slave-girl
Locate the four defects: foul smell (dafar), adultery (zina), being a child of adultery, and disbelief (kufr). Understand that these are mentioned specifically for the female slave (al-ama).
The text states these are defects in the slave-girl, not the male slave, due to the intended purpose.
2
Understand the 'illa (reason) why these are defects
The commentator explains that these defects impede the primary purpose of purchasing a slave-girl, which is istifrash (bedding) and seeking offspring. This talil is the core of the ruling.
Dafar and kufr may repel the master; zina and being a child of zina bring disgrace on offspring.
3
Compare with the male slave
Notice that the text says these defects are not generally defects in a male slave because the purpose of a male slave is service (istikhdam), which is not necessarily hindered by these factors.
There is an exception: if dafar is excessive or zina becomes habitual (more than twice), then it becomes a defect even in a male slave.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i dispute on zina in male slaves
Imam al-Shafi'i considers zina a defect in a male slave, analogizing it to theft. The Hanafi response is that religious matters are not considered in transactions; the contract is sound.
The thamarah al-khilaf: a Hanafi buyer cannot return a male slave for past zina unless habitual; a Shafi'i can.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدفر والزنا وولده في الأمة، والكفر فيهما وعدم الحيض، والدفر: بفتح الدال المهملة وفتح الفاء وسكونها نتن الإبط. (عيني)] قال في "الجمهرة": الدفر هو النتن مطلقا، يقال رجل دافر، وامرأة دافرة، وأما الذفر بالذال المعجمة، فهو حدة الرائحة من طيب أو نتن، وربما خص به الطيب، فقيل: مسك أذفر، ويقال في نتن ريح الأنف أيضاً وصرح في البزازية" بأن نتن ريح الأنف عيب. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٢/٢] (فتح) وولده: [ أي ولد الزنا، وهذه الأربعة تكون عيبا في الأمة دون الغلام؛ لأنه يخل بالمقصود منها، وهو الاستفراش وطلب الولد. (عيني)] أي وكون المبيع ولد الزنا، فحذف المضاف والمضاف إليه. في الأمة: متعلق بالأربعة المذكورة؛ لأنه قد يراد منها الاستفراش. وهذه المعاني تمنع منه، بخلاف الغلام؛ لأنه للاستخدام، وهذه الأشياء لا تخل به، إلا أن يكون البخر والدفر فاحشا بحيث يمنع القرب من المولى، أو يكون الزنا عادة له بأن يتكرر منه أكثر من مرتين؛ لأن اتباع النساء مخل بالخدمة، واليسر عيب وهو الذي يعمل بشماله ولا يعمل بيمينه. والعيوب كلها لا بد لها من المعاودة عند المشتري حتى يرد بها إلا الزنا في الجارية، فإنه روي عن محمد في الأمالي : لو اشترى جارية بالغة، وقد كانت زنت عند البائع، فللمشتري أن يردها وإن لم يزن عنده؛ للحوق العار بالأولاد، وعند الشافعي الزنا في الغلام عيب كالسرقة رمز الحقائق: ٢٢/٢] (فتح) والكفر فيهما: [ أي الكفر عيب في الأمة والغلام (عيني)] أي الكفر بأقسامه، وكذا الرفض والاعتزال عيب في الغلام والجارية؛ لأن طبع المسلم يتنفر عن صحبة الكافر، فلو اشترى عبداً على أنه كافر، فوجده مسلماً لا لأنه زوال للعيب، وقال الشافعي يرده لفوات الوصف المرغوب فيه؛ لأن استعباد الكافر وإذلاله مطلوب للمسلم. رمز الحقائق: ۲۳/۲] ولنا أن هذا الأمر راجع إلى الديانة، ولا عبرة به في المعاملات. (فتح، عناية) وعدم الحيض: لأن ارتفاع الدم مستمرا في البالغة علامة الداء والمعتبر في انقطاعه أقصى غاية البلوغ، وهو سبع عشرة سنة عند أبي حنيفة، وخمس عشرة عندهما والحاصل أن عدم الحيض لا يكون عيباً عند عدم الإمكان؛ لكونها صغيرة أو آئسة إذا كان عالماً بأيامها، أما إذا اشتراها على أنها تحيض، فإذا هي آئسة له الرد، ويعرف الانقطاع بقول الأمة؛ لأنه لا يعرفه غيرها، ويستحلف البائع مع ذلك إن كان بعد القبض، فترد بنكوله، وإن كان قبله فكذلك يستحلف في الصحيح. وعن أبي يوسف يرد بلا يمين البائع، ولو ادعى انقطاعه في مدة قصيرة لا تسمع دعواه، وفي المدة المديدة تسمع، وأقلها ثلاثة أشهر عند أبي يوسف وأربعة أشهر وعشر عند محمد، وسنتان عند أبي حنيفة وزفر، ولا تسمع دعوى انقطاعه إلا بذكر سببه من داء أو حبل؛ لأنه إذا كان السبب غير الداء والحبل لا يكون عيبا بأن كانت ممتدة الطهر، والمرجع في الحبل قول النساء وفي الداء قول الأطباء أي عدلين منهم، وقال الشيخ أبو المعين النسفي في "شرح الجامع الكبير": وإن كان العيب خفياً لا يطلع عليه إلا الأطباء، يثبت بقول عدل منهم، وكذلك إذا كان لا يطلع عليه إلا النساء، يثبت بقول واحدة عدلة [ رمز الحقائق: ٢٣/٢] (فتح)
And the foul smell, adultery, and her child in the slave-girl, and disbelief in both, and absence of menstruation. Al-Dafar: with fatha on the daal and fatha on the fa and its sukun means stench of the armpit. (Ayni) He said in al-Jamhara: al-dafar is absolute stench... (full translation ommitted for brevity)
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses defects (uyub) in slaves that give the buyer the right of return. The author first lists four defects specific to female slaves: foul smell (dafar), adultery (zina), being a child of adultery, and disbelief (kufr). He explains that these are defects in a slave-girl because the primary purpose of purchasing her is istifrash (bedding) and seeking offspring, which these defects impede. In a male slave, these are generally not defects unless the foul smell is excessive or adultery becomes habitual (more than twice), as it interferes with service. The exception for zina: if a slave-girl committed zina before purchase, the buyer can return her even if it did not recur, due to the disgrace on children. Imam al-Shafi'i considers zina a defect in a male slave as well, but the Hanafi response is that such matters pertain to religion and are not considered in transactions. Regarding absence of menstruation, the author details the intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa considers the maximum age for possible menstruation as 17 years, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say 15. If a woman is past that age or too young, lack of menses is not a defect. The buyer can return if he bought her expecting menstruation but she turns out post-menopausal. The seller is required to take an oath if the claim is after delivery. The minimal period for claiming cessation is disputed: 3 months (Abu Yusuf), 4 months 10 days (Muhammad), 2 years (Abu Hanifa and Zufar). The claim must mention a cause like disease or pregnancy; otherwise it is not a defect. Evidence: women's statements for pregnancy, two just physicians for disease, or one woman if only women can know.

Key Terms
الدفر (al-dafar): Foul smell, especially from the armpit; considered a defect if it repels the master.
الاستفراش (al-istifrash): Using a female slave for sexual relations, the primary purpose of purchase.
الغلام (al-ghulam): Male slave; his primary purpose is service (istikhdam).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is a male slave considered defective if he has committed zina (adultery) once before purchase?
A) Yes, because zina is a moral defect.
B) No, unless it becomes habitual (more than twice).
C) Yes, if the buyer intended to use him for service requiring trust.
D) No, because zina is not considered a defect in any case.

Key Takeaway
Defects in sales are defined by the purpose of the contract; for female slaves, istifrash is central; for male slaves, service, and religious matters are not transactional defects.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Amenorrhea as a Defect: Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on Age and Duration
Discusses amenorrhea as a defect with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on age, duration, and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which lack of menstruation is a defect in a slave-girl, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreements on the age limit and minimal period for claiming cessation.

Core Idea
Lack of menses is a defect only if the slave-girl is of menstruating age and not post-menopausal.

This section of the passage focuses on amenorrhea as a defect in a female slave. The Hanafi school engages in a detailed intra-madhhab dispute about the age at which a woman is considered post-menopausal, the minimum period after which a claim of cessation can be made, and the procedure for proof. Understanding these nuances shows how the madhhab handles evidentiary and interpretive differences within its own ranks.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for amenorrhea to be a defect
The text states that lack of menses is only a defect if it occurs in a woman who is of menstruating age (baligha). If she is a minor or post-menopausal (a'isa), it is not a defect.
The buyer must have been unaware of her status; if he knew she was a'isa, he cannot claim defect.
2
Determine the ages: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa holds that the maximum age for a woman to possibly menstruate is 17 years. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) say it is 15 years. A woman older than this is considered a'isa.
This disagreement affects whether a 16-year-old without menses is considered defective. Under Abu Hanifa, she could still possibly menstruate; under the others, she is a'isa and not defective.
3
Understand the procedure for claiming return
The buyer claims that the slave-girl does not menstruate. The seller must take an oath if the claim is after delivery. Abu Yusuf says before delivery no oath is needed; the relied-upon view (sahih) is that oath is required even before delivery.
If the seller refuses to swear, the buyer can return her (nukul).
4
Analyze the required duration for a valid claim
If the buyer claims the cessation of menses occurred in a short period, it is not accepted. The minimal periods for acceptance are disputed: Abu Hanifa and Zufar: 2 years; Abu Yusuf: 3 months; Muhammad: 4 months 10 days.
Additionally, the claim must mention a cause (disease or pregnancy). If the cause is not disease or pregnancy, it is not a defect (e.g., naturally long periods without menses).
5
Note the evidence and testimony rules
Evidence for pregnancy is the word of women; evidence for disease is the testimony of two just physicians. According to Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, for hidden defects that only physicians can detect, one just physician suffices; similarly, for matters only women know, one just woman suffices.
This is a relaxation of the normal two-witness rule for cases where only one gender has access to information.

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses defects (uyub) in slaves that give the buyer the right of return. The author first lists four defects specific to female slaves: foul smell (dafar), adultery (zina), being a child of adultery, and disbelief (kufr). He explains that these are defects in a slave-girl because the primary purpose of purchasing her is istifrash (bedding) and seeking offspring, which these defects impede. In a male slave, these are generally not defects unless the foul smell is excessive or adultery becomes habitual (more than twice), as it interferes with service. The exception for zina: if a slave-girl committed zina before purchase, the buyer can return her even if it did not recur, due to the disgrace on children. Imam al-Shafi'i considers zina a defect in a male slave as well, but the Hanafi response is that such matters pertain to religion and are not considered in transactions. Regarding absence of menstruation, the author details the intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa considers the maximum age for possible menstruation as 17 years, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say 15. If a woman is past that age or too young, lack of menses is not a defect. The buyer can return if he bought her expecting menstruation but she turns out post-menopausal. The seller is required to take an oath if the claim is after delivery. The minimal period for claiming cessation is disputed: 3 months (Abu Yusuf), 4 months 10 days (Muhammad), 2 years (Abu Hanifa and Zufar). The claim must mention a cause like disease or pregnancy; otherwise it is not a defect. Evidence: women's statements for pregnancy, two just physicians for disease, or one woman if only women can know.

Key Terms
آئسة (a'isa): Post-menopausal woman; one who has passed the age of menstruation (usually 55 years, but here defined by the age limits given by the Imams).
الطهر (al-tuhr): Period of purity between menses; a woman with long tuhr may have naturally long intervals.
عدل (adl): Just, upright; a witness whose testimony is accepted. One just physician or woman suffices for hidden defects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimum duration of amenorrhea that can be claimed as a defect according to Abu Hanifa?
A) 3 months
B) 4 months 10 days
C) 2 years
D) 1 year

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school provides specific age limits and duration requirements for amenorrhea to be a defect, with valid internal disagreements, reflecting the school's method of deriving rulings from empirical observation and tradition.

Defects in Slaves and the Rule on New Defect
Lists additional defects in slaves and explains the legal effect when a new defect occurs after purchase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify defects in slaves according to the Hanafi school and state the buyer's remedies when a new defect arises after purchase.

Core Idea
A pre-existing defect gives the buyer a right to return or claim reduction; a new defect shifts the remedy to compensation.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq continues the enumeration of defects in slaves, including istihadah, old cough, and debt. It also introduces a crucial rule: when a new defect occurs after the buyer takes possession, the buyer cannot return the item but may claim a reduction in value. Understanding this principle is vital for commercial transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify each listed defect and the rationale
Read through the defects (istihadah, old cough, debt, etc.) and note the reasoning that makes each a defect (e.g., istihadah is continuous blood indicating disease).
Pay attention to the distinction between defects affecting appearance vs. value.
2
Distinguish between immediate and deferred debt
Note that only debt immediately payable (e.g., maintenance debt) and not covered by the price is a defect; deferred debts (e.g., from unauthorized transactions) are not.
This is a specific Hanafi detail.
3
Understand the rule on new defect
Learn that if a new defect occurs after purchase, the buyer cannot return the slave but may claim the reduction in value (naqsan). Return is only possible if the seller agrees.
Reduction is calculated as the difference between market value with and without the defect at time of discovery.
4
Apply the reduction calculation method
The reduction is appraised by experts: the slave's value with the defect and without it, at the time of purchase (or discovery). The difference is the amount the buyer can claim.
This is implied in the text; later sources detail the method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(البحر الرائق، جوهري) والدين: أي ماليته تكون مشغولة به وأراد به ما يطلب في الحال، وفي بعض النسخ مكان الدين "الدبر" بالباء والراء بمعنى جراحة الظهر أي الدين الذي له مطالب في الحال أي ما قبل العتق كدين النفقة إذا تزوج بإذن السيد، ولا يباع مرة بعد أخرى إلا في دين النفقة فقط؛ لأن مالية العبد تكون مشغولة بالدين وتقدم الغرماء على المولى، وهذا تعليل يقتضي تقييد الدين بما إذا كان الثمن لا يفي به، فإن كان لم يكن عيباً حينئذ لا دين مؤجل إلى ما بعد العتق كدين لزمه بالمبايعة بغير إذن فإنه ليس بعيب. (فتح عيني) والشعر والماء إلخ وفي "التحفة : العيوب على نوعين: أحدهما: ما يوجب فوات جزء من المبيع وتغييره من حيث الظاهر دون الباطن، أما الأول فكثير نحو العمى، والعور، والصمم، والشلل، والزمانة، والإصبع الناقصة، والسن السوداء والسن الساقطة، والظفر الأسود، والخرس، والبكم، والقروح، والشجاج، وأثر الجراحة، والأمراض التي في سائر البدن كالحميات وأما الثاني فما يوجب النقصان من حيث المعنى دون الصورة كالسعال القديم وارتفاع الحيض في زمان طويل، أدناه شهران فصاعداً في الجواري. ومنها صهوبة الشعر أي الحمرة إذا فحشت بحيث تضرب إلى البياض، والشمط أي اختلاط البياض بالسواد في الشعر، والشيب في العبيد والجواري، والحبل في الجارية عيب لا في البهائم والنكاح في الجارية والغلام عيب، وانتفاخ الأنثيين والإصبعة الزائدة والعنة والخصي عيب، وعدم الختان في العبد البالغ إن كان مولداً عيب، وإن كان مجلوباً دار الحرب رب فليس بعيب كما إذا كان صغيراً، وحذف الحروف في المصحف كله أو في بعضه عيب؛ لأن في ذلك كله ينقص الثمن عند التجار. (عيني، مسكين) في العين قيد فيهما؛ لأنهما يضعفان البصر ويورثان العمى. (ط) عيني) عند المشتري: بعد اطلاعه على العيب الذي كان عند البائع (عيني) رجع بنقصانه : لأنه تعذر الردّ بسبب العيب الحادث. (عيني) أو رده إلخ: أي لو حدث عيب عند المشتري واطلع على عيب كان عند البائع، رجع بنقصانه؛ لأنه استحق تسليمه سليماً عن العيب أو رد المبيع فيما يملك البائع إسقاط حقه وتملكه، فإذا رضي البائع يخير المشتري بين الرد والإمساك من غير رجوع بنقصان، وإنما يرجع بالنقصان حيث لم يرض البائع بالرد. وطريق معرفة النقصان =

Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage continues the enumeration of defects in slaves. Istihadah (chronic non-menstrual bleeding) is a defect in an adult woman as it indicates disease. Old cough (al-su'al al-qadim) is a defect only if caused by disease, not a habitual cough or cold. Debt (al-dayn) is a defect if it is immediately payable and the price does not cover it; deferred debts after emancipation (like debts from unauthorized transactions) are not defects. The text then lists many other defects from al-Tuhfah: blindness, one-eyedness, deafness, paralysis, chronic illness, missing finger, black tooth, missing tooth, black nail, muteness, dumbness, ulcers, wounds, scars, fevers, etc. For slave-girls: long absence of menses (minimum two months), reddish hair (if excessive), white and black mixed hair (shamat), gray hair, pregnancy (in a slave-girl but not in animals), marriage (in slave-girl or boy), swollen testicles, extra finger, impotence, castration, lack of circumcision in a slave born in dar al-Islam (but not in those brought from dar al-harb), and omission of letters in a Quran copy (since it reduces value). The passage ends with the rule when a new defect occurs after purchase: the buyer may claim the reduction in value (naqsan) because return is impossible due to the new defect; if the seller agrees, the buyer can return the item, but if the seller does not consent, the buyer may keep it and claim the difference in price. The method of calculating the reduction is not fully given here, but it involves appraising the item with and without the defect.

Key Terms
الاستحاضة (al-istihadah): Chronic non-menstrual bleeding from the uterus, considered a defect in an adult woman as it indicates disease.
السعال القديم (al-su'al al-qadim): Old cough, i.e., a chronic cough resulting from disease, not a temporary cough from cold.
النقصان (al-naqsan): The reduction in value due to the defect; the buyer claims this when return is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer discovers a pre-existing defect in a purchased slave after a new defect has occurred, what are his options according to the Hanafi school?
A) He can only return the slave.
B) He can only claim a reduction in price.
C) He can claim a reduction in price, or return the slave if the seller agrees.
D) He cannot do anything because the new defect invalidates his right.

Key Takeaway
A pre-existing defect in a slave gives the buyer the right to claim compensation or return with consent; a new defect after purchase prevents return but not compensation.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Types of Defects in Sales (Khiyar al-Ayb)
Listing and classification of defects in sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the two categories of defects in Hanafi law and identify at least five specific defects from each category.

Core Idea
A defect is anything that reduces the item's market value among merchants, whether physical or meaning-based.

The Hanafi school provides a comprehensive list of defects that give the buyer the right to return or claim compensation. This lesson introduces the types of defects and their classification as outlined in the classical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two major categories
The passage from al-Tuhfah divides defects into two categories: those that cause loss of a part or change in physical form (ẓāhir), and those that cause deficiency in meaning (maʿnā) without changing the appearance.
2
List examples of physical form defects
Examples include blindness, one-eyedness, deafness, paralysis, missing finger, black tooth, missing tooth, black nail, muteness, speechlessness, ulcers, head wounds, scar of wound, and fevers.
These are defects that are visible or affect the body's structure.
3
List examples of meaning-based defects
Examples include chronic cough, cessation of menses (istihāḍah), redness of hair (ṣuhūbah), mixed gray hair (shamaṭ), pregnancy in a slave girl, marriage of a slave, swelling of testicles, extra finger, impotence, castration, lack of circumcision (if born in Islam), and missing letters in a Quran copy.
These defects affect the value without altering the outward form.
4
Understand the key criterion
The defining characteristic of a defect is that it reduces the item's price among merchants. Even intangible conditions like debt or marital status are included if they diminish the item's worth.
