Chapter of option of defect. Book of sales. Istihadah (chronic menstruation), chronic cough, debt, abscess (or back wound), hair, and water in the eye. Then if another defect occurs with the buyer, he resorts to (claiming) its deficiency, or returns (the item) with the seller's consent. (Ayni) Istihadah: continuous blood, it is a sign of illness in a mature woman. (Ayni, Ma'din) Chronic cough: with damm on sin, meaning istifa (chronicity?) because its continuity indicates illness. (Ayni) The apparent meaning that it be old means it is from illness, so the consideration is that it be from illness, not oldness; and it excludes ordinary cough and cold, for these are not defects. (Bahr al-Ra'iq, Jawhari) Debt: because his financial liability is occupied with it, and what is meant is what is demanded immediately; in some copies instead of "debt" is "al-dabar" with ba and ra meaning a wound on the back, i.e., the debt that has a demand immediately, i.e., before emancipation, like the debt of maintenance if he marries with the master's permission. It is not sold time after time except only in the debt of maintenance; because the slave's financial value is occupied with the debt and the creditors take precedence over the master. This reasoning requires restricting debt to when the price does not cover it; if it does, it is not a defect at that time. Deferred debt until after emancipation, like a debt incurred by transaction without permission, is not a defect. (Fath Ayni) And hair and water etc. And in al-Tuhfah: Defects are of two types: one: what necessitates loss of a part of the sold item and changes it in appearance but not inner. As for the first, many like blindness, one-eyedness, deafness, paralysis, chronic illness, missing finger, black tooth, missing tooth, black nail, muteness, speechlessness, ulcers, head wounds, scar of wound, and diseases in the rest of the body like fevers. As for the second, what necessitates deficiency in meaning without form, like chronic cough and cessation of menses for a long time, the minimum being two months or more in slave girls. Among them: reddishness of hair i.e. redness if excessive to the point of whiteness, mixed gray i.e. mixing of white with black in hair, grayness in male and female slaves, pregnancy in a slave girl is a defect but not in animals, marriage in a slave girl and slave boy is a defect, swelling of testicles, extra finger, impotence, castration is a defect, lack of circumcision in an adult male slave if born in Islam is a defect, but if brought from dar al-harb it is not a defect as if he were young, and missing letters in the entire Quran or part of it is a defect; because all this decreases the price among merchants. (Ayni, Miskin) In the eye, it is restricted to both because they weaken sight and cause blindness. (T, Ayni) With the buyer: after his awareness of the defect that was with the seller. (Ayni) He resorts to its deficiency because return is prevented due to the newly occurring defect. (Ayni) Or his return etc.: i.e. if a defect occurs with the buyer and he becomes aware of a defect that was with the seller, he resorts to its deficiency because he was entitled to receive it sound. As for returning the sold item, the seller has the right to forfeit his right and take possession. So if the seller consents, the buyer chooses between return and retention without resorting to deficiency. And he only resorts to deficiency if the seller does not consent to return. The method of knowing the deficiency is by valuing it with this defect, then valuing it sound; if the difference between the two values is known, he resorts to his share of the price, so if the deficiency is one-tenth of the value, he resorts to one-tenth of the price; if one-third, to one-third. Malik and Ahmad in one narration said: the buyer may return it and return with it the deficiency of the new defect that occurred with the buyer, because the justification for return exists, which is awareness of the defect. Our proof: the right of return was established for the buyer to remove harm from himself in a way that does not harm the seller. If he returns it after it became defective with him, the seller is harmed because it left his ownership sound and he is not obligated, and the buyer's harm can be removed by resorting to his share of the price. (Ayni, Fath, Zayla'i) His seller because he consented to bear the harm, and without his consent he does not return. (Ayni) He resorts to the defect: i.e. the buyer resorts to the deficiency of the defect, contrary to if he bought a camel and slaughtered it and found its intestines spoiled, where he does not resort to deficiency according to Abu Hanifa, because slaughter is destruction of value as if he bought a slave and killed him. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 24/2) (Fath) He has that: i.e. the seller may accept because the refusal is for his right and he has consented to forfeit his right. (Ayni) He resorts to it, contrary to... he does not resort at all, whether he was aware of the defect at the time of sale or not, and this is the apparent narration. And from them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): that if he sewed it then sold it, the recourse to deficiency is not invalidated because he did not become a preventer of return by sale since return was prevented before by sewing without knowledge of the defect, and his selling it after prevention of return has no effect. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 25/2) (Miskin) Something on the seller because he became a preventer of the sold item by sale since return is not prevented by cutting with the seller's consent, so he was a thwart of return. (Ayni) So if he cut it: i.e. the buyer cut it and did not sew it, or dyed it: with any dye that increases the value of the cloth. (T, Ayni) Or mixed: i.e. mixed the sawiq that he bought with ghee. (Ayni, T) on a defect i.e. in the cloth or sawiq, that was with the seller in these things. (T, Ayni) He resorts to its deficiency: i.e. the buyer resorts to the seller for deficiency of the defect because return is prevented due to the increase. (Ayni) As if he sold it etc.: i.e. if the buyer sold the sewn cloth or dyed cloth or mixed sawiq, he resorts to deficiency because return is prevented due to the increase; similarly if he sold it after seeing the defect, he resorts to deficiency. And know that increase is of two types: attached and detached. Attached is of two types: arising from the original like fatness and beauty, and it does not prevent return because the increase is a pure follow due to generation; and not arising like dye, sewing, and mixing, and these prevent return by defect unanimously. Detached is of two types: arising like child and fruit, and it prevents return; and not arising like earnings, and it does not prevent return by defect, and the difference is that earnings are not part of the sold item at all because...
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses the chapter on the option of defect (khiyar al-ayb) in sales. It begins by listing several conditions that constitute defects: istihadah (chronic menstruation), chronic cough, debt (that is immediately due), an abscess or wound on the back, hair, and water in the eye. It explains each in detail, noting that a chronic cough is only a defect if it stems from illness, not from a temporary cold. Debt is a defect if the slave's value is tied up and the sale price does not cover it; deferred debts or those incurred without the master's permission are not defects. The passage then quotes al-Tuhfah, which categorizes defects into two types: those that cause loss of a part or change in appearance (like blindness, missing finger, etc.) and those that cause deficiency in meaning without altering appearance (like chronic cough, long cessation of menses, redness of hair, grayness, pregnancy in a slave girl, etc.). Missing letters in a Quran copy is a defect because it reduces value.

The main ruling discussed is: if a defect that existed with the seller is discovered after another defect has occurred with the buyer, the buyer cannot return the item (due to the new defect) but can claim compensation (the difference in value) from the seller. However, if the seller consents to accept the return despite the new defect, it is allowed. The method of compensation is to compare the value of the item with the old defect and its value without it, then the buyer recovers that proportion of the price. This is based on the principle that the buyer's right to return sound goods is established, but if a new defect makes return harmful to the seller, the buyer's harm is alleviated by compensation. The passage includes a disagreement with Malik and Ahmad, who allow the buyer to return the item with compensation for the new defect. The Hanafi response is that the buyer's right to return is to remove harm without causing harm to the seller; since the seller would be harmed by receiving a defective item, the buyer can only claim compensation. The text also gives examples: if the buyer cuts a cloth and finds a defect, he can claim compensation; if he sews, dyes, or mixes it, the same applies. If he kills a camel or a slave, no compensation is due because that destroys the value entirely. The passage concludes with a classification of increases: attached increases (like fatness) do not prevent return, but attached increases like dyeing do; detached increases like earnings do not prevent return, but children and fruit do.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-ayb): The right of the buyer to return a defective item or claim compensation when a hidden defect is discovered after sale.
عيب (ayb): A defect that reduces the value of the sold item in the estimation of merchants; includes both physical and meaning-based deficiencies.
نقصان (nuqsan): The decrease in value caused by the defect; compensation is proportional to the difference in price.
الاستحاضة (istihāḍah): Chronic menstruation; continuous bleeding that is a sign of illness in a mature woman, considered a defect in a slave girl.
السعال القديم (al-suʿāl al-qadīm): Chronic cough that stems from an illness, considered a meaning-based defect as it indicates an underlying disease.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT considered a defect in Hanafi sales law?
A) A slave girl who has chronic menstruation (istihadah)
B) A slave who is uncircumcised but was born in Islam
C) A slave who has a temporary cold with cough
D) A slave who has a missing tooth

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of defect is broad, encompassing any attribute that lowers market value among merchants, including intangible ones.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Ruling When a New Defect Arises After Purchase
Ruling on khiyar al-ayb when new defect prevents return.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-ayb when a new defect occurs and compare it with the Maliki/Hanbali position.

Core Idea
The buyer cannot return a defective item if a new defect occurred, but can claim compensation proportionately.

A common scenario: you buy an item, later discover an old defect, but you have already caused a new defect. Can you still return it? The Hanafi school says no — but you can recover the loss in value from the seller.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
The case is: a buyer purchases an item, and after taking possession, a new defect occurs (e.g., cutting a cloth). Later, the buyer discovers an old defect that existed with the seller at the time of sale.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The buyer may claim the deficiency (nuqsan) in price proportional to the old defect's reduction in value. The buyer may also return the item if the seller consents to accept the return despite the new defect.
3
Analyze the proof (dalil aqli)
The buyer's right to return is established to remove harm from himself without harming the seller. If the buyer returns the item after it has become defective with him, the seller would be harmed because the item left his ownership sound. The buyer's harm can be removed by monetary compensation.
This is a rational argument based on the principle of avoiding harm (la darar).
4
Contrast with opposing view
Malik and Ahmad (in one narration) hold that the buyer may return the item and also compensate the seller for the new defect. Their justification is that the reason for return (discovery of the old defect) still exists.
The practical difference: Hanafi keeps the item and gets a price reduction; Maliki/Hanbali returns the item and pays for the new defect.
5
Note the role of seller's consent
If the seller agrees to take back the item despite the new defect, the buyer has the choice to return or retain. This is because the seller has the right to forfeit his own protection from harm.

Source Text

باب خيار العيب كتاب البيوع والاستحاضة والسعال القديم والدين والدبر والشعر والماء في العين، فلو حدث عيب آخر عند المشتري رجع بنقصانه، أو ردّه برضاء البائع (عيني) والاستحاضة: وهو استمرار الدم وهو علامة الداء في البالغة (عيني (معدن والسعال القديم: [بضم السين السرفة؛ لأن دوامه يدل على الداء (عيني)] والظاهر أن المراد من كونه قديما ما كان عن داء، فالمنظور إليه كونه عن داء لا القدم، واحترز به عن السعال المعتاد والزكام فإنه ليس بعيب. (البحر الرائق، جوهري) والدين: الأن ماليته تكون مشغولة به وأراد به ما يطلب في الحال، وفي بعض النسخ مكان الدين "الدبر" بالباء والراء بمعنى جراحة الظهر أي الدين الذي له مطالب في الحال أي ما قبل العتق كدين النفقة إذا تزوج بإذن السيد، ولا يباع مرة بعد أخرى إلا في دين النفقة فقط؛ لأن مالية العبد تكون مشغولة بالدين وتقدم الغرماء على المولى، وهذا تعليل يقتضي تقييد الدين بما إذا كان الثمن لا يفي به، فإن كان لم يكن عيباً حينئذ لا دين مؤجل إلى ما بعد العتق كدين لزمه بالمبايعة بغير إذن فإنه ليس بعيب. (فتح عيني) والشعر والماء إلخ وفي "التحفة : العيوب على نوعين: أحدهما: ما يوجب فوات جزء من المبيع وتغييره من حيث الظاهر دون الباطن، أما الأول فكثير نحو العمى، والعور، والصمم، والشلل، والزمانة، والإصبع الناقصة، والسن السوداء والسن الساقطة، والظفر الأسود، والخرس، والبكم، والقروح، والشجاج، وأثر الجراحة، والأمراض التي في سائر البدن كالحميات وأما الثاني فما يوجب النقصان من حيث المعنى دون الصورة كالسعال القديم وارتفاع الحيض في زمان طويل، أدناه شهران فصاعداً في الجواري. ومنها صهوبة الشعر أي الحمرة إذا فحشت بحيث تضرب إلى البياض، والشمط أي اختلاط البياض بالسواد في الشعر، والشيب في العبيد والجواري، والحبل في الجارية عيب لا في البهائم والنكاح في الجارية والغلام عيب، وانتفاخ الأنثيين والإصبعة الزائدة والعنة والخصي عيب، وعدم الختان في العبد البالغ إن كان مولداً عيب، وإن كان مجلوباً دار الحرب رب فليس بعيب كما إذا كان صغيراً، وحذف الحروف في المصحف كله أو في بعضه عيب؛ لأن في ذلك كله ينقص الثمن عند التجار. (عيني، مسكين) في العين قيد فيهما؛ لأنهما يضعفان البصر ويورثان العمى. (ط) عيني) عند المشتري: بعد اطلاعه على العيب من الذي كان عند البائع (عيني) رجع بنقصانه : لأنه تعذر الردّ بسبب العيب الحادث. (عيني) أو رده إلخ: أي لو حدث عيب عند المشتري واطلع على عيب كان عند البائع، رجع بنقصانه؛ لأنه استحق تسليمه سليماً . عن العيب أو رد المبيع فيما يملك البائع إسقاط حقه وتملكه، فإذا رضي البائع يخير المشتري بين الرد والإمساك من غير رجوع بنقصان، وإنما يرجع بالنقصان حيث لم يرض البائع بالرد. وطريق معرفة النقصان بأن يقوم بهذا العيب، ثم يقوّم وهو سالم، فإذا عرف التفاوت الذي بين القيمتين يرجع عليه بحصته من الثمن حتى إذا كان النقصان عشر القيمة، يرجع عليه بعشر الثمن، وإن كان ثلثاً فبثلثه، وقال مالك وأحمد في رواية: للمشتري أن يردّه وأن يرد معه نقصان العيب الحادث عند المشتري؛ لقيام المجوز للرد ، وهو الاطلاع على العيب. ولنا أن حق الرد ثبت للمشتري ليدفع به الضرر عن نفسه على وجه لا يتضرر به البائع، فلو رده بعد ما تعيب عنده يتضرر به البائع؛ لأنه خرج عن ملكه سليماً فلا يلزمه، وضرر المشتري أمكن رفعه بالرجوع بحصته من الثمن. (عيني، فتح، زيلعي) بائعه لأنه به، رضي بالتزام الضرر وبدون رضائه لا يرد (عيني) رجع بالعيب: أي رجع المشتري بنقصان العيب، بخلاف ما إذا اشترى بعيراً، فنحره، فوجد أمعاءه فاسدة، حيث لا يرجع بالنقصان عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن النحر إفساد للمالية كما إذا كان عبداً فقتله المشتري. (رمز الحقائق: ٢٤/٢) (فتح) له ذلك: أي للبائع يجوز القبول؛ لأن الامتناع لحقه وقد رضي بإسقاط حقه. (عيني) يرجع به، بخلاف لم يرجع إلخ مطلقا سواء كان عالما بالعيب وقت البيع أو لا، وهو ظاهر الرواية، وعنهما: أنه ما إذا خاطه ثم باعه حيث لا يبطل الرجوع بالنقصان؛ لأنه لم يصر حابساً له بالبيع لامتناع الرد قبله بالخياطة من غير علم بالعيب وبيعه بعد امتناع الرد لا تأثير له (رمز الحقائق: ٢٥/٢) (مسكين) بشيء على البائع؛ لأنه صار حابسا للمبيع بالبيع إذ الرد غير ممتنع بالقطع برضاء البائع، فكان مفوتا للرد. (عيني) فلو قطعه: أي قطع المشتري و لم يخطه أو صبغه : بأي صبغ كان مما يزيد قيمة الثوب. (ط، عيني) أو لت: أي خلط السويق الذي اشتراه عيني، ط على عيب أي في الثوب أو السويق، كان عند البائع في هذه الأشياء. (ط، عيني) رجع بنقصانه: أي رجع المشتري على البائع بنقصان العيب؛ لتعذر الرد بسبب الزيادة. (عيني) كما لو باعه إلخ: أي لو باع المشتري الثوب المخيط أو الثوب المصبوغ أو السويق الملتوت رجع بنقصانه لتعذر الرد بسبب الزيادة، فكذا لو باعه بعد رؤية العيب يرجع بالنقصان واعلم أن الزيادة نوعان: متصلة، ومنفصلة، والمتصلة نوعان: متولدة من الأصل كالسمن والجمال، وهي لا تمنع الرد؛ لأن الزيادة تبع محض باعتبار التولد، وغير متولدة كالصبغ والخياطة واللت وهي تمنع الرد بالعيب ،اتفاقا والمنفصلة نوعان متولدة كالولد والثمر، وهي تمنع الرد، وغير متولدة كالكسب وهي لا تمنع الرد بالعيب، والفرق أن الكسب ليس بمبيع بحال ما؛ لأنه .
Chapter of option of defect. Book of sales. Istihadah (chronic menstruation), chronic cough, debt, abscess (or back wound), hair, and water in the eye. Then if another defect occurs with the buyer, he resorts to (claiming) its deficiency, or returns (the item) with the seller's consent. (Ayni) Istihadah: continuous blood, it is a sign of illness in a mature woman. (Ayni, Ma'din) Chronic cough: with damm on sin, meaning istifa (chronicity?) because its continuity indicates illness. (Ayni) The apparent meaning that it be old means it is from illness, so the consideration is that it be from illness, not oldness; and it excludes ordinary cough and cold, for these are not defects. (Bahr al-Ra'iq, Jawhari) Debt: because his financial liability is occupied with it, and what is meant is what is demanded immediately; in some copies instead of "debt" is "al-dabar" with ba and ra meaning a wound on the back, i.e., the debt that has a demand immediately, i.e., before emancipation, like the debt of maintenance if he marries with the master's permission. It is not sold time after time except only in the debt of maintenance; because the slave's financial value is occupied with the debt and the creditors take precedence over the master. This reasoning requires restricting debt to when the price does not cover it; if it does, it is not a defect at that time. Deferred debt until after emancipation, like a debt incurred by transaction without permission, is not a defect. (Fath Ayni) And hair and water etc. And in al-Tuhfah: Defects are of two types: one: what necessitates loss of a part of the sold item and changes it in appearance but not inner. As for the first, many like blindness, one-eyedness, deafness, paralysis, chronic illness, missing finger, black tooth, missing tooth, black nail, muteness, speechlessness, ulcers, head wounds, scar of wound, and diseases in the rest of the body like fevers. As for the second, what necessitates deficiency in meaning without form, like chronic cough and cessation of menses for a long time, the minimum being two months or more in slave girls. Among them: reddishness of hair i.e. redness if excessive to the point of whiteness, mixed gray i.e. mixing of white with black in hair, grayness in male and female slaves, pregnancy in a slave girl is a defect but not in animals, marriage in a slave girl and slave boy is a defect, swelling of testicles, extra finger, impotence, castration is a defect, lack of circumcision in an adult male slave if born in Islam is a defect, but if brought from dar al-harb it is not a defect as if he were young, and missing letters in the entire Quran or part of it is a defect; because all this decreases the price among merchants. (Ayni, Miskin) In the eye, it is restricted to both because they weaken sight and cause blindness. (T, Ayni) With the buyer: after his awareness of the defect that was with the seller. (Ayni) He resorts to its deficiency because return is prevented due to the newly occurring defect. (Ayni) Or his return etc.: i.e. if a defect occurs with the buyer and he becomes aware of a defect that was with the seller, he resorts to its deficiency because he was entitled to receive it sound. As for returning the sold item, the seller has the right to forfeit his right and take possession. So if the seller consents, the buyer chooses between return and retention without resorting to deficiency. And he only resorts to deficiency if the seller does not consent to return. The method of knowing the deficiency is by valuing it with this defect, then valuing it sound; if the difference between the two values is known, he resorts to his share of the price, so if the deficiency is one-tenth of the value, he resorts to one-tenth of the price; if one-third, to one-third. Malik and Ahmad in one narration said: the buyer may return it and return with it the deficiency of the new defect that occurred with the buyer, because the justification for return exists, which is awareness of the defect. Our proof: the right of return was established for the buyer to remove harm from himself in a way that does not harm the seller. If he returns it after it became defective with him, the seller is harmed because it left his ownership sound and he is not obligated, and the buyer's harm can be removed by resorting to his share of the price. (Ayni, Fath, Zayla'i) His seller because he consented to bear the harm, and without his consent he does not return. (Ayni) He resorts to the defect: i.e. the buyer resorts to the deficiency of the defect, contrary to if he bought a camel and slaughtered it and found its intestines spoiled, where he does not resort to deficiency according to Abu Hanifa, because slaughter is destruction of value as if he bought a slave and killed him. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 24/2) (Fath) He has that: i.e. the seller may accept because the refusal is for his right and he has consented to forfeit his right. (Ayni) He resorts to it, contrary to... he does not resort at all, whether he was aware of the defect at the time of sale or not, and this is the apparent narration. And from them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): that if he sewed it then sold it, the recourse to deficiency is not invalidated because he did not become a preventer of return by sale since return was prevented before by sewing without knowledge of the defect, and his selling it after prevention of return has no effect. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 25/2) (Miskin) Something on the seller because he became a preventer of the sold item by sale since return is not prevented by cutting with the seller's consent, so he was a thwart of return. (Ayni) So if he cut it: i.e. the buyer cut it and did not sew it, or dyed it: with any dye that increases the value of the cloth. (T, Ayni) Or mixed: i.e. mixed the sawiq that he bought with ghee. (Ayni, T) on a defect i.e. in the cloth or sawiq, that was with the seller in these things. (T, Ayni) He resorts to its deficiency: i.e. the buyer resorts to the seller for deficiency of the defect because return is prevented due to the increase. (Ayni) As if he sold it etc.: i.e. if the buyer sold the sewn cloth or dyed cloth or mixed sawiq, he resorts to deficiency because return is prevented due to the increase; similarly if he sold it after seeing the defect, he resorts to deficiency. And know that increase is of two types: attached and detached. Attached is of two types: arising from the original like fatness and beauty, and it does not prevent return because the increase is a pure follow due to generation; and not arising like dye, sewing, and mixing, and these prevent return by defect unanimously. Detached is of two types: arising like child and fruit, and it prevents return; and not arising like earnings, and it does not prevent return by defect, and the difference is that earnings are not part of the sold item at all because...
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses the chapter on the option of defect (khiyar al-ayb) in sales. It begins by listing several conditions that constitute defects: istihadah (chronic menstruation), chronic cough, debt (that is immediately due), an abscess or wound on the back, hair, and water in the eye. It explains each in detail, noting that a chronic cough is only a defect if it stems from illness, not from a temporary cold. Debt is a defect if the slave's value is tied up and the sale price does not cover it; deferred debts or those incurred without the master's permission are not defects. The passage then quotes al-Tuhfah, which categorizes defects into two types: those that cause loss of a part or change in appearance (like blindness, missing finger, etc.) and those that cause deficiency in meaning without altering appearance (like chronic cough, long cessation of menses, redness of hair, grayness, pregnancy in a slave girl, etc.). Missing letters in a Quran copy is a defect because it reduces value.

The main ruling discussed is: if a defect that existed with the seller is discovered after another defect has occurred with the buyer, the buyer cannot return the item (due to the new defect) but can claim compensation (the difference in value) from the seller. However, if the seller consents to accept the return despite the new defect, it is allowed. The method of compensation is to compare the value of the item with the old defect and its value without it, then the buyer recovers that proportion of the price. This is based on the principle that the buyer's right to return sound goods is established, but if a new defect makes return harmful to the seller, the buyer's harm is alleviated by compensation. The passage includes a disagreement with Malik and Ahmad, who allow the buyer to return the item with compensation for the new defect. The Hanafi response is that the buyer's right to return is to remove harm without causing harm to the seller; since the seller would be harmed by receiving a defective item, the buyer can only claim compensation. The text also gives examples: if the buyer cuts a cloth and finds a defect, he can claim compensation; if he sews, dyes, or mixes it, the same applies. If he kills a camel or a slave, no compensation is due because that destroys the value entirely. The passage concludes with a classification of increases: attached increases (like fatness) do not prevent return, but attached increases like dyeing do; detached increases like earnings do not prevent return, but children and fruit do.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-ayb): The right of the buyer to return a defective item or claim compensation when a hidden defect is discovered after sale.
نقصان (nuqsan): The decrease in value caused by the defect; compensation is proportional to the difference in price.
ضرر (ḍarar): Harm; the principle of avoiding harm (la darar) is central to the Hanafi proof against return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a buyer discovers an old defect after a new defect has occurred (e.g., cutting a cloth), what is the buyer's primary recourse?
A) The buyer can return the item with compensation for the new defect.
B) The buyer can return the item only if the seller has no objection.
C) The buyer can claim compensation for the old defect but cannot return.
D) The buyer has no recourse whatsoever.

Key Takeaway
When a new defect prevents return, the buyer's remedy is proportional compensation, not return, to avoid harming the seller.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
12 min
Method of Compensation and Effect of Increases
Detail on compensation calculation and rules for increases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the deficiency amount and determine whether different types of increases (ziyadat) affect the right to return or compensation.

Core Idea
Compensation is proportional to the value loss, and attached increases can prevent return while detached ones may not.

After understanding that compensation is the remedy, this lesson dives into how to calculate it precisely and how increases like sewing, dyeing, or the birth of a child impact the buyer's rights.

Guided Path
1
Learn the valuation method
The deficiency is calculated by comparing the item's value with the defect (qīmah bi hādhā al-ʿayb) to its value without any defect (qīmah sawmaʿan). The proportion of the difference is applied to the actual price paid.
Example: if defect reduces value by 1/10, buyer recovers 1/10 of the price.
2
Identify when compensation is not due
If the buyer totally destroys the item (e.g., slaughtering a camel, killing a slave), no compensation is due because the item's value is completely eliminated. This is per Abu Hanifa.
This is an exception to the general rule of compensation.
3
Classify increases (ziyadat)
Increases are divided into attached (muttaṣila) and detached (munfaṣila). Attached increases are further divided into those arising from the original (e.g., fatness) which do not prevent return, and those added (sewing, dyeing) which prevent return. Detached increases: generated (child, fruit) prevent return; earnings do not prevent return.
The key distinction is whether the increase is part of the item's nature or external.
4
Apply to examples
If a buyer sews a cloth and then finds a defect, the sewing prevents return, so compensation is claimed. If a slave gives birth (detached generated increase), return is prevented. If the slave earns money (detached non-generated), return is still allowed.
There is an intra-Hanafi difference: if the buyer sews then sells, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say recourse is not invalidated because return was already prevented by the sewing.

(عيني) أو رده إلخ: أي لو حدث عيب عند المشتري واطلع على عيب كان عند البائع، رجع بنقصانه؛ لأنه استحق تسليمه سليماً . عن العيب أو رد المبيع فيما يملك البائع إسقاط حقه وتملكه، فإذا رضي البائع يخير المشتري بين الرد والإمساك من غير رجوع بنقصان، وإنما يرجع بالنقصان حيث لم يرض البائع بالرد. وطريق معرفة النقصان بأن يقوم بهذا العيب، ثم يقوّم وهو سالم، فإذا عرف التفاوت الذي بين القيمتين يرجع عليه بحصته من الثمن حتى إذا كان النقصان عشر القيمة، يرجع عليه بعشر الثمن، وإن كان ثلثاً فبثلثه، وقال مالك وأحمد في رواية: للمشتري أن يردّه وأن يرد معه نقصان العيب الحادث عند المشتري؛ لقيام المجوز للرد ، وهو الاطلاع على العيب. ولنا أن حق الرد ثبت للمشتري ليدفع به الضرر عن نفسه على وجه لا يتضرر به البائع، فلو رده بعد ما تعيب عنده يتضرر به البائع؛ لأنه خرج عن ملكه سليماً فلا يلزمه، وضرر المشتري أمكن رفعه بالرجوع بحصته من الثمن. (عيني، فتح، زيلعي) بائعه لأنه به، رضي بالتزام الضرر وبدون رضائه لا يرد (عيني) رجع بالعيب: أي رجع المشتري بنقصان العيب، بخلاف ما إذا اشترى بعيراً، فنحره، فوجد أمعاءه فاسدة، حيث لا يرجع بالنقصان عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن النحر إفساد للمالية كما إذا كان عبداً فقتله المشتري. (رمز الحقائق: ٢٤/٢) (فتح) له ذلك: أي للبائع يجوز القبول؛ لأن الامتناع لحقه وقد رضي بإسقاط حقه. (عيني) يرجع به، بخلاف لم يرجع إلخ مطلقا سواء كان عالما بالعيب وقت البيع أو لا، وهو ظاهر الرواية، وعنهما: أنه ما إذا خاطه ثم باعه حيث لا يبطل الرجوع بالنقصان؛ لأنه لم يصر حابساً له بالبيع لامتناع الرد قبله بالخياطة من غير علم بالعيب وبيعه بعد امتناع الرد لا تأثير له (رمز الحقائق: ٢٥/٢) (مسكين) بشيء على البائع؛ لأنه صار حابسا للمبيع بالبيع إذ الرد غير ممتنع بالقطع برضاء البائع، فكان مفوتا للرد. (عيني) فلو قطعه: أي قطع المشتري و لم يخطه أو صبغه : بأي صبغ كان مما يزيد قيمة الثوب. (ط، عيني) أو لت: أي خلط السويق الذي اشتراه عيني، ط على عيب أي في الثوب أو السويق، كان عند البائع في هذه الأشياء. (ط، عيني) رجع بنقصانه: أي رجع المشتري على البائع بنقصان العيب؛ لتعذر الرد بسبب الزيادة. (عيني) كما لو باعه إلخ: أي لو باع المشتري الثوب المخيط أو الثوب المصبوغ أو السويق الملتوت رجع بنقصانه لتعذر الرد بسبب الزيادة، فكذا لو باعه بعد رؤية العيب يرجع بالنقصان واعلم أن الزيادة نوعان: متصلة، ومنفصلة، والمتصلة نوعان: متولدة من الأصل كالسمن والجمال، وهي لا تمنع الرد؛ لأن الزيادة تبع محض باعتبار التولد، وغير متولدة كالصبغ والخياطة واللت وهي تمنع الرد بالعيب ،اتفاقا والمنفصلة نوعان متولدة كالولد والثمر، وهي تمنع الرد، وغير متولدة كالكسب وهي لا تمنع الرد بالعيب، والفرق أن الكسب ليس بمبيع بحال ما؛ لأنه .
Chapter of option of defect. Book of sales. Istihadah (chronic menstruation), chronic cough, debt, abscess (or back wound), hair, and water in the eye. Then if another defect occurs with the buyer, he resorts to (claiming) its deficiency, or returns (the item) with the seller's consent. (Ayni) Istihadah: continuous blood, it is a sign of illness in a mature woman. (Ayni, Ma'din) Chronic cough: with damm on sin, meaning istifa (chronicity?) because its continuity indicates illness. (Ayni) The apparent meaning that it be old means it is from illness, so the consideration is that it be from illness, not oldness; and it excludes ordinary cough and cold, for these are not defects. (Bahr al-Ra'iq, Jawhari) Debt: because his financial liability is occupied with it, and what is meant is what is demanded immediately; in some copies instead of "debt" is "al-dabar" with ba and ra meaning a wound on the back, i.e., the debt that has a demand immediately, i.e., before emancipation, like the debt of maintenance if he marries with the master's permission. It is not sold time after time except only in the debt of maintenance; because the slave's financial value is occupied with the debt and the creditors take precedence over the master. This reasoning requires restricting debt to when the price does not cover it; if it does, it is not a defect at that time. Deferred debt until after emancipation, like a debt incurred by transaction without permission, is not a defect. (Fath Ayni) And hair and water etc. And in al-Tuhfah: Defects are of two types: one: what necessitates loss of a part of the sold item and changes it in appearance but not inner. As for the first, many like blindness, one-eyedness, deafness, paralysis, chronic illness, missing finger, black tooth, missing tooth, black nail, muteness, speechlessness, ulcers, head wounds, scar of wound, and diseases in the rest of the body like fevers. As for the second, what necessitates deficiency in meaning without form, like chronic cough and cessation of menses for a long time, the minimum being two months or more in slave girls. Among them: reddishness of hair i.e. redness if excessive to the point of whiteness, mixed gray i.e. mixing of white with black in hair, grayness in male and female slaves, pregnancy in a slave girl is a defect but not in animals, marriage in a slave girl and slave boy is a defect, swelling of testicles, extra finger, impotence, castration is a defect, lack of circumcision in an adult male slave if born in Islam is a defect, but if brought from dar al-harb it is not a defect as if he were young, and missing letters in the entire Quran or part of it is a defect; because all this decreases the price among merchants. (Ayni, Miskin) In the eye, it is restricted to both because they weaken sight and cause blindness. (T, Ayni) With the buyer: after his awareness of the defect that was with the seller. (Ayni) He resorts to its deficiency because return is prevented due to the newly occurring defect. (Ayni) Or his return etc.: i.e. if a defect occurs with the buyer and he becomes aware of a defect that was with the seller, he resorts to its deficiency because he was entitled to receive it sound. As for returning the sold item, the seller has the right to forfeit his right and take possession. So if the seller consents, the buyer chooses between return and retention without resorting to deficiency. And he only resorts to deficiency if the seller does not consent to return. The method of knowing the deficiency is by valuing it with this defect, then valuing it sound; if the difference between the two values is known, he resorts to his share of the price, so if the deficiency is one-tenth of the value, he resorts to one-tenth of the price; if one-third, to one-third. Malik and Ahmad in one narration said: the buyer may return it and return with it the deficiency of the new defect that occurred with the buyer, because the justification for return exists, which is awareness of the defect. Our proof: the right of return was established for the buyer to remove harm from himself in a way that does not harm the seller. If he returns it after it became defective with him, the seller is harmed because it left his ownership sound and he is not obligated, and the buyer's harm can be removed by resorting to his share of the price. (Ayni, Fath, Zayla'i) His seller because he consented to bear the harm, and without his consent he does not return. (Ayni) He resorts to the defect: i.e. the buyer resorts to the deficiency of the defect, contrary to if he bought a camel and slaughtered it and found its intestines spoiled, where he does not resort to deficiency according to Abu Hanifa, because slaughter is destruction of value as if he bought a slave and killed him. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 24/2) (Fath) He has that: i.e. the seller may accept because the refusal is for his right and he has consented to forfeit his right. (Ayni) He resorts to it, contrary to... he does not resort at all, whether he was aware of the defect at the time of sale or not, and this is the apparent narration. And from them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): that if he sewed it then sold it, the recourse to deficiency is not invalidated because he did not become a preventer of return by sale since return was prevented before by sewing without knowledge of the defect, and his selling it after prevention of return has no effect. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 25/2) (Miskin) Something on the seller because he became a preventer of the sold item by sale since return is not prevented by cutting with the seller's consent, so he was a thwart of return. (Ayni) So if he cut it: i.e. the buyer cut it and did not sew it, or dyed it: with any dye that increases the value of the cloth. (T, Ayni) Or mixed: i.e. mixed the sawiq that he bought with ghee. (Ayni, T) on a defect i.e. in the cloth or sawiq, that was with the seller in these things. (T, Ayni) He resorts to its deficiency: i.e. the buyer resorts to the seller for deficiency of the defect because return is prevented due to the increase. (Ayni) As if he sold it etc.: i.e. if the buyer sold the sewn cloth or dyed cloth or mixed sawiq, he resorts to deficiency because return is prevented due to the increase; similarly if he sold it after seeing the defect, he resorts to deficiency. And know that increase is of two types: attached and detached. Attached is of two types: arising from the original like fatness and beauty, and it does not prevent return because the increase is a pure follow due to generation; and not arising like dye, sewing, and mixing, and these prevent return by defect unanimously. Detached is of two types: arising like child and fruit, and it prevents return; and not arising like earnings, and it does not prevent return by defect, and the difference is that earnings are not part of the sold item at all because...
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses the chapter on the option of defect (khiyar al-ayb) in sales. It begins by listing several conditions that constitute defects: istihadah (chronic menstruation), chronic cough, debt (that is immediately due), an abscess or wound on the back, hair, and water in the eye. It explains each in detail, noting that a chronic cough is only a defect if it stems from illness, not from a temporary cold. Debt is a defect if the slave's value is tied up and the sale price does not cover it; deferred debts or those incurred without the master's permission are not defects. The passage then quotes al-Tuhfah, which categorizes defects into two types: those that cause loss of a part or change in appearance (like blindness, missing finger, etc.) and those that cause deficiency in meaning without altering appearance (like chronic cough, long cessation of menses, redness of hair, grayness, pregnancy in a slave girl, etc.). Missing letters in a Quran copy is a defect because it reduces value.

The main ruling discussed is: if a defect that existed with the seller is discovered after another defect has occurred with the buyer, the buyer cannot return the item (due to the new defect) but can claim compensation (the difference in value) from the seller. However, if the seller consents to accept the return despite the new defect, it is allowed. The method of compensation is to compare the value of the item with the old defect and its value without it, then the buyer recovers that proportion of the price. This is based on the principle that the buyer's right to return sound goods is established, but if a new defect makes return harmful to the seller, the buyer's harm is alleviated by compensation. The passage includes a disagreement with Malik and Ahmad, who allow the buyer to return the item with compensation for the new defect. The Hanafi response is that the buyer's right to return is to remove harm without causing harm to the seller; since the seller would be harmed by receiving a defective item, the buyer can only claim compensation. The text also gives examples: if the buyer cuts a cloth and finds a defect, he can claim compensation; if he sews, dyes, or mixes it, the same applies. If he kills a camel or a slave, no compensation is due because that destroys the value entirely. The passage concludes with a classification of increases: attached increases (like fatness) do not prevent return, but attached increases like dyeing do; detached increases like earnings do not prevent return, but children and fruit do.

Key Terms
زيادة متصلة (ziyāda muttaṣila): An increase that is physically attached to the sold item, such as sewing, dyeing, or fatness.
زيادة منفصلة (ziyāda munfaṣila): An increase that is separate from the sold item, such as a child, fruit, or earnings.
متولدة (mutawallida): Generated from the item itself, such as fatness, child, or fruit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases a sheep, the sheep gives birth to a lamb (detached generated increase), and then the buyer finds a pre-existing defect. What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The buyer can return the sheep and keep the lamb.
B) The buyer cannot return the sheep but can claim compensation.
C) The buyer can return both the sheep and the lamb.
D) The buyer has no recourse.

Key Takeaway
Compensation is based on the proportion of value loss, and the type of increase determines whether return is possible or compensation is the only remedy.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Alteration of Defective Goods: When Return Is Blocked, Compensation Must Follow
Ruling with proof on buyer's actions that bar return of defective goods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for when a buyer's alteration prevents return of defective goods, calculate the compensation, and evaluate the opposing Maliki/Hanbali position.

Core Idea
A buyer who alters defective goods cannot return them but can claim compensation proportional to the value reduction.

This passage from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies a key principle in the option of defect (khiyar al-‘ayb). When a buyer alters a purchased item (e.g., cuts, sews, dyes) and later discovers a pre-existing defect, the alteration blocks return but does not extinguish the right to compensation. Understanding this distinction is essential for applying the Hanafi rule that harm to the seller is avoided while the buyer's loss is remedied.

Guided Path
1
Identify the buyer's actions and their rulings
List the specific actions mentioned: cutting without sewing, selling after cutting, cutting and sewing, dyeing, mixing with ghee. For each, note whether return is allowed or compensation is given.
Cutting alone (without sale) does not bar compensation claim; selling does.
2
Extract the compensation method
Understand how compensation is calculated: value the item with the defect, then value it sound, then claim the proportionate share of the price (e.g., if loss is 1/10 of value, claim 1/10 of price).
This is a proportional claim, not a fixed amount.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The right to return was established to remove harm from the buyer without harming the seller. Returning after alteration harms the seller because the seller gave it up sound. Compensation removes harm from the buyer without harming the seller.
The proof is based on avoiding harm (la darar wa la dirar).
4
Examine the inter-madhhab dispute
State the Maliki/Hanbali position (return allowed plus compensation for new defect) and the Hanafi response. Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).
The dispute hinges on whether the justification for return (discovery of defect) remains valid after alteration.
5
Apply the typology of increases
Distinguish between connected increases (e.g., dyeing, sewing) which prevent return, and disconnected increases (e.g., fattening, fruit) which may also affect return rules. Note that some disconnected increases (like earnings) do not prevent return.
See the detailed classification in the commentary.

بائعه، ومن اشترى ثوباً فقطعه فوجد به عيباً رجع بالعيب، فإن قبله أي المشتري المبيع و لم يخطه أي بالثوب أي المشتري على البائع كذلك، أي قبل الثوب المذكور مقطوعا له ذلك، فإن باعه المشتري لم يرجع بشيء، فلو قطعه وخاطه، أو صبغه، أولت الثوب المذكور بالسمن، فاطلع على عيب رجع بنقصانه كما لو باعه بعد رؤية العيب، أي كما يرجع بالنقصان أن يقوم بهذا العيب، ثم يقوّم وهو سالم، فإذا عرف التفاوت الذي بين القيمتين يرجع عليه بحصته من الثمن حتى إذا كان النقصان عشر القيمة، يرجع عليه بعشر الثمن، وإن كان ثلثاً فبثلثه، وقال مالك وأحمد في رواية: للمشتري أن يردّه وأن يرد معه نقصان العيب الحادث عند المشتري؛ لقيام المجوز للرد، وهو الاطلاع على العيب. ولنا أن حق الرد ثبت للمشتري ليدفع به الضرر عن نفسه على وجه لا يتضرر به البائع، فلو رده بعد ما تعيب عنده يتضرر به البائع؛ لأنه خرج عن ملكه سليماً فلا يلزمه، وضرر المشتري أمكن رفعه بالرجوع بحصته من الثمن. (عيني، مسكين، عناية)
He sold it, and whoever buys a garment and cuts it and finds a defect, he claims for the defect. If the buyer accepts the sold item and does not sew it, he may do so. If he sells it, he claims nothing. If he cuts and sews it, or dyes it, or mixes the food with ghee, then finds a defect, he claims for the loss, as if he sold it after seeing the defect. He values it with the defect, then values it sound, then claims the proportion of the price. If the loss is one-tenth of the value, he claims one-tenth of the price; if a third, a third. Malik and Ahmad in one narration said: the buyer may return it and return with it the loss of the new defect, because the justification for return (discovery of defect) still exists. Our proof: the right to return was established for the buyer to remove harm from himself without harming the seller. If he returns after it became defective with him, it would harm the seller because he gave it up sound; the buyer's harm can be removed by claiming his share of the price. ... (continues with further commentary on nuances and types of increase).
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the chapter of option of defect from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It explains the ruling when a buyer performs actions on defective goods that prevent return. The author lists scenarios: cutting without sewing (still allows claim), selling after cutting (bars claim), cutting and sewing (bars return but allows claim for loss), dyeing, mixing with ghee (same as sewing). The method of compensation is proportional to depreciation. A dispute with Malik and Ahmad is recorded: they allow return plus compensation for new defect. Hanafi view: return would harm seller, so only compensation. The passage then distinguishes between connected and disconnected increases, detailing which prevent return (e.g., dyeing does, fattening does not) based on whether the increase is inseparable or separate from the original item.

Key Terms
عيب (‘ayb): A defect in the purchased item that existed at the time of sale but was unknown to the buyer.
نقصان (nuqsān): The difference in value caused by the defect, used to calculate compensation when return is not possible.
زيادة متصلة (ziyādah muttaṣilah): An increase to the item that is physically connected and cannot be separated, such as dyeing or sewing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases a piece of cloth, cuts and sews it into a shirt, then discovers a hole that existed at the time of sale. What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The buyer can return the shirt and get a full refund.
B) The buyer can only claim compensation for the defect; return is not allowed.
C) The buyer cannot claim anything because he altered the cloth.
D) The buyer must return the shirt but can claim compensation for the hole.

Key Takeaway
A buyer who alters a defective item with a connected increase cannot return it but retains the right to claim compensation for the pre-existing defect.

Buyer's Destruction of Defective Goods: Death, Freeing, Killing, and Eating
Rulings when the buyer destroys the defective item and its effect on compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rulings for each buyer act that destroys defective goods, identify the role of istihsan in overriding qiyas, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
When the buyer destroys defective goods, compensation is allowed by istihsan in freeing and eating, but not in killing by Abu Hanifa.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq systematically covers five scenarios where the buyer's action prevents returning the defective item. The rulings illustrate the Hanafi method of balancing qiyas (analogy) with istihsan (juristic preference) to achieve practical justice. We will examine each case, the reasoning, and the disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five buyer actions
List all buyer actions that destroy or dispose of the defective item: death of a slave, freeing, kitaba (contract of manumission), killing, and eating.
Each leads to impossibility of return.
2
Determine the default qiyas ruling
For each action, think about what strict analogy would require. For example, killing and eating are liable acts that would normally bar any claim (qiyas to selling).
Qiyas generally bars compensation when the buyer's liable act prevents return.
3
Apply istihsan where applicable
Identify where the Hanafi school departs from qiyas via istihsan. For freeing and eating, istihsan allows compensation because the act ends ownership by law or is customary consumption.
Istihsan is preferred over qiyas for these cases.
4
Note intra-Hanafi disputes
For killing and eating, there are differences among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad. For killing, Abu Yusuf allows claim; for eating, Sahibayn allow claim.
For kitaba, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf also differ.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The text explicitly states that the fatwa is on the view of Sahibayn for eating. For other disputed cases, no clear tarjih is given, so the Imam's position is generally followed by default unless specified.
The mufta bihi is crucial for practical fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(ط)] عطف على "باع" أي كذلك يرجع بالنقصان لو مات العبد لانتهاء الملك بالموت، وامتناع رده على البائع حكمي ولا بفعل المشتري، فلا يمنع الرجوع بالنقصان، ولا فرق بين أن يكون الموت بعد رؤية العيب أو قبله. (عيني) يرجع أو أعتقه مجاناً : [ أي العبد بلا مال ثم اطلع على العيب، فإنه يرجع بالنقصان والتدبير والاستيلاد كالإعتاق. (ط)] أي كذلك بالنقصان إذا أعتق العبد، والمراد من الإعتاق أن يوجد منه قبل العلم بالعيب، وإن أعتقه بعد يرجع العلم به لا يرجع بالنقصان؛ لأن إقدامه على الإعتاق دليل الرضاء، والقياس أن لا يرجع بالنقصان، وإن كان قبل العلم بالعيب، وهو قول الشافعي كالقتل؛ رمز الحقائق: ٢٥/٢] لامتناع الرد بفعله، فصار كقتلة بجامع الاشتراك في إفساد المالية. وجه الاستحسان : أن الإعتاق إنهاء للملك، فامتنع الرد بحكم الشرع، فصار كالموت، وكذلك لو دبره أو استولد الجارية. (فتح) أو قتله: أي كذلك لم يرجع إذا قتل المشتري العبد قبل اطلاعه على العيب؛ لأن الرد امتنع بفعله، وهو مضمون عليه في الآخرة، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يرجع بالنقصان؛ لأن قتله لا يتعلق به حكم دنيوي من القصاص والدية، فكان كالموت حتف أنفه. بسبب هو عن القصاص كان عمداً، والدية إن كان خطأ، ولنا: أن القتل لا يكون إلا مضمونا؛ لقوله عليه: "ليس في الإسلام دم مفرج أي مبطل، وإنما سقط عن المولى الملك، فصار كالمستفيد به عوضاً، سلامة نفسه فكأنه باعه حتى لو كان مديوناً ضمنه بخلاف امتناع الردّ بفعله في نحو صبغ الثوب؛ لأن هناك امتناع الرد بسبب زيادة المبيع لحق الشرع، وهو شبهة الربا والعين قائم على حاله. (عيني، فتح) فأكله أي كله أو بعضه قبل الاطلاع على العيب، فلا يرجع بشيء عند أبي حنيفة؛ لتعذر رده بفعل مضمون من المشتري في المبيع فأشبه البيع، وقالا يرجع بالنقصان؛ لأنه فعل ما يقصد بشرائه ويعتاد فعله فأشبه الإعتاق، والفتوى على قولهما، وبه قالت الثلاثة وأخذه الطحاوي رمز الحقائق: ٢٥/٢] (فتح)
Or if the slave dies: similarly, he claims for the loss because the goods perished at the buyer's place after discovery of defect; return is prevented literally by destruction. This is connected to 'sold' meaning similarly he claims for the loss if the slave dies because ownership ends with death, and prevention of return is legal, not by buyer's act. No difference whether death after seeing the defect or before. Or if he frees him without payment: similarly claims for loss if he frees the slave before knowledge of defect. The intended meaning of freeing is that it occurs before knowledge; if after knowledge, he does not claim because his act indicates satisfaction. Qiyas (by Shafi'i) says no claim because the buyer's act prevents return like killing. The istihsan: freeing ends ownership by law, so like death. Similarly for tadbir and istilad. If he makes a kitaba contract, he claims nothing because he holds the slave for the price, and holding the price is like holding the item. From Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's view: he claims for the loss. If he kills him: similarly does not claim because return prevented by his act, and he is liable in the afterlife. From Abu Yusuf: he claims because killing has no worldly consequence, so like natural death. Our proof: killing is always liable; the Prophet said: 'There is no blood wasted in Islam.' He gives up ownership, so it is as if he sold it. If he eats it (all or part) before discovery, he claims nothing according to Abu Hanifa because his act prevents return, like selling. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say he claims for the loss because eating is the intended use, like freeing. The fatwa is on their view, and the three imams and Tahawi adopted it.

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the option of defect, now focusing on cases where the buyer destroys the goods. The rulings are: (1) If the slave dies naturally, the buyer can still claim compensation for the defect because death prevents return by legal operation, not by the buyer's act. (2) If the buyer frees the slave without payment before knowing the defect, he can claim compensation (by istihsan, comparing to death). Shafi'i qiyas compares it to killing, thus no claim. (3) If he makes a kitaba contract, Abu Hanifa says no claim because he holds the slave for payment; but Abu Yusuf says claim. (4) If he kills the slave, Abu Hanifa says no claim because his act is blameworthy; Abu Yusuf allows claim because no worldly punishment. (5) If he eats the food, Abu Hanifa says no claim (like selling); Sahibayn say claim because eating is normal use. The fatwa is on Sahibayn's view for eating.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsān): Juristic preference: departure from qiyas for a stronger evidence or necessity, used to allow claim for compensation when freeing or eating.
عتق (‘itq): Freeing a slave; in this context, it ends the buyer's ownership and thus prevents return, but istihsan allows compensation claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) He can claim compensation for the defect from the seller.
D) He must pay the seller the full price.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses istihsan to allow compensation for defective goods in freeing and eating, even though qiyas would bar the claim, and the fatwa follows the Sahibayn on eating.

Death of the Sold Item Does Not Forfeit the Right to Claim Reduction
Explanation that death does not forfeit the right to claim reduction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when the sold item perishes after defect discovery and explain why the buyer retains the right to claim the reduction (arsh).

Core Idea
Death of the sold item does not forfeit the buyer's right to compensation for defects, because the impediment is not by the buyer's action.

In the previous lesson, we learned that when a buyer discovers a defect, he may return the item or claim the arsh (reduction). But what if the item perishes before return? This passage clarifies that if the item dies (or is destroyed) after the buyer knows of the defect, the right to claim the reduction remains. The key distinction is whether the destruction was caused by the buyer or by an external factor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the disputed ruling
The scenario is a purchased slave who dies after the buyer discovers a defect. The ruling is that the buyer can still claim the reduction in price (arsh) from the seller.
The matn phrase 'aw mata al-'abd' triggers this explanation.
2
Understand the legal impediment
Death prevents actual return of the item (real impediment), but this prevention is not due to any action by the buyer.
Prevention is 'hukmi' (legal/judicial) not 'hissi' (physical) by buyer.
3
Distinguish between real and legal prevention
If the buyer himself causes destruction (e.g., kills the slave), he forfeits his right to compensation. But if destruction is by act of God, the right remains.
This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Apply the principle of istihsan
Qiyas (analogy) would suggest no compensation because the item is destroyed. However, istihsan (juristic preference) allows compensation because the buyer did not cause the destruction.
Istihsan overrides qiyas here.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that death forfeits compensation entirely. The Hanafi school differs and this ruling is mufta bihi.
The difference stems from different usul principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو مات العبد: [ أي كذلك بالنقصان إذا هلك المبيع عند المشتري بعد اطلاعه على العيب؛ لأن الرد امتنع حقيقة بالهلاك. (ط)] عطف على "باع" أي كذلك يرجع بالنقصان لو مات العبد لانتهاء الملك بالموت، وامتناع رده على البائع حكمي ولا بفعل المشتري، فلا يمنع الرجوع بالنقصان، ولا فرق بين أن يكون الموت بعد رؤية العيب أو قبله. (عيني)
Or if the slave dies: [meaning similarly he claims the reduction if the sold item perishes with the buyer after he becomes aware of the defect, because return is prevented in reality by perishing. (T)] It is a conjunction on 'sold' meaning similarly he claims the reduction if the slave dies because ownership ends by death, and the prevention of returning it to the seller is legal (hukmi) and not by the buyer's action, so it does not prevent claiming the reduction. There is no difference whether the death occurred after seeing the defect or before. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement 'or if the slave dies'. It clarifies that if the purchased slave dies after the buyer discovers a defect, the buyer can still claim compensation for the defect (arjūʻ bi al-nuqṣān). The reason is that the death prevents actual return of the item, but this prevention is by divine decree (hukmi), not by the buyer's own action. Therefore, it does not preclude the buyer's right to seek compensation for the diminution in value caused by the defect. The ruling applies regardless of whether the death occurred before or after the buyer saw the defect. This is based on istihsan, as qiyas would suggest no compensation because the item is destroyed. But istihsan prevails because the buyer did not cause the destruction.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option of the buyer to return a defective item or claim compensation for the defect after discovery.
الرجوع بالنقصان (al-ruju' bi al-nuqṣān): Claiming the difference in value between a sound item and a defective item from the seller as compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The buyer has no recourse because he saw the defect.

Key Takeaway
The buyer's right to compensation for a defect is not forfeited by the death of the item if the death was not caused by the buyer.

Freeing a Defective Slave Without Payment: Istihsan Over Qiyas
Distinguishing between destructive and legal termination of ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a buyer frees a slave without payment before discovering a defect and explain why istihsan permits compensation despite the analogy to killing.

Core Idea
Freeing a slave before defect discovery is like death, not killing – compensation is due via istihsan.

When a buyer frees a purchased slave for free and later discovers a defect, can he claim a price reduction? Strict analogy (qiyas) says no, because his own act prevents return, similar to killing the slave. However, the Hanafi school prefers istihsan, treating the freeing as a legal termination of ownership akin to death, and therefore allows compensation. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the critical distinction between destructive and non-destructive actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The buyer frees the slave without payment (gratuitously) before learning of any defect. After freeing, he discovers the defect. The question: can he still claim compensation (al-naqsan) from the seller?
2
State the qiyas (analogy) argument
The Shafi‘i view relies on qiyas: freeing prevents return of the slave, just as killing does. Since the buyer’s own act destroys the possibility of return, no compensation is due.
Killing shares the 'illa of spoiling the monetary value (ifsad al-maliyyah).
3
Apply istihsan – the Hanafi counter-argument
The Hanafi school uses istihsan: freeing is a legal act that terminates ownership by the Lawgiver’s ruling, not by the buyer’s destruction. It is analogous to death, not to killing. The impediment to return is legal (shar‘i), not physical.
The key distinction: freeing does not destroy the slave’s value; it transfers ownership to the slave himself via a legal ruling.
4
Note the condition: timing of freeing
The Hanafi relaxation applies only if the freeing occurred before knowledge of the defect. If the buyer freed after learning the defect, his action indicates satisfaction (rida) and forfeits the right to compensation.
This condition prevents abuse – the buyer cannot escape loss if he knew and still freed.
5
Extend to similar cases
The same rule applies to tadbir (declaring freedom after the master’s death) and istilad (bearing a child to the master) – both are legal terminations of ownership, not destructive acts, so compensation is still due.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو أعتقه مجاناً : [ أي العبد بلا مال ثم اطلع على العيب، فإنه يرجع بالنقصان والتدبير والاستيلاد كالإعتاق. (ط)] أي كذلك بالنقصان إذا أعتق العبد، والمراد من الإعتاق أن يوجد منه قبل العلم بالعيب، وإن أعتقه بعد يرجع العلم به لا يرجع بالنقصان؛ لأن إقدامه على الإعتاق دليل الرضاء، والقياس أن لا يرجع بالنقصان، وإن كان قبل العلم بالعيب، وهو قول الشافعي كالقتل؛ رمز الحقائق: ٢٥/٢] لامتناع الرد بفعله، فصار كقتلة بجامع الاشتراك في إفساد المالية. وجه الاستحسان : أن الإعتاق إنهاء للملك، فامتنع الرد بحكم الشرع، فصار كالموت، وكذلك لو دبره أو استولد الجارية. (فتح)
Or if he frees it for free: [meaning the slave without payment then he becomes aware of the defect, then he claims the reduction, and tadbir and istilad are like freeing. (T)] Meaning similarly he claims the reduction if he frees the slave, and the intended meaning of freeing is that it occurs from him before knowledge of the defect. If he frees it after knowledge, he does not claim the reduction because his proceeding to free is evidence of satisfaction. The qiyas is that he does not claim the reduction even if it was before knowledge of the defect, and this is the saying of al-Shafi'i, like killing, due to prevention of return by his action, so it becomes like his killing due to sharing in spoiling the monetary value. The basis of istihsan is that freeing terminates ownership, so return is prevented by legal ruling, so it becomes like death. Similarly if he makes tadbir or istilad of the slave-girl. (Fath)
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where the buyer frees the slave without any payment before discovering a defect. According to qiyas (analogy), the buyer should not be able to claim compensation because his own action (freeing) prevents return, similar to killing the slave. This is the position of Imam al-Shafi'i. However, the Hanafi school adopts istihsan, which considers that freeing terminates ownership by legal ruling (like death), not by the buyer's destructive action. Therefore, the buyer may claim the reduction. This distinction applies only if the freeing occurred before knowledge of the defect; if after, it indicates satisfaction and no compensation. The same applies to tadbir (declaring the slave free upon death) and istilad (giving birth to the master's child). The proof is that the legal impediment is from the Lawgiver, not the buyer's act, so it resembles death.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): The principle of preferring a ruling that leads to a more equitable outcome over strict analogy (qiyas).
التدبير (al-tadbir): A declaration by the master that a slave will be free upon the master's death.
الاستيلاد (al-istilad): The act of a slave-girl giving birth to the master's child, which makes her an umm walad (mother of the child) and she becomes free upon the master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The buyer cannot claim any compensation because his own action prevented return.
B) The buyer can claim the reduction because freeing is legally like death.
C) The buyer must return the slave and get a full refund.
D) The buyer has no recourse because he freed the slave.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, freeing a defective slave before knowledge of the defect does not forfeit the right to compensation because freeing is a legal termination of ownership, akin to death, not a destructive act.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Buyer's Compensation After Freeing or Killing the Slave
Intra-Hanafi dispute on buyer's compensation after freeing or killing the defective slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on compensation when a buyer frees a defective slave for payment or kills him before discovering the defect, and distinguish the arguments of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
The buyer who destroys or legally disables the subject matter forfeits compensation according to Abu Hanifa.

Imagine you buy a slave, then before you notice a hidden defect, you either set him free upon payment or accidentally kill him. Can you go back to the seller and claim a reduction for the defect? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement. This lesson unpacks the rulings and reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on this precise issue.

Guided Path
1
State the problem
Identify the two scenarios: freeing for payment (including kitaba) and killing. The buyer discovers a defect after the action. The question is whether the buyer can claim a reduction in price.
Both scenarios involve the buyer's own deliberate action.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa says no compensation in either case. His proof: the buyer has 'detained the substitute' (habas al-badal) by his action – freeing creates a legal commitment, killing physically destroys. Detaining the substitute is like detaining the original, so the right to return falls.
The principle: حبس البدل كحبس المبدل.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and his proof
Abu Yusuf says the buyer can claim reduction. He argues that freeing for payment is not a destruction, and killing has no worldly legal consequence (no qisas or diyah) so it is like natural death, allowing compensation.
Abu Yusuf distinguishes between legal and physical obstruction.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to Abu Yusuf
The text provides the Hanafi rebuttal: killing always carries liability (ḥadīth: 'no blood left unavenged'), and the buyer's ownership transfers the benefit to him – it is as if he sold the slave. Therefore, compensation is forfeited.
This is based on a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy) to the sale itself.
5
Identify the mufta bihi and the practical consequence
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view (no compensation). This means in practice the buyer cannot claim a reduction. The dispute (thamarah al-khilaf) is real: under Abu Yusuf, the buyer would get a price reduction; under Abu Hanifa, he gets nothing.
Tarjih is based on the authority of Abu Hanifa's majority view and the strength of the analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أعتقه على مال أو كاتبه ثم اطلع على عيب لم يرجع بشيء؛ لأن المشتري حبس بدله، وحبس البدل كحبس المبدل، وعن أبي حنيفة وهو قول أبي يوسف أنه يرجع بنقصان العيب. ثم اعلم أن المراد بالحبس ليس قبضه؛ إذ من صوره ما لو قال: إن أديت إلي ألفاً في شهر كذا فأنت حر والحاصل أن الكتابة مثل الإعتاق على مال. (فتح) أو قتله: أي كذلك لم يرجع إذا قتل المشتري العبد قبل اطلاعه على العيب؛ لأن الرد امتنع بفعله، وهو مضمون عليه في الآخرة، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يرجع بالنقصان؛ لأن قتله لا يتعلق به حكم دنيوي من القصاص والدية، فكان كالموت حتف أنفه. بسبب هو عن القصاص كان عمداً، والدية إن كان خطأ، ولنا: أن القتل لا يكون إلا مضمونا؛ لقوله عليه: "ليس في الإسلام دم مفرج أي مبطل، وإنما سقط عن المولى الملك، فصار كالمستفيد به عوضاً، سلامة نفسه فكأنه باعه حتى لو كان مديوناً ضمنه بخلاف امتناع الردّ بفعله في نحو صبغ الثوب؛ لأن هناك امتناع الرد بسبب زيادة المبيع لحق الشرع، وهو شبهة الربا والعين قائم على حاله. (عيني، فتح)
For if he frees it for payment or makes a kitaba, then becomes aware of a defect, he does not claim anything because the buyer detained its substitute, and detaining the substitute is like detaining the substituted. And from Abu Hanifa, and it is the saying of Abu Yusuf, that he claims the reduction of the defect. Then know that the intended meaning of detaining is not its seizure; because among its forms is if he said: If you pay me a thousand in such-and-such a month, you are free. The conclusion is that kitaba is like freeing for payment. (Fath) Or he kills it: meaning similarly he does not claim if the buyer kills the slave before becoming aware of the defect, because return is prevented by his action, and it is a liability upon him in the afterlife. And from Abu Yusuf that he claims the reduction, because his killing is not connected to any worldly ruling of retaliation or blood money, so it was like death by natural causes. The reason for the absence of retaliation is if it was intentional, and blood money if it was accidental. And our proof: that killing is always with liability due to his saying عليه: "There is no blood in Islam that is left unavenged," i.e., invalidated. Rather, the ownership fell from the master, so it became like one who benefits from compensation, safety of himself, as if he sold it, such that if he was in debt, he would make it liable, unlike the prevention of return by his action in the case of dyeing the cloth, because there prevention of return is due to increase of the sold item by a legal right, which is the suspicion of riba and the item remains as it is. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage covers two scenarios: (1) The buyer frees the slave for payment (including kitaba), and (2) the buyer kills the slave, both before discovering a defect. According to Abu Hanifa, the buyer does not receive any compensation because his action (freeing for payment or killing) constitutes a detaining of the substitute, which is like detaining the original. However, there is a transmission from Abu Hanifa (also the view of Abu Yusuf) that in the case of freeing for payment, the buyer does claim the reduction. The reason is that 'detaining' here does not mean physical possession but rather a legal commitment. Regarding killing, Abu Hanifa says no compensation because the buyer's action prevents return, and he is liable in the afterlife. Abu Yusuf differs, arguing that since no worldly penalty applies (the killing might be intentional or accidental, but the legal effect is that the slave is no longer property), it is like natural death, so compensation is allowed. The Hanafi response to Abu Yusuf is that killing always carries liability (based on the hadith 'No blood is left unavenged') and the ownership is transferred to the master, so it is as if the buyer sold it. This is contrasted with the case of dyeing cloth, where the prevention of return is due to a legitimate increase (suspicion of riba) but the item still exists.

Key Terms
الكتابة (al-kitaba): A contract where a slave agrees to pay his master a certain sum in installments to earn freedom.
حبس البدل (habs al-badal): The buyer's detaining of the substitute (e.g., the price or the slave service) which acts as a legal impediment to returning the original.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if the buyer kills the slave before discovering a defect?
A) The buyer can claim the full price back.
B) The buyer can claim a reduction for the defect.
C) The buyer cannot claim any compensation.
D) The buyer must pay the seller for the loss.

Key Takeaway
In a sale with a defect, any deliberate action by the buyer that prevents the return of the subject matter (like freeing for payment or killing) forfeits the right to compensation, per the relied-upon Hanafi position.

When the Buyer Eats the Food Before Defect Discovery: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on eating food and compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on compensation when the buyer eats food before defect discovery, explain the qiyas reasoning behind each, and identify which view is the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
The fatwa allows compensation for the defect even after eating — eating is a customary use, not a forfeiture of rights.

Imagine you buy a bag of dates and eat some, only then discovering mold inside. Does your act of eating forfeit your right to complain? In Hanafi fiqh, this is a point of internal disagreement. Abu Hanifa (ra) held that any liable action by the buyer on the item prevents return and compensation, likening it to selling the item. However, his two principal students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), argued that eating is the very purpose of buying food — a customary use — and therefore the buyer should still be able to claim a reduction for the defect. The later Hanafi school adopted their view as the operative fatwa. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind each position and shows how qiyas (analogical reasoning) is applied to different scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the two positions
The buyer eats the food (all or part) before knowing about a defect. State the ruling according to Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
Abu Hanifa: no compensation. Sahibayn: compensation allowed.
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's qiyas
Abu Hanifa analogizes eating to selling the item — both are liable actions that make return impossible. Understand the illa (effective cause): a liable (maddaman) act of the buyer that prevents return.
The asl (original case) is a sale by the buyer: if he sells the defective item to someone else, he loses the right to return. Similarly, eating is a final act that eliminates the possibility of return.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's qiyas
The Sahibayn analogize eating to freeing a slave ('itq). Freeing is an act intended by purchase and customary; it does not forfeit the right to compensation for a defect. Eating is similarly a customary and intended use.
The key difference: the Sahibayn see eating as a natural consequence of buying food, not a deliberate act to undermine the seller's rights.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and thamarah al-khilaf
The text explicitly states 'al-fatwa 'ala qawlihima' — the fatwa is on the view of the Sahibayn. The practical consequence (thamarah) is that courts and muftis today would allow the buyer to claim a reduction.
This ruling is also supported by the three other imams (likely referring to the other three schools) and adopted by Imam al-Tahawi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو قتله ... فأكله أي كله أو بعضه قبل الاطلاع على العيب، فلا يرجع بشيء عند أبي حنيفة؛ لتعذر رده بفعل مضمون من المشتري في المبيع فأشبه البيع، وقالا يرجع بالنقصان؛ لأنه فعل ما يقصد بشرائه ويعتاد فعله فأشبه الإعتاق، والفتوى على قولهما، وبه قالت الثلاثة وأخذه الطحاوي (فتح)
Or he kills it ... or he eats it, meaning all of it or part of it before becoming aware of the defect, so he does not claim anything according to Abu Hanifa, because return is precluded by a liable action of the buyer on the sold item, so it resembles the sale. And they both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said he claims the reduction, because he did an action that is customary and intended by purchase, so it resembles freeing. And the fatwa is on their saying, and this is what the three (imams) said and al-Tahawi adopted it. (Fath)
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage discusses the case where the buyer eats the food (all or part) before discovering a defect. Abu Hanifa rules that the buyer cannot claim any compensation because his act of eating prevents return by a liable action, similar to selling the item (which would forfeit the right). However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the buyer can claim the reduction. Their reasoning is that eating is a customary action intended by the purchase; it is not a deliberate act to forfeit the right, so it is akin to freeing the slave (where compensation is allowed). The fatwa is given according to the Sahibayn's view, and this is also the position of the three other imams (Abu Hanifa's companions?) Actually 'the three' likely refers to the other three schools? No, it says 'the three' meaning Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Tahawi? The text is brief. In any case, the operative ruling (mufta bihi) is that the buyer can claim the reduction for the defect when he has eaten the food.

Key Terms
الفتوى (al-fatwa): The legal ruling that is given in practice; the operative position in the school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fatwa position in the Hanafi school, if a buyer eats food before discovering a defect, what is the ruling?
A) The buyer cannot claim any reduction.
B) The buyer can claim the reduction for the defect.
C) The buyer must return the remains and get a full refund.
D) The buyer has no recourse because he consumed the item.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, consuming food before defect discovery does not forfeit the right to compensation; the fatwa allows claim of reduction based on the Sahibayn's analogy to freeing a slave.
