Ruling When the Buyer Eats Part of the Food (Intra-Hanafi Dispute)
Explanation of the detailed rules for partial consumption and return of remainder

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the different Hanafi positions on partial consumption of a defective food item and state the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
Partial consumption does not automatically forfeit all rights; the mufta bihi allows return of remainder and reduction for eaten part.

When a buyer consumes part of a defective food before discovering the defect, the parties' rights become entangled. The Hanafi school offers multiple views, with the relied-upon fatwa balancing the interests of both buyer and seller. This lesson dissects the opinions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and the final fatwa position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi positions
Identify the rulings of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and the fatwa position regarding partial consumption.
Note that all opinions are within the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the key differences
Pinpoint the conditions each imam attaches to returning the remainder and claiming reduction.
Focus on consent of seller and unconditional return.
3
Analyse the reasoning
Trace the rationale: Abu Hanifa's strictness vs. the Sahibayn's flexibility based on feasibility of partial return.
The concept of 'tab'eed' (partition) is central.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Understand the practical consequence: what the buyer actually recovers under each view.
The fatwa allows both return of remainder and reduction for eaten part.
5
Apply the fatwa
Apply the mufta bihi ruling to a concrete scenario involving partial consumption.
Remember that eating after knowledge nullifies the right by consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو بعضه لم يرجع بشيء، ولو اشترى بيضاً أو قناء أو جوزاً، ووجده فاسداً ينتفع المشتري على البائع به رجع بنقصان العيب، وإلا بكل الثمن كله أو أكثره بالنقصان لم يرجع بشيء: أي لا يرجع بشيء عند أبي حنيفة، ولا يرد ما بقي منه فيما إذا أكله كله، وقالا يرجع . فيما أكل، ثم قال أبو يوسف يرد ما بقي إن رضي البائع؛ لأن استحقاق الرد في البعض دون الكل، فيتوقف على رضاه، وقال محمد: يرد الباقي مطلقاً؛ لأن رده ممكن حيث لا يضره التبعيض، فيرجع بالنقصان فيما أكله؛ لتعذر رده. [رمز الحقائق ٢٦/٢] وعنهما أيضاً أنه يرد ما بقي ويرجع بنقصان ما أكل وعليه الفتوى، ولو كان الأكل بعد العلم بالعيب لم يرجع بالاتفاق، وفي بيع البعض عنهما روايتان: في إحدى الروايتين لا مذهب أبي حنيفة، وفي أخرى يرد ما بقي ويرجع بنقصان عيب ما باع كما قال زفر، والفتوى على أنه لا يرجع بنقصان ويرد الباقي بحصته، وهو رواية عن محمد. (فتح، مسكين)
Or part of it: he does not claim anything. If he buys eggs or qina' or walnuts, and finds them spoiled — if the buyer benefits from it against the seller, he claims the reduction of the defect, otherwise the full price. All of it or most of it: with the reduction he does not claim anything: meaning he does not claim anything according to Abu Hanifa, nor does he return what remains if he ate all of it. And they both said: he claims the reduction for what he ate. Then Abu Yusuf said: he returns what remains if the seller is pleased, because the right to return is for part not the whole, so it depends on his consent. And Muhammad said: he returns the remainder absolutely, because returning it is possible when partition does not harm him, so he claims the reduction for what he ate due to impossibility of its return. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 26/2] And from them both also that he returns what remains and claims the reduction for what he ate, and upon this is the fatwa. If the eating was after knowledge of the defect, he does not claim by consensus. And in the sale of the part, there are two narrations from them: in one narration, it is not the madhhab of Abu Hanifa; in another, he returns what remains and claims the reduction for the defect of what he sold, as Zufr said. And the fatwa is that he does not claim the reduction and returns the remainder with its portion, and this is a narration from Muhammad. (Fath, Miskin)
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage elaborates on partial consumption. If the buyer eats only part of the food before discovering the defect, the rulings vary. Abu Hanifa says the buyer gets nothing and cannot return the remainder. The Sahibayn say he can claim reduction for the eaten part. Abu Yusuf adds that he returns the remainder only if the seller agrees, because returning part without consent may harm the seller. Muhammad says he must return the remainder unconditionally, and claim reduction for the eaten part, because partial return is feasible. The fatwa is on a third view: the buyer returns the remainder and claims reduction for what he ate. If the buyer ate after knowing the defect, he loses the right by consensus. The passage also touches on selling part of the item and its effect on the right to compensation, with multiple narrations; the fatwa is that the buyer returns the remainder and does not claim reduction, but returns it with its portion of the price.

Key Terms
التبعيض (al-tab'eed): Partition or partial return; the feasibility of returning part of a defective item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The buyer can return the remaining half and claim a reduction for the half eaten.
C) The buyer must keep all and claim full reduction.
D) The buyer can only claim reduction for the half eaten but cannot return the remainder.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi position allows the buyer to return the uneaten portion of a defective food and claim a reduction for the consumed part.

Ruling on Spoiled Eggs and Nuts: Full Refund or Reduction?
Explanation of ruling on spoiled items after breaking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on spoiled items after breaking to determine whether the buyer gets a full refund or a reduction.

Core Idea
If a broken item retains any benefit, the buyer claims reduction; if completely useless, full refund.

When a buyer breaks eggs or nuts and finds them spoiled, the Hanafi school distinguishes based on whether the item has any beneficial use. If completely worthless (not fit for humans or animals), the buyer can demand a full refund because it is not considered wealth. If it has some remaining benefit (e.g., fit for fodder or edible by poor), the buyer claims a reduction for the defect, since the act of breaking prevents return. This lesson unpacks the conditions and the inter-madhhab disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition of the item after breaking
Determine if the item is completely without benefit (putrid, hollow) or has some use (fodder, edible by some).
2
Check the buyer's action
Ensure the buyer has not consumed any and broke the items only to test.
If buyer knew defect before breaking and broke anyway, he loses the right to claim.
3
Apply the ruling
If no benefit: full refund. If some benefit: claim reduction (arsh).
The reduction compensates for the defect without returning the item.
4
Consider special cases
Shell value (ostrich eggs) and small proportion of spoilage affect the ruling.
If shell has value, two opinions exist. If spoilage is minimal (1-2%), sale is valid by istihsan.
5
Evaluate the Shafi'i position
Understand the difference: Shafi'i allows return if breaking was necessary; Hanafi gives reduction.
The Hanafi response emphasizes the seller's authorization was in buyer's ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اشترى بيضاً أو قناءً أو جوزاً أو لوزا أو فستقا أو بندقا أو بطيخا فكسره. (ط) عيني) ووجده فاسداً: بأن كان منتناً أو مراً أو خاوياً بحيث لا يصلح لأكل الناس، ولا لعلف الدواب، ولم يتناول منه بعد شيئاً بعد ما ذاقه، فله أن بالثمن كله؛ لأنه تبين بالكسر أنه ليس بمال؛ إذ المال ما ينتفع به، إما في الحال أو في المآل، والمذكور ليس كذلك، فإن كان القثاء فيه لب يأكله بعض الفقراء، أو يصلح للعلف، أو كان بعض لب الجوز ونحوه يؤكل. وأما البيض فلا يتصور فيه الانتفاع؛ لأن قشره لا قيمة له إلا في بيض النعامة؛ لأن ماليته باعتبار القشر، ففي كون هذه الأشياء فاسدة يرجع بنقصان العيب؛ لأنه امتنع الرد بكسر المشتري إلا إذا رضي البائع بالمكسور، وقال الشافعي: إذا كسر مقدار ما لا بد له منه للعلم بالعيب كواحد أو اثنين يرده؛ لأن البائع سلط المشتري بالكسر، قلنا: البائع قد رضي بكسره في ملك المشتري لا في ملك نفسه؛ لأن المبيع لم يبق في ملكه، فلم يكن التسليط إلا في ملك المشتري، فيجب رعاية حقهما بالرجوع بالنقصان. (عيني، عناية) ينتفع به: مع فساده لأكل بعض الناس أو لعلف الدواب. (ط) رجع إلخ: لتعذر الرد بالكسر ، هذا إذا علم بالعيب بعد الكسر ، فلو علم به قبله فكسره لم يرجع. (ط، عيني) وإلا بكل الثمن: [وإن لم يكن منتفعا أصلا جواب المسائل الأربعة (عيني)] أي وإن لم يجد فاسداً منتفعاً به بل وجد غير منتفع به أصلاً، رجع بكل الثمن، هذا إذا لم يكن لقشره قيمة، أما إذا كان لقشره قيمة، قيل: يرجع بحصة اللب، ويصح العقد في القشر بحصته؛ لأنه مال متقوم. وقيل: يرد القشر، ويرجع بكل الثمن، ثم هذا إذا وجد الكل فاسداً، وإن وجد البعض فاسداً وهو قليل، صح البيع استحساناً؛ لأنه لا يخلو عن قليل من الفاسد عادة، فلا يمكن التحرز عنه، وذلك مثل الواحد والاثنين من كل مائة، فليس له أن يخاصم البائع بسببه، وإن كان الفاسد كثيراً لا يصح في الكل، ويرجع بكل الثمن عند أبي حنيفة،
And if he buys eggs or qina' or walnuts or almonds or pistachios or hazelnuts or watermelons and breaks them, and finds them spoiled: such as being putrid or bitter or hollow such that they are not suitable for human consumption nor for animal fodder, and he has not yet consumed anything after tasting. Then he can have the full price because it becomes clear by breaking that it is not wealth; wealth is what is beneficial either immediately or in the future, and these are not so. If the cucumber has a kernel that some poor people eat, or it is suitable for fodder, or some of the walnut kernel etc. is eaten. As for eggs, benefit is not conceivable because their shell has no value except in ostrich eggs because their monetary value is based on the shell. So in the case of these things being spoiled, he claims the reduction of the defect, because return is prevented by the buyer's breaking unless the seller accepts the broken goods. Al-Shafi'i said: If he breaks as much as he needs to know the defect, like one or two, he returns it because the seller authorized the breaking. We said: The seller consented to breaking it in the buyer's ownership, not in his own ownership, because the sold item is no longer in his ownership, so the authorization was only in the buyer's ownership. Thus both rights must be observed by claiming the reduction. (Ayni, Inayah) 'He benefits from it': despite its spoilage for eating by some people or for animal fodder. (T) 'He claims' etc.: due to impossibility of return by breaking. This is if he knows the defect after breaking; if he knows before breaking and then breaks, he does not claim. (T, Ayni) 'Otherwise the full price': [And if it is not beneficial at all, answer to the four questions (Ayni)] meaning if he finds it not beneficial at all, he claims the full price. This is if its shell has no value. If its shell has value, it is said: he claims the portion of the kernel, and the contract is valid for the shell with its portion because it is valuable property. And it is said: he returns the shell and claims the full price. Then this is if all is spoiled. If some is spoiled and it is little, the sale is valid by istihsan because it is customary to have a little spoilage, so it cannot be avoided, like one or two in a hundred, so he cannot sue the seller for it. If the spoilage is much, the sale is invalid entirely and he claims the full price according to Abu Hanifa.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where a buyer purchases eggs, nuts, fruit, etc., and after breaking them open finds them spoiled. The ruling depends on whether the item has any remaining beneficial use. If it is completely useless (not fit for humans or animals), the buyer can return it and claim the full price, because it is not considered wealth. If it has some minor benefit (like being edible for some people or usable as fodder), then the buyer claims only the reduction (arsh) for the defect, because return is prevented by his own action of breaking. The passage also notes an inter-madhhab disagreement: al-Shafi'i holds that the buyer can return the item if he breaks only what is necessary to test (one or two), but the Hanafi respond that the seller only authorized breaking in the buyer's ownership, so both parties' rights are preserved by the reduction. The text further details: if the shell has value (e.g., ostrich eggs), there are two opinions on whether the buyer claims reduction for the kernel or returns the shell and claims full price. Additionally, if only a small proportion of the batch is spoiled (e.g., 1-2%), the sale is valid by istihsan because such a small amount is unavoidable. If a large proportion is spoiled, the sale is invalid and the buyer gets a full refund according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
المال (al-mal): Wealth; that which has beneficial use either immediately or in the future.
الفساد (al-fasad): Spoilage that renders the item unfit for normal use.
العيب (al-ayb): Defect that reduces the value or utility of an item in a sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The buyer can only claim a reduction.

D) The buyer can return the broken eggs to the seller.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on spoiled items after breaking hinges on whether any benefit remains: full refund for useless items, reduction for partially useful items, with special rules for shell value and proportion of spoilage.

Returning a Defective Item After Partial Consumption
Intra-Hanafi dispute on returning a defective item after partial consumption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on returning a defective item after partial consumption and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Partial consumption does not forfeit return—the Hanafi fatwa allows returning the remainder with compensation.

What happens if you buy dates, find some defective, but eat a few first? Can you still return the rest? The Hanafi imams differed on this. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and shows you the relied-upon ruling, which balances the buyer's right to redress with the practical impossibility of returning what was consumed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Recognize that the central issue is whether a buyer can return a defective item after consuming part of it. Write down the three positions: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.
2
Analyze each position's logic
For each imam, extract the reason (ta'lil). Abu Hanifa says no return if all or most is eaten; Abu Yusuf allows return of remainder only with seller's consent; Muhammad allows unconditional return of remainder with compensation.
Focus on how each imam views the possibility of partial return ('tab'id).
3
Identify the mufta bihi
The text states 'وعليه الفتوى' (upon this is the fatwa) after the combined view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. This is the operative position for fatwa.
4
Apply to a hypothetical
Test your understanding: A buyer eats 3 of 10 defective apples. Apply the mufta bihi: return the remaining 7 and claim compensation for the 3 eaten.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو بعضه لم يرجع بشيء: أي لا يرجع بشيء عند أبي حنيفة، ولا يرد ما بقي منه فيما إذا أكله كله، وقالا يرجع . فيما أكل، ثم قال أبو يوسف يرد ما بقي إن رضي البائع؛ لأن استحقاق الرد في البعض دون الكل، فيتوقف على رضاه، وقال محمد: يرد الباقي مطلقاً؛ لأن رده ممكن حيث لا يضره التبعيض، فيرجع بالنقصان فيما أكله؛ لتعذر رده. [رمز الحقائق ٢٦/٢] وعنهما أيضاً أنه يرد ما بقي ويرجع بنقصان ما أكل وعليه الفتوى، ولو كان الأكل بعد العلم بالعيب لم يرجع بالاتفاق، وفي بيع البعض عنهما روايتان: في إحدى الروايتين لا مذهب أبي حنيفة، وفي أخرى يرد ما بقي ويرجع بنقصان عيب ما باع كما قال زفر، والفتوى على أنه لا يرجع بنقصان ويرد الباقي بحصته، وهو رواية عن محمد.
Or part of it: He does not get anything back. Meaning: He does not get anything back according to Abu Hanifa, and he does not return what remains of it if he ate all of it. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He gets back (compensation) for what he ate. Then Abu Yusuf said: He returns what remains if the seller is pleased, because the entitlement to return is for a part without the whole, so it depends on his consent. And Muhammad said: He returns the remainder absolutely, because its return is possible where partiality does not harm him, so he gets back the decrease for what he ate, because its return is impossible. And from them (the two) also that he returns what remains and gets back the decrease for what he ate, and upon this is the fatwa. And if the eating was after knowledge of the defect, he does not get anything back by agreement. And regarding selling part of it, from them (the two) there are two narrations: in one narration, it is the madhhab of Abu Hanifa that he does not get anything back; in another, he returns what remains and gets back the decrease for the defect of what he sold, as Zufar said. And the fatwa is that he does not get back the decrease and he returns the remainder at its portion, and this is a narration from Muhammad.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage discusses the scenario where a buyer consumes part of a defective item after purchase and before returning it. The ruling differs among the Hanafi imams. According to Abu Hanifa, if the buyer eats all or most of the item, he cannot return anything and loses the right to compensation for the defect. However, if he eats only a part, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) allow him to return the remaining part and claim compensation for the eaten portion. Abu Yusuf conditions this return on the seller's consent, while Muhammad allows it unconditionally. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the buyer returns the remaining part and claims compensation for what he ate. If the buyer ate after knowing the defect, he loses all recourse by agreement. If he sold part of the defective item, similar rulings apply.

Key Terms
النقصان (al-nuqsan): The decrease in value due to the defect; compensation paid by the seller to the buyer.
الرد (al-radd): The return of the defective item to the seller.
التبعِـيض (al-tab`id): Partial return, i.e., returning only part of the purchased item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what should a buyer do if he eats part of a defective item before discovering the defect?
A) He cannot return anything.
B) He can return the remaining part and claim compensation for the eaten part.
C) He must return the entire item including the eaten part.
D) He can only claim compensation for the defect without returning anything.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi allows a buyer who consumes part of a defective item before discovering the defect to return the remainder and claim compensation for what was eaten.

Hanafi Ruling on Broken-to-Inspect Items and the Shafi'i Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute on return of broken items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a buyer breaks an item to inspect it and finds it defective, and explain why the Hanafi school does not allow the item to be returned to the seller, distinguishing between items that are completely worthless and those that still have some benefit.

Core Idea
A broken item cannot be returned; the buyer claims compensation unless the item is completely worthless, in which case he gets a full refund.

The Hanafi school addresses a unique scenario: items that must be broken to discover defects, like eggs and nuts. The ruling hinges on whether the item retains any beneficial value after breaking. If it does, the buyer cannot return it because the breaking occurred in his ownership; he instead receives compensation for the defect. If the item is utterly worthless, the sale is void and the full price is returned. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, allowing return if only a necessary number were broken.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
Recognize that the sale involves items that require breaking (e.g., eggs, nuts, watermelons) to inspect for defects.
This special category is governed by additional rules due to the destructive nature of inspection.
2
Determine the nature of the defect
Assess whether the defective item is completely worthless (e.g., rotten egg with no usable shell) or retains some benefit (e.g., pulp for food or fodder).
The definition of 'mal' (property) is key: something with no benefit at all is not considered mal.
3
Apply the Hanafi ruling for each case
If completely worthless, the buyer gets a full refund because no valid contract existed. If some benefit remains, the buyer cannot return the item but claims compensation for the defect.
Compensation is based on the difference in value due to the defect.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i counter-argument
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the buyer can return the item if he broke only the necessary amount (e.g., one or two), because the seller implicitly authorized the break.
The Shafi'i position is based on implied consent for essential inspection.
5
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal is that the seller's permission was for breaking in the buyer's ownership (since the item is already sold), not in the seller's ownership, so return is impossible; compensation protects both parties.
This highlights the Hanafi principle of respecting both parties' rights after the transfer of ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اشترى بيضاً أو قناء أو جوزاً أو لوزا أو فستقا أو بندقا أو بطيخا فكسره. (ط) عيني) ووجده فاسداً: بأن كان منتناً أو مراً أو خاوياً بحيث لا يصلح لأكل الناس، ولا لعلف الدواب، ولم يتناول منه بعد شيئاً بعد ما ذاقه، فله أن بالثمن كله؛ لأنه تبين بالكسر أنه ليس بمال؛ إذ المال ما ينتفع به، إما في الحال أو في المآل، والمذكور ليس كذلك، فإن كان القثاء فيه لب يأكله بعض الفقراء، أو يصلح للعلف، أو كان بعض لب الجوز ونحوه يؤكل. وأما البيض فلا يتصور فيه الانتفاع؛ لأن قشره لا قيمة له إلا في بيض النعامة؛ لأن ماليته باعتبار القشر، ففي كون هذه الأشياء فاسدة يرجع بنقصان العيب؛ لأنه امتنع الرد بكسر المشتري إلا إذا رضي البائع بالمكسور، وقال الشافعي: إذا كسر مقدار ما لا بد له منه للعلم بالعيب كواحد أو اثنين يرده؛ لأن البائع سلط المشتري بالكسر، قلنا: البائع قد رضي بكسره في ملك المشتري لا في ملك نفسه؛ لأن المبيع لم يبق في ملكه، فلم يكن التسليط إلا في ملك المشتري، فيجب رعاية حقهما بالرجوع بالنقصان. (عيني، عناية) ينتفع به: مع فساده لأكل بعض الناس أو لعلف الدواب. (ط) رجع إلخ: لتعذر الرد بالكسر ، هذا إذا علم بالعيب بعد الكسر ، فلو علم به قبله فكسره لم يرجع. (ط، عيني) وإلا بكل الثمن: [وإن لم يكن منتفعا أصلا جواب المسائل الأربعة (عيني)] أي وإن لم يجد فاسداً منتفعاً به بل وجد غير منتفع به أصلاً، رجع بكل الثمن، هذا إذا لم يكن لقشره قيمة، أما إذا كان لقشره قيمة، قيل: يرجع بحصة اللب، ويصح العقد في القشر بحصته؛ لأنه مال متقوم. وقيل: يرد القشر، ويرجع بكل الثمن، ثم هذا إذا وجد الكل فاسداً، وإن وجد البعض فاسداً وهو قليل، صح البيع استحساناً؛ لأنه لا يخلو عن قليل من الفاسد عادة، فلا يمكن التحرز عنه، وذلك مثل الواحد والاثنين من كل مائة، فليس له أن يخاصم البائع بسببه، وإن كان الفاسد كثيراً لا يصح في الكل، ويرجع بكل الثمن عند أبي حنيفة، = 
 
عندهما يجوز بحصة الصحيح منه والمراد بالكثير ما زاد على الثلاثة في قدر المائة، لا الكثير الذي هو زائد على النصف حتى لو اشترى مائة بيضة، فوجد فيها ثلاثة مذرة لا يكون له أن يرجع بشيء ا اتفاقاً، وأما إذا اشترى عشرة من الجوز، فوجد فيها خمسة خاوية، قيل: يجوز البيع في الخمسة الصحيحة بالاتفاق، ويرجع بنصف الثمن، وقيل: يفسد البيع في الكل عنده؛ لأنه جمع بين المال ،وغيره وذلك مفسد للعقد كالجمع بين الحر والقن، وصح البيع في الخمسة الصحيحة عندهما بنصف الثمن؛ لأن الثمن ينقسم على الأجزاء لا على القيمة. (عيني، فتح)
And if he bought eggs or qitha' or walnuts or almonds or pistachios or hazelnuts or watermelon and broke them. (T, Ayni) And found them defective: such as being rotten or bitter or hollow so that they are not suitable for human consumption nor for animal fodder, and he did not take anything from them after tasting. So he can get back the entire price, because it became clear by breaking that it is not property (mal), since property is something that can be benefited from, either in the present or in the future, and the mentioned items are not like that. If the qitha' has pulp that some poor people eat, or it is suitable for fodder, or some of the walnut kernel etc. is eaten. As for eggs, benefit cannot be conceived because their shell has no value except in ostrich eggs, because their value is based on the shell. So in these items being defective, he gets back the decrease due to the defect, because the return is prevented by the buyer's breaking, unless the seller is satisfied with the broken item. And al-Shafi'i said: If he broke an amount that is necessary to know the defect, like one or two, he can return it, because the seller authorized the buyer to break it. We (Hanafis) say: The seller consented to its breaking in the buyer's ownership, not in his own ownership, because the sold item no longer remains in his ownership, so the authorization was only in the buyer's ownership, so regard must be given to both parties by recourse to the decrease. (Ayni, Inayah) Benefiting from it: despite its defect for eating by some people or for animal fodder. (T) 'Returns etc.': because return is prevented by the breaking; this if he knew the defect after breaking; if he knew it before and broke it, he does not get back. (T, Ayni) 'Otherwise the entire price': [And if it is not beneficial at all, an answer to the four questions (Ayni)] i.e., if he did not find defective benefiting from it but found it completely non-beneficial, he gets back the entire price; this if its shell has no value. If its shell has value, it is said: he gets back the portion of the kernel, and the contract is valid for the shell at its portion because it is valuable property. And it is said: he returns the shell and gets back the entire price. Then this is if he found all defective. If he found some defective and it is little, the sale is valid by istihsan because a little defect is customary and cannot be avoided, like one or two per hundred. So he cannot litigate with the seller because of it. And if the defective is many, the sale is not valid in the whole, and he gets back the entire price according to Abu Hanifa, and according to them (the two companions) it is valid for the portion of the sound part. The meaning of 'many' is what exceeds three in a hundred, not the many that exceeds half. So if he bought a hundred eggs and found three rotten, he has no recourse to anything by agreement. And if he bought ten walnuts and found five hollow, it is said: the sale of the five sound ones is valid by agreement, and he gets back half the price. And it is said: the sale is invalid in the whole according to him (Abu Hanifa) because it combines property and non-property, and that invalidates the contract like combining a free person and a slave. And the sale of the five sound ones is valid according to them (the two companions) for half the price, because the price is divided over the parts, not over the value. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage addresses a special category of items that must be broken to inspect their quality, such as eggs, nuts, and watermelons. The Hanafi ruling depends on whether the item has any remaining value after breaking. If the item is completely worthless (e.g., a rotten egg with no usable shell), the buyer receives a full refund because the item is not considered property (mal). If some value remains (e.g., pulp for food or fodder), the buyer cannot return the broken item but can claim compensation for the defect. An inter-madhhab dispute with al-Shafi'i arises: the Shafi'i school allows the buyer to return the item if he broke only what is necessary for inspection. The Hanafi response is that the seller's implicit permission was for breaking in the buyer's ownership (since the item already belongs to the buyer), not in the seller's ownership, so return is not possible; instead, compensation protects both parties. The passage also discusses the quantity of defective items in a batch: a few (1-2 per hundred) are overlooked by istihsan; many (more than 3 per hundred) invalidate the entire sale according to Abu Hanifa, while the two companions allow the contract to stand for the sound portion proportionally.

Key Terms
المال (al-mal): Property that has value and can be benefited from; a rotten egg with no usable shell is not mal.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference applied to overlook a small number of defects (1-2 per hundred) in a batch.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a Hanafi buyer breaks an egg and finds it completely rotten with no usable shell, what is his recourse?
A) He can return the broken egg to the seller.
B) He can claim compensation for the defect.
C) He can get a full refund because it is not property.
D) He can get no compensation.

Key Takeaway
A buyer who breaks an item to inspect cannot return it, but gets compensation (if valuable) or full refund (if worthless).

Quantity of Defective Items: Few vs Many and the Role of Istihsan
Intra-Hanafi dispute on quantity of defectives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a few defectives (1-2 per hundred) are overlooked by istihsan, while many defectives (more than 3 per hundred) trigger either invalidation of the entire sale (Abu Hanifa) or partial validity (Sahibayn).

Core Idea
Few defectives are ignored by istihsan; many defectives cause the sale to be void in whole (Abu Hanifa) or valid for the sound portion (Sahibayn).

This lesson extends the previous discussion to cases where only a portion of the items in a batch are defective. The Hanafi school uses istihsan to overlook a small number (1-2 per hundred) because such defects are customary and unavoidable. For larger numbers, the school splits: Imam Abu Hanifa voids the entire sale, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) uphold the sale for the sound items proportionally.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantity threshold for 'few' vs 'many'
Few defectives are defined as 1-2 per hundred. Many defectives are more than 3 per hundred. The Hanafi definition differs from the common understanding of 'many' as more than half.
The threshold is per hundred, not absolute number; 4 out of 100 is many, but 5 out of 200 is still few (2.5 per hundred).
2
Apply istihsan for few defectives
If the defective items are few, the sale is valid by istihsan (juristic preference) and the buyer has no right to litigate or claim compensation—the defect is overlooked.
Istihsan is used because it is impossible to avoid a small number of defects in such items.
3
Apply the rule for many defectives according to Abu Hanifa
If the defective items are many (more than 3 per hundred), Abu Hanifa holds that the entire sale is invalid (batil). The buyer gets back the full price.
Reason: combining property (sound items) and non-property (worthless defective items) in one sale invalidates the contract, similar to combining a free person and a slave.
4
Apply the rule for many defectives according to the Sahibayn
The two companions view that the sale is valid for the sound portion proportionally. The buyer keeps the sound items and gets a refund for the defective ones, based on the number of items, not value.
The price is divided over the parts (number of items), not over the value of each.
5
Work through an example
Example: 10 walnuts bought, 5 found hollow. Agreement: sale valid for 5 sound ones, buyer gets half the price back. Under Abu Hanifa, the whole sale might be invalid if the defective proportion is many (5/10 is 50%, which is >3%, so many; but note: the definition of many is >3 per hundred, which is 3%, so 50% is clearly many). However, there is a disagreement on whether this example falls under the many rule or an exception. The text presents two opinions: one says valid for the 5 by agreement, another says Abu Hanifa invalidates the whole.
This example shows the intra-Hanafi difference in application.

Source Text

According to the Hanafi school, what is the threshold for 'many' defectives in a batch of 100?

Court-Ordered vs Mutual Consent Return in a Chain of Sales
Ruling on chain returns distinguishing court order from mutual consent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a court-ordered return by a sub-buyer allows the original buyer to return to his seller, while a mutual consent return generally does not, and identify the exception for non-recurring defects.

Core Idea
Court-ordered return rescinds the contract from the start; mutual consent return is a new sale that blocks chain return.

When a defective item is resold and the second buyer discovers the defect, the manner in which he returns it determines whether the original buyer can in turn return it to the first seller. This distinction, rooted in the Hanafi classification of returns, is crucial for fair trade. In this lesson we explore the core rule and its exception for defects that could not have arisen after the first sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the key distinction
Restate the chain: Seller A sells to Buyer B; B sells to C; C returns the item to B due to a defect. Now B wishes to return to A. The ruling hinges on whether C's return was by court order or mutual consent.
The text uses 'بقضاء' (by court order) and 'برضاء' (by mutual consent).
2
Understand the legal nature of court-ordered return
Court-ordered return is classified as faskh (rescission) from the origin. This means the contract between B and C is treated as if it never existed, so B never sold the item and can return it to A.
The text: 'لأن الرد بالقضاء فسخ من الأصل'.
3
Understand mutual consent return
Mutual consent return is generally considered a new sale (bay' jadid), not a rescission. Therefore B cannot pass the return to A because B's sale to C was valid and complete; C's return is a separate transaction.
Exception: if the defect is non-recurring (e.g., extra finger), there is a view that B can still return to A due to certainty that the defect existed at the time of the first sale.
4
Apply the exception for non-recurring defects
The text discusses a difference of opinion: some say for defects that do not normally arise after the sale (like an extra finger), the first seller is certain to have known, so B may return even without court order. The correct view (al-asahh) is that B may not return in any case of mutual consent.
This shows the tension between certainty of pre-existing defect and the principle that mutual consent return is a new sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعيب: قيده بـ "عيب"، فلو رد عليه بخيار رؤية أو شرط رده على بائعه ولو بغير قضاء، وهو محمول على ما لا علم للبائع بالعيب وقت البيع، فلو كان بعلمه فرد عليه لا يرده مطلقاً وإن رد عليه بالقضاء؛ لأن إقدامه على البيع بعد العلم به دلالة الرضاء. بقضاء متعلق بقوله: "ردّ" بعد ما تعلق به قوله: "بعيب"، ويحتمل أن يكون الباء من "بعيب" للسببية ومن "بقضاء" على الملابسة أي لو باع المشتري المبيع الذي اشتراه فرد المشتري الثاني المبيع على المشتري الأول بسبب عيب بقضاء بأن أنكر كون العيب عنده، فأثبته المشتري بالبينة أو لم يقم المشتري البينة وحلف القاضي البائع فأبى اليمين، أو المشتري الأول المبيع على بائعه؛ لأنه فسخ إلا إذا حدث به عيب آخر عنده، فيرجع بالنقصان، فإذا كان هذا الرد فسخاً في حق الكل فيكون كأنه لم يبعه. (فتح) على بائعه: لأن الرد بالقضاء فسخ من الأصل. (عيني) ولو برضاء لا: أي لو كان الرد على المشتري الأول بالتراضي لا بقضاء القاضي لا يرده على بائعه، وقيل: هو محمول فيما يحدث مثله كالمرض، وأما في عيب لا يحدث مثله كالإصبع الزائدة فيرده للتيقن به عند البائع الأول ولو لم يكن بقضاء القاضي، والأصح أنه لا يرد عليه في الكل، وهذا إذا كان الرد بعد القبض، وإن كان قبله فله أن يرد على بائعه، وإن كان بالتراضي في غير العقار؛ لأن بيع غير العقار قبل القبض لا يجوز، فلا يمكن جعله بيعاً جديداً في حق غيرهما، فيجعل فسخاً في حق الكل، والأظهر في العقار عند أبي حنيفة أنه بيع جديد في حق البائع الأول، وعند محمد فسخ وعند أبي يوسف بيع في حق الكل، ولا فرق بين أن يكون قضاء القاضي ببينة أو بإقرار أو بنكول. [ رمز الحقائق: ٢٦/٢] (فتح)
By defect: He restricted it with 'defect', so if he returns it by option of sight or condition, he returns it to his seller even without a court order, and this is interpreted as when the seller had no knowledge of the defect at the time of sale. If he knew, he does not return it at all even if by court order, because his proceeding with the sale after knowledge indicates consent. 'By court order' is attached to his (the author's) saying 'returns' after attaching 'by defect' to it. It is also possible that the ba in 'by defect' is for causation and the ba in 'by court order' for accompaniment — i.e., if the buyer sold the purchased item and the second buyer returned it to the first buyer due to a defect by a court order, as when the seller denied the defect and the buyer proved it by evidence or the seller refused to take an oath, then the first buyer returns it to the seller because it is a rescission (faskh), unless a new defect occurred at the buyer, in which case he claims compensation. So if this return is a faskh with respect to all, it is as if he did not sell it. (Fath) To his seller: because the return by court order is a rescission from the origin. (Ayni) And if by mutual consent, no: i.e., if the return to the first buyer was by mutual consent not by court order, he does not return it to his seller. And it is said: it is interpreted in defects that occur anew like illness; as for a defect that does not occur anew like an extra finger, he returns it because of certainty that the original seller knew, even if not by court order. The correct view is that he does not return it in all cases. This is if the return was after possession. If it was before possession, he can return it to his seller even if by mutual consent in non-real estate, because the sale of non-real estate before possession is not permissible, so it cannot be considered a new sale regarding others, so it is made a faskh for all. The more apparent in real estate according to Abu Hanifa is that it is a new sale regarding the first seller; according to Muhammad it is a faskh; according to Abu Yusuf it is a sale regarding all. And there is no difference whether the court's order is based on evidence, confession, or refusal to take an oath. [Ramz al-Haqaiq 26/2] (Fath)
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage discusses a chain of returns: a buyer purchases a defective item, then sells it to another person, who later returns it due to a defect. The key issue is whether the first buyer can then return the item to the original seller. The ruling distinguishes based on the manner of return. If the second buyer returns the item by a court order (qada'), the first buyer can in turn return it to the original seller because the court-ordered return is considered a rescission (faskh) from the outset, as if the first sale had not occurred. However, if the second buyer returns the item by mutual consent (rida'), the first buyer generally cannot return it to his seller, because mutual consent return is considered a new sale, not a rescission. There are nuances: if the defect is of a type that could have occurred after the first sale, the first buyer cannot return; but if the defect is one that could not have arisen later (e.g., an extra finger), there is a view that he can still return. The text also discusses the case when the return occurs before the first buyer took possession of the item from the original seller.

Key Terms
الفسخ (al-faskh): Rescission of a contract, treating it as if it never existed; court-ordered return is considered faskh.
القضاء (al-qada'): A court order or judicial decree; contrasted with mutual consent (rida').
التراضي (al-taradi): Mutual consent between the parties, without court involvement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer sells a defective item to another person, and the second buyer returns it by mutual consent. Can the first buyer return it to the original seller?
A) Yes, because the item is defective.
B) No, because mutual consent return is considered a new sale.
C) Yes, but only if the defect existed before the first sale.
D) No, unless the original seller agrees.

Key Takeaway
Court-ordered return rescinds the contract from the origin, allowing chain return; mutual consent return is a new sale that blocks it, even for defects that certainly pre-existed.

Pre-Possession Returns and Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on Real Estate
Ruling on pre-possession returns and real estate ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule for pre-possession mutual consent returns in non-real estate and state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on real estate returns.

Core Idea
Before possession, mutual consent return is faskh for movables; for real estate, there is a major intra-Hanafi difference.

The ruling on chain returns changes when the return occurs before the first buyer took possession of the item from the original seller. Additionally, when the item is real estate, the Hanafi masters disagree on whether a mutual consent return is a new sale or a rescission. This lesson clarifies these important nuances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pre-possession scenario
The text states that if the return takes place before the first buyer (B) took possession from the original seller (A), then B can return to A even if C's return was by mutual consent, but only for non-real estate.
Reason: selling something before receiving possession is prohibited for non-real estate, so the contract between B and C is invalid; thus C's return is necessarily a rescission.
2
Understand the real estate exception
For real estate, the rule differs because sale before possession is allowed. There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa says mutual consent return is a new sale regarding the first seller; Muhammad says it is a faskh; Abu Yusuf says it is a sale regarding all (everyone).
The text says 'الأظهر في العقار عند أبي حنيفة أنه بيع جديد' (The more apparent in real estate according to Abu Hanifa is that it is a new sale).
3
Analyze the practical effect
Under Abu Hanifa's view, the chain return is blocked; under Muhammad's, it is allowed; under Abu Yusuf's, it is also blocked because it is a sale regarding all. The mufta bihi (operative position) is not explicitly stated, but Abu Hanifa's is labeled 'azhar' (more apparent).
This shows the complexity and the need to know the differences within the school.
4
Note the uniform ruling on court orders
The text concludes that in all cases, there is no difference whether the court order is based on evidence (bayyinah), confession (iqrar), or refusal to take an oath (nukul). This underscores the binding nature of a judicial decree.
The commentary (Ayni) emphasizes that the court order is always faskh regardless of the evidence.

البيع الجديد (al-bay' al-jadid): A new sale, distinct from rescission.

Checkpoint
According to the more apparent view (al-azhar) in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a mutual consent return of real estate (post-possession) regarding the first seller?
A) The return is a faskh, so the first buyer can return to his seller.
B) The return is a new sale only with respect to the first seller, so the first buyer cannot return.
C) The return is a new sale with respect to all, so the first buyer cannot return.
D) The return is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Before possession, mutual consent return of non-real estate allows chain return; for real estate, the more apparent view is that it blocks chain return due to the validity of the original sale.

Buyer's Claim of Defect: Non-Compulsion to Pay and Duty to Prove
Ruling on buyer's claim of defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a buyer's claim of defect and explain the evidentiary requirement.

Core Idea
A buyer claiming a defect is not forced to pay, but must prove the defect existed at the time of taking possession.

In the Hanafi school, when a buyer takes possession of a purchased item and later claims it has a defect, the law steps in to balance fairness. The buyer is not immediately compelled to pay the price—this protects a potentially truthful claimant. However, the buyer does not get a free pass; he must present evidence (bayyinah) to substantiate the defect. This lesson unpacks the rationale and procedural steps from the classical text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
Determine what legal action is being discussed and what the ruling states. Here, the act is the buyer's claim of a defect after possession. The ruling: the buyer is not forced to pay the price.
The ruling protects the buyer from immediate compulsion.
2
Understand the Rationale (Ta'lil)
Why is the buyer not forced? Because there is a possibility (ihtimal) that the buyer is truthful. The law gives the benefit of the doubt.
This is a procedural protection, not a judgment of truthfulness.
3
Identify the Buyer's Obligation
Despite not being forced to pay, the buyer must produce evidence (bayyinah) to prove the defect. Without evidence, the buyer cannot avoid payment permanently.
Bayyinah in Hanafi fiqh typically means two male witnesses or one male and two females.
4
Specify the Timing of the Defect
The buyer must prove that the defect existed at the time he took possession (qabd). If the defect existed only with the seller earlier but disappeared at possession, the buyer cannot return the item.
Possession is a critical point in sales liability.
5
Apply to a Real Scenario
Consider a buyer who purchases a car and later claims the engine is defective. He may refuse to pay immediately but must provide a mechanic's report or expert testimony to prove the defect existed at delivery.
If he fails to prove, he must pay.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم يجبر على الخ: أي لو قبض المشتري المبيع وادعى عيبا فيه لم يجبر المشتري على أداء الثمن للبائع لاحتمال أن يكون صادقاً في دعواه، ولكن يقيم البينة لإثبات العيب بأن المشتري وجد بالمبيع عيبا عند نفسه؛ لأنه إذا لم يوجد العيب عند المشتري ليس له أن يرده بالعيب وإن كان عند البائع؛ لاحتمال أنه زال، فإذا أنه وجد عند نفسه = برهن
He is not forced etc.: i.e., if the buyer took possession of the sold item and claimed a defect in it, the buyer is not forced to pay the price to the seller, because of the possibility that he is truthful in his claim. But he must produce evidence to prove the defect that the buyer found in the item according to himself; because if the defect does not exist with the buyer, he cannot return it by defect even if it existed with the seller, because of the possibility that it disappeared. So if he finds it with himself, he proves.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage addresses a procedural point in sales law. When a buyer claims that the purchased item has a defect after taking possession, the buyer cannot be compelled to pay the price immediately. The law gives the buyer the benefit of the doubt that he might be truthful. However, the buyer must provide evidence to establish the defect. The text notes that the buyer must prove the defect exists at his end (when he took possession), because if the defect was present only with the seller and later disappeared, the buyer cannot return it. The passage seems incomplete (ending abruptly with 'proves' or 'he presents evidence').

Key Terms
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence or proof required from the buyer to establish the defect.
القبض (al-qabd): Taking physical possession of the purchased item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this lesson, what must a buyer do after claiming a defect in a purchased item?
A) Immediately pay the price and then file a claim.
B) Return the item to the seller without payment.
C) Produce evidence (bayyinah) to prove the defect.
D) Obtain a written statement from the seller.

Key Takeaway
Buyer's claim of defect does not force immediate payment, but the buyer bears the burden of proof.

Ruling on Returning a Defective Item After Resale
Ruling with commentary on return after resale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the difference between a court-ordered return and a mutual consent return in terms of the first buyer's right to return to his seller.

Core Idea
A court-ordered return is a rescission; a mutual consent return is a new sale.

This lesson examines a key ruling in the Chapter of the Option of Defect (Khiyar al-'Ayb). When a buyer resells a defective item and the second buyer returns it, the legal nature of that return determines whether the first buyer can pass it back to his original seller. Understanding this distinction is crucial for preserving rights in a chain of sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two return scenarios
Read the main sentence: 'ولو باع المبيع فرد عليه بعيب بقضاء ردّه على بائعه، ولو برضاء لا'. Distinguish between court-ordered (qada) and mutual consent (rida) returns.
2
Understand the legal nature
A court-ordered return is a rescission (faskh) from the origin, treating the resale as if it never happened. A mutual consent return is a new sale (bay' jadid) between the first buyer and second buyer.
3
Explore the intra-Hanafi dispute
For real estate, Abu Hanifa holds that mutual consent return is a new sale vis-à-vis the first seller; Muhammad considers it a rescission; Abu Yusuf considers it a sale for all parties.
The mufta bihi is that the first buyer cannot return to his seller in any case of mutual consent.
4
Apply the ruling
In practice, if you resell an item and the buyer returns it by mutual agreement, you cannot return it to your original seller. If a court orders the return, you can.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب خيار العيب
كتاب البيوع ولو باع المبيع فرد عليه بعيب بقضاء ردّه على بائعه، ولو برضاء لا،
فيه
المشتري
وعندهما يجوز بحصة الصحيح منه والمراد بالكثير ما زاد على الثلاثة في قدر المائة، لا الكثير الذي هو زائد على النصف حتى لو اشترى مائة بيضة، فوجد فيها ثلاثة مذرة لا يكون له أن يرجع بشيء ا اتفاقاً، وأما إذا اشترى عشرة من الجوز، فوجد فيها خمسة خاوية، قيل: يجوز البيع في الخمسة الصحيحة بالاتفاق، ويرجع بنصف الثمن، وقيل: يفسد البيع في الكل عنده؛ لأنه جمع بين المال ،وغيره وذلك مفسد للعقد كالجمع بين الحر والقن، وصح
البيع في الخمسة الصحيحة عندهما بنصف الثمن؛ لأن الثمن ينقسم على الأجزاء لا على القيمة. (عيني، فتح) بعيب: قيده بـ "عيب"، فلو رد عليه بخيار رؤية أو شرط رده على بائعه ولو بغير قضاء، وهو محمول على ما لا علم للبائع بالعيب وقت البيع، فلو كان بعلمه فرد عليه لا يرده مطلقاً وإن رد عليه بالقضاء؛ لأن إقدامه على البيع بعد العلم به دلالة الرضاء.
بقضاء متعلق بقوله: "ردّ" بعد ما تعلق به قوله: "بعيب"، ويحتمل أن يكون الباء من "بعيب" للسببية ومن "بقضاء" على الملابسة أي لو باع المشتري المبيع الذي اشتراه فرد المشتري الثاني المبيع على المشتري الأول بسبب عيب بقضاء بأن أنكر كون العيب عنده، فأثبته المشتري بالبينة أو لم يقم المشتري البينة وحلف القاضي البائع فأبى اليمين، أو المشتري الأول المبيع على بائعه؛ لأنه فسخ إلا إذا حدث به عيب آخر عنده، فيرجع بالنقصان، فإذا كان هذا الرد فسخاً في حق الكل فيكون كأنه لم يبعه. (فتح)
على بائعه: لأن الرد بالقضاء فسخ من الأصل. (عيني) ولو برضاء لا: أي لو كان الرد على المشتري الأول بالتراضي لا بقضاء القاضي لا يرده على بائعه، وقيل: هو محمول فيما يحدث مثله كالمرض، وأما في عيب لا يحدث مثله كالإصبع الزائدة فيرده للتيقن به عند البائع الأول ولو لم يكن بقضاء القاضي، والأصح أنه لا يرد عليه في الكل، وهذا إذا كان الرد بعد القبض، وإن كان قبله فله أن يرد على بائعه، وإن كان بالتراضي في غير العقار؛ لأن بيع غير العقار قبل القبض لا يجوز، فلا يمكن جعله بيعاً جديداً في حق غيرهما، فيجعل فسخاً في حق الكل، والأظهر في العقار عند أبي حنيفة أنه بيع جديد في حق البائع الأول، وعند محمد فسخ وعند أبي يوسف بيع في حق الكل، ولا فرق بين أن يكون قضاء القاضي ببينة أو بإقرار أو بنكول. [ رمز الحقائق: ٢٦/٢] (فتح)
Chapter of the Option of Defect
Book of Sales: And if he sold the merchandise and it was returned to him due to a defect by court order, he returns it to his seller; and if by mutual consent, no.
In it: the buyer
And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is permissible with the portion of the sound one from it, and what is meant by 'much' is that which exceeds three in the amount of one hundred, not the 'much' which is more than half; so if he bought one hundred eggs and found three rotten, he has no right to demand anything back by agreement. But if he bought ten walnuts and found five empty, it is said: the sale is valid for the five sound ones by agreement, and he gets back half the price; and it is said: the sale is invalid in all according to him (Abu Hanifa) because it combines property and other than it, and that corrupts the contract like combining a free person and a slave. And the sale of the five sound ones is valid according to them for half the price, because the price is divided on parts, not on value. (Ayni, Fath) The phrase 'due to a defect': He restricted it with 'defect'; so if it is returned to him under the option of seeing or a condition, he returns it to his seller even without a court order, and it is interpreted as where the seller had no knowledge of the defect at the time of sale; if he had knowledge and it was returned to him, he does not return it at all even if returned by court order, because his proceeding with the sale after knowledge is an indication of satisfaction. 'By court order' is attached to his statement 'returned' after his statement 'due to a defect' was attached; and it is possible that the preposition in 'due to a defect' is for causation and in 'by court order' for accompaniment, i.e., if the buyer sold the merchandise he bought, and the second buyer returned the merchandise to the first buyer due to a defect by court order, such that the seller denied the defect being with him, so the buyer proved it by evidence, or the buyer did not provide evidence and the judge swore the seller and he refused the oath, then the first buyer [returns] the merchandise to his seller, because it is a rescission, unless another defect arose while it was with him, in which case he claims a reduction. So if this return is a rescission with respect to all, then it is as if he never sold it. (Fath) 'To his seller': because the return by court order is a rescission from the origin. (Ayni) 'And if by mutual consent, no': meaning if the return to the first buyer was by mutual consent not by judge's ruling, he does not return it to his seller. And it is said: it is interpreted in defects that are likely to occur like illness; but in a defect that is not likely to occur like an extra finger, he returns it because of certainty that it was with the first seller even if not by judge's ruling; and the soundest view is that he does not return it in all cases. And this is if the return is after taking possession; if it was before, he can return it to his seller even if by mutual consent in non-real estate, because the sale of non-real estate before possession is not valid, so it cannot be made a new sale with respect to others, so it is made a rescission in all respects. And the more apparent view in real estate according to Abu Hanifa is that it is a new sale with respect to the first seller; according to Muhammad it is a rescission; and according to Abu Yusuf it is a sale with respect to all. And there is no difference between the judge's ruling based on evidence, confession, or refusal to swear. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 26/2] (Fath)
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with commentary from the chapter on the Option of Defect (Khiyar al-'Ayb). The main ruling states: If a buyer (first buyer) purchases an item and then resells it, and the second buyer returns the item to the first buyer due to a defect by court order (qada), then the first buyer may return the item to his original seller (the first seller). However, if the return is by mutual consent (rida) between the first and second buyer, the first buyer cannot return it to his seller. The commentary explains the reasoning: A court-ordered return is a rescission (faskh) of the contract from the beginning, so it is as if the first buyer never sold it, allowing him to return it to his seller. A mutual consent return is a new sale (bay' jadid) from the first buyer to the second, so the first buyer cannot pass it back to his seller. The commentary also discusses a detailed case: if the item is bought in bulk (e.g., 100 eggs or 10 walnuts) and some are defective, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), if the defective portion is not 'much' (defined as more than 3 out of 100), the sale is valid for the sound items and the buyer gets a proportional refund. Abu Hanifa holds that if the transaction combines sound and defective items (like free and slave), the entire contract is invalid. The passage further explains that the ruling depends on whether the defect was known to the seller at the time of sale, and whether the return happened before or after taking possession. It also notes that for real estate, there is a dispute among the three imams: Abu Hanifa considers a mutual consent return as a new sale regarding the first seller; Muhammad as a rescission; Abu Yusuf as a sale for all parties. The relied-upon view (al-asahh) is that the first buyer cannot return the item to his seller in all cases of mutual consent.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The legal right of the buyer to return a purchased item due to a pre-existing defect.
رد (radd): Return of the defective item by the buyer to the seller.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of the contract, treating it as void from the beginning.
بيع جديد (bay' jadid): A new sale, meaning the original sale is not undone but a new transaction occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a second buyer returns a defective item to the first buyer by mutual consent, what is the ruling for the first buyer?
A) He may return the item to his original seller.
B) He may not return the item to his original seller.
C) He may return it only if the defect existed at the time of his purchase.
D) The sale is void.

Key Takeaway
The nature of a return (court-ordered vs mutual consent) determines whether it is a rescission or a new sale, affecting the first buyer's right to return to his seller.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
10 min
Bulk Purchase with Mixed Defective Items: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on bulk purchase with defective items and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on bulk purchases with defective items and apply the ruling according to the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
If defective portion is not 'much,' sale is valid for sound items with refund; otherwise, dispute.

This lesson zooms into a specific case within the Option of Defect: buying a bulk quantity with some defective units. The text discusses the definition of 'much' and a dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn. Understanding this helps in commercial transactions where goods are sold in lots.

Guided Path
1
Identify the bulk purchase scenario
The text gives two examples: 100 eggs with 3 rotten, and 10 walnuts with 5 empty. Recognize that these are bulk purchases with mixed quality.
2
Define 'much' (kathir)
According to the text, 'much' is defined as more than 3 units per 100. For 100 eggs, 3 is not much; for 10 walnuts, 5 is much (since 5 > 3).
Note: 'much' here is not more than half, but excess over 3 in a hundred.
3
Understand the agreement and dispute
If the defective portion is not 'much' (e.g., 3/100), there is agreement that the sale is valid for sound items with a proportional refund. If it is 'much' (e.g., 5/10), Abu Hanifa invalidates the entire contract, while the Sahibayn validate the sound portion with refund.
4
Analyze the reasoning
Abu Hanifa: Combining mal (property) and ghair mal (non-property) corrupts the contract, similar to mixing a free person and a slave. Sahibayn: The price is divisible into parts, so the contract can be partially valid.

Source Text

وعندهما يجوز بحصة الصحيح منه والمراد بالكثير ما زاد على الثلاثة في قدر المائة، لا الكثير الذي هو زائد على النصف حتى لو اشترى مائة بيضة، فوجد فيها ثلاثة مذرة لا يكون له أن يرجع بشيء ا اتفاقاً، وأما إذا اشترى عشرة من الجوز، فوجد فيها خمسة خاوية، قيل: يجوز البيع في الخمسة الصحيحة بالاتفاق، ويرجع بنصف الثمن، وقيل: يفسد البيع في الكل عنده؛ لأنه جمع بين المال ،وغيره وذلك مفسد للعقد كالجمع بين الحر والقن، وصح

Buyer's Claim of Defect: Proof and Oath Procedure
Ruling statement with expansion on procedural details and dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the procedure when a buyer claims a defect after taking possession, identify when the buyer must provide proof and when the seller must take an oath, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the seller's oath.

Core Idea
A buyer claiming a defect must prove it; the seller swears only after the defect is established at the buyer.

This lesson covers the detailed rules when a buyer claims a defect in a purchased item after taking possession. The key principle is that the buyer is not forced to pay immediately, but must provide evidence (bayyinah) to support his claim. The passage also explains the oath procedure, including a dispute within the Hanafi school on when the seller must be sworn, and special rules for absentee witnesses and claims of absconding (ibaq).

Guided Path
1
Identify the buyer's right to withhold payment
The passage first states that the buyer is not compelled to pay the price if he claims a defect, because he might be truthful. This is a procedural safeguard.
This right is provisional; the buyer must eventually prove the defect or the seller's oath will compel payment.
2
Understand the requirement of proof (bayyinah)
The buyer must provide evidence (bayyinah) to establish the defect's existence at the present time (with himself). If he cannot, he loses the right to return the item. He also needs to prove the defect existed at the seller's time.
Bayyinah usually means two male witnesses or one male and two females.
3
Trace the oath procedure if no proof is available
If the buyer has no evidence, the seller is asked to take an oath. If the seller swears, he is free; if he refuses (nukul), the defect is established. A second oath may be required regarding the defect's presence during the seller's ownership.
Nukul (refusal to swear) is considered proof in most cases except hudud.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on the second oath
According to Abu Hanifa, the seller is not sworn on the second point until the buyer proves the defect's existence at his end. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the seller should be sworn regardless. The passage adopts Abu Hanifa's view as the soundest.
The dispute hinges on when the buyer becomes a litigant (khasm) – only after the defect is established at his end.
5
Apply special rules: absentee witnesses and absconding claim
If the buyer's witnesses are far away, the judge grants a three-day postponement but then orders the seller to swear and the buyer to pay. For a claim of absconding (ibaq), the buyer must first prove the slave absconded while with him before the seller is sworn.
Some defects like fornication of a slave-girl do not require the buyer to prove existence at his end.

Source Text

لم يجبر على الخ: أي لو قبض المشتري المبيع وادعى عيبا فيه لم يجبر المشتري على أداء الثمن للبائع لاحتمال أن يكون صادقاً في دعواه، ولكن يقيم البينة لإثبات العيب بأن المشتري وجد بالمبيع عيبا عند نفسه؛ لأنه إذا لم يوجد العيب عند المشتري ليس له أن يرده بالعيب وإن كان عند البائع؛ لاحتمال أنه زال، فإذا أنه وجد عند نفسه = برهن

يحتاج إلى أن يبرهن أيضاً أن هذا العيب كان بالمبيع عند البائع لاحتمال أنه حدث عند المشتري، أما إذا أقام المشتري البينة على أن العيب قائم عنده يحلف البائع اتفاقاً، فلا يستحق عليه الرد، فإذا ثبت أنه كان فيه عند البائع فسخ العقد بينهما؛ لثبوته في الحالين عنده وعند البائع والحاصل أن إثبات العيب عند أحدهما فقط غير كاف للرد. [رمز الحقائق: ۲۷/۲] (فتح) يبرهن أي يقيم المشتري البينة على ما ادعاه (مسكين) أو يحلف بائعه بسكون الحاء وفتحها مع تشديد اللام إذا لم يكن للمشتري بينة على وجود العيب بالمبيع عند البائع، وقيامه في الحال على قولهما؛ لأن البائع لو أقر به لزمه، فإذا أنكره يحلف، فإن حلف برئ، وإن نكل ثبت قيام العيب للحال ثم يحلف ثانياً على أن هذا العيب
لم يكن فيه عند البائع، فإن حلف برئ، وإن نكل فسخ القاضي العقد بينهما، واختلفوا على قول أبي حنيفة. فقيل: يحلف، لقوله عليه: "اليمين على من أنكر وهو منكر عن العيب في الحالين، وقيل: لا يحلف، وهو الأصح؛ لأن الحلف يترتب على دعوى صحيحة، ولا تصح الدعوى إلا من خصم، ولا يصير المشتري خصماً فيه إلا بعد قيام العيب عنده، ولا يلزم من ترتب البينة ترتب اليمين كما في الحدود والأشياء الستة أي النكاح والرجعة والرق وغيرها. [ رمز الحقائق: ۲۷/۲] (فتح) شهودي بالشام إلخ: أراد بالشام مطلق الغيبة، يعني إذا كان شهود المشتري غيبا فقال: أمهلني حتى يحضر شهودي لم يلتفت القاضي إليه إلا إذا طلب المهلة ثلاثة أيام، ولكن يحلف البائع ويأمره بنقد الثمن؛ لأن في الانتظار ضرراً بالبائع، فإن نكل البائع عن اليمين لزم العيب؛ لأن النكول حجة في ثبوت العيب، بخلاف الحدود حيث لا يفيد النكول فيها حجة، ولهذا لم يحلف فيها رمز الحقائق: ۲۷/۲] (فتح) بائعه: لأن في الانتظار ضررا بالبائع وليس فيه كثير ضرر للمشتري؛ لأنه متى أقام البينة رد المبيع وأخذ الثمن. (عيني) إباقا: أي إباق العبد الذي اشتراه وأنكر البائع (عيني) لم يحلف بائعه: أي إذا ادعى المشتري إباقاً بأن جاء بالعبد إلى البائع، وقال: بعتني أبقاً، فأنكر البائع، وأراد المشتري تحليفه، لم يحلف البائع حتى يبرهن المشتري أن العبد أبق عند المشتري، والحاصل أن كل عيب لابد فيه من المعاوضة عند المشتري لابد من وجود إثباته عند المشتري؛ لتقع الخصومة لقدمه وحدوثه كالبول في الفراش والسرقة، وأما ما لا يشترط وجوده عند المشتري كزنا الجارية، فإنه يحلف عليه ابتداء عند عدم البرهان. (فتح، البحر الرائق)
فإن برهن: أي المشتري على أنه أبق عنده حلف بالله الحاكم البائع حينئذ؛ لأن البائع لم ينتصب خصما حتى يثبت المشتري أن العيب وجد في المبيع عند المشتري عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما يحلف ولو لم يثبت كما مر. (عيني)
He is not compelled to etc.: meaning if the buyer took possession of the merchandise and claimed a defect in it, the buyer is not compelled to pay the price to the seller due to the possibility that he is truthful in his claim. But he provides evidence to establish the defect, because the buyer found a defect in the merchandise with himself; because if the defect is not found with the buyer, he has no right to return it due to the defect even if it was with the seller, because of the possibility that it has vanished. So if he finds it with himself, he must prove it.
But he provides proof, or his seller takes an oath. If he says: 'My witnesses are in Syria', he pays it [the price] if his seller takes the oath.
[PAGE_283] But he provides proof, or his seller takes an oath. If he says: 'My witnesses are in Syria', he pays it [the price] if his seller takes an oath. The buyer (the buyer of the price) then if he claims absconding, his seller does not take an oath until the buyer proves that he absconded with him. If he proves, he swears by Allah.
Meaning the buyer, meaning he provides evidence, meaning that the purchased slave needs also to prove that this defect was in the merchandise with the seller, because of the possibility that it occurred with the buyer. But if the buyer provides evidence that the defect is present with him, the seller takes an oath by agreement, so he does not deserve the return. If it is established that it was with the seller, the contract between them is rescinded; because of its establishment in both states – with him and with the seller. The conclusion is that establishing the defect with only one is not sufficient for return. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 27/2] (Fath) 'He provides proof' meaning the buyer provides evidence on what he claims (Miskin) 'or his seller takes an oath' with sukun of the ha and fatha with tashdid of the lam if the buyer has no evidence of the existence of the defect in the merchandise with the seller, and its existence at present according to them; because if the seller admitted it, it would be binding on him. So if he denies, he takes an oath; if he swears, he is free; if he refuses (nukul), then the existence of the defect at present is established. Then he swears a second time that this defect was not with the seller; if he swears, he is free; if he refuses, the judge rescinds the contract between them. And they differed on the saying of Abu Hanifa: it is said he swears, because of his statement (s): 'The oath is upon the denier' and he is a denier of the defect in both states; and it is said he does not swear, and this is the soundest, because the oath depends on a valid claim, and the claim is only valid from a litigant, and the buyer does not become a litigant in it except after the existence of the defect with him, and the establishment of evidence does not necessitate the establishment of the oath, as in hudud and the six things: i.e., marriage, returning to marriage, slavery, and others. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 27/2] (Fath) 'My witnesses are in Syria etc.': By 'Syria' he meant absolute absence. That is, if the buyer's witnesses are absent, and he says: 'Give me time until my witnesses come', the judge does not pay attention to him unless he asks for a three-day postponement. But the seller takes an oath and orders him to pay the price, because delaying harms the seller. If the seller refuses the oath, the defect becomes established because refusal is evidence in proving the defect, unlike hudud where refusal is not evidence. [Ramz al-Haqaiq: 27/2] (Fath) 'His seller': because in waiting is harm to the seller and there is not much harm to the buyer, because when he provides evidence, the merchandise is returned and he takes the price. (Ayni) 'Absconding': i.e., absconding of the slave he bought, and the seller denied. (Ayni) 'His seller does not take an oath': i.e., if the buyer claims absconding by bringing the slave to the seller and saying: 'You sold me an absconder', and the seller denies, and the buyer wants to swear him, the seller does not take an oath until the buyer proves that the slave absconded with the buyer. The conclusion is that every defect must be established at the buyer for the litigation to occur for its existence and occurrence, like bed-wetting and theft. As for what does not require its existence at the buyer, like the fornication of a slave-girl, he swears on it initially in the absence of proof. (Fath, al-Bahr al-Raiq) 'If he proves': i.e., the buyer proves that he absconded with him, then the judge swears the seller at that time, because the seller does not become a litigant until the buyer establishes that the defect existed in the merchandise with the buyer according to Abu Hanifa. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he swears even if he did not establish, as has passed. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the procedure when a buyer claims a defect in a purchased item after taking possession. The main ruling is: The buyer is not compelled to pay the price if he claims a defect, because he might be truthful. However, he must establish the defect through evidence (bayyinah) or through the seller's oath. The passage then explains various scenarios. First, the buyer must prove that the defect exists at the present time (with himself). If he cannot, he cannot return the item. Then, he must also prove that the defect existed at the time of sale with the seller, otherwise the seller can claim it arose after purchase. The procedure for the oath is detailed: if the buyer has no evidence, the seller is asked to swear that he knew of no defect; if he refuses, the defect is established. There is a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the second oath: according to one view, the seller swears twice (that the defect does not exist now and that it did not exist before); the sounder view is that he swears only once, because the buyer does not become a litigant until the defect's existence is established. The passage also addresses special cases: if the buyer's witnesses are far away (absent), the judge gives a three-day postponement but then makes the seller swear and orders payment; if the seller refuses, the defect is proven. Another case is a claim that a slave absconded: the buyer must first prove that the slave absconded while with him; then the seller is sworn. This is because some defects (like running away) require proof of occurrence at the buyer's end to establish litigation. The passage concludes that every defect that involves exchange (like loss) must be established at the buyer for the seller to be a litigant; but defects like fornication of a slave-girl do not require this.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, usually testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females.
يمين (yamin): Oath taken by the party against whom the claim is made.
نكول (nukul): Refusal to take an oath, which is treated as admission of the opponent's claim.
إباق (ibaq): Absconding of a slave from the owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer claims that a purchased slave has absconded, when is the seller required to take an oath?
A) Immediately upon the buyer's claim.
B) After the buyer proves the slave absconded while with him.
C) After the buyer proves the slave absconded while with the seller.
D) After the seller demands the oath.

Key Takeaway
A buyer claiming a defect must prove the defect's existence at the time of claim and also that it existed at the seller's end; the seller's oath is tied to valid litigation.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Proving Defects and the Seller's Oath in Khiyar al-'Ayb
Procedural rules for khiyar al-'ayb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule for proving a defect in khiyar al-'ayb and apply the condition that the buyer must become a litigant before the seller swears, including the intra-Hanafi dispute on ibaq.

Core Idea
The seller only swears after the buyer proves the defect existed with him.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq lays out the procedural rules for khiyar al-'ayb. The buyer must either produce evidence that the defect existed with the seller at the time of sale, or the seller takes an oath denying it. The text highlights a critical principle: the seller's oath is only administered after the buyer establishes a valid claim, which requires proving the defect's existence in his own possession for certain types of defects. We will walk through the general rule, the case of absent witnesses, and the special example of ibaq (running away) where Abu Hanifa differs from his two students.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general procedure
State the two ways a buyer can prove a defect: by producing evidence (bayyinah) that it existed with the seller, or by the seller's oath. Understand that the buyer must prove both the present existence and the pre-existence of the defect.
2
Analyze the absent witnesses scenario
If the buyer's witnesses are absent, he can request a three-day delay; otherwise, the seller swears and pays the price. Note the reason: delay harms the seller, and the buyer can later return the item and reclaim the price if he produces evidence.
3
Examine the ibaq (running away) case
Understand that according to Abu Hanifa, when a buyer claims the slave was a runaway, the seller does not swear until the buyer proves the slave ran away while in his possession. This is because the buyer only becomes a litigant after establishing the defect's occurrence with him.
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ: they make the seller swear even without such proof.
4
Apply the litigant principle
Generalize: the buyer must become a 'khashm' (litigant) for the defect before the seller can be sworn. This is why for defects that do not require existence with the buyer (e.g., fornication of a slave girl), the seller swears immediately.

باب خيار العيب ولكن يبرهن، أو يحلف بائعه، فإن قال شهودي بالشام، دفع إليه إن حلف بائعه، أي المشتري أي المشتري الثمن فإن ادعى إباقاً لم يحلف بائعه حتى يبرهن المشتري أنه أبق عنده، فإن برهن حلف بالله = أي المشتري أي يقيم البينة أي أن العبد المشتري يحتاج إلى أن يبرهن أيضاً أن هذا العيب كان بالمبيع عند البائع لاحتمال أنه حدث عند المشتري، أما إذا أقام المشتري البينة على أن العيب قائم عنده يحلف البائع اتفاقاً، فلا يستحق عليه الرد، فإذا ثبت أنه كان فيه عند البائع فسخ العقد بينهما؛ لثبوته في الحالين عنده وعند البائع والحاصل أن إثبات العيب عند أحدهما فقط غير كاف للرد. [ رمز الحقائق: ۲۷/۲] (فتح) شهودي بالشام إلخ: أراد بالشام مطلق الغيبة، يعني إذا كان شهود المشتري غيبا فقال: أمهلني حتى يحضر شهودي لم يلتفت القاضي إليه إلا إذا طلب المهلة ثلاثة أيام، ولكن يحلف البائع ويأمره بنقد الثمن؛ لأن في الانتظار ضرراً بالبائع، فإن نكل البائع عن اليمين لزم العيب؛ لأن النكول حجة في ثبوت العيب، بخلاف الحدود حيث لا يفيد النكول فيها حجة، ولهذا لم يحلف فيها رمز الحقائق: ۲۷/۲] (فتح) بائعه: لأن في الانتظار ضررا بالبائع وليس فيه كثير ضرر للمشتري؛ لأنه متى أقام البينة رد المبيع وأخذ الثمن.
