(عيني) ما أبق عندك قط ولا يعرف أهي حادثة عند المشتري أم لا، وأما العيوب التي لا يحدث مثلها كالإصبع الزائدة والناقصة فإن القاضي يقضي بالرد من غير تحليف البائع؛ لتيقنه بوجوده عند البائع. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٨/٢] والحاصل أن العيوب أنواع خفي كإباق وعلم حكمه، وظاهر كعور وصمم وإصبع زائدة وناقصة، فيقضي بالرد بلا يمين للتيقن به إذا لم يدّع البائع رضا المشتري به ومالا يعرفه إلا الأطباء كوجع الكبد، فيكفي فيه قول عدل ولإثباته عند بائعه قول عدلين وما لا يعرفه إلا النساء كرتق وعقر فيقبل في قيامه للحال قول امرأة واحدة الثقة، ثم إن كان بعد القبض لا يرده بقولهن بل يحلف البائع وإن كان قبله فكذلك عند محمد، وعند أبي يوسف: يرد بقولهن من غير تحليف البائع (عيني، فتح)
Chapter of the Option of Defect: But he must provide proof, or his seller swears. If he says, 'My witnesses are in Syria,' it is given to him if his seller swears. [Commentary: i.e., the buyer, i.e., the buyer pays the price. If he claims running away, his seller is not made to swear until the buyer proves that the slave ran away while with him. If he proves, the seller swears by Allah. i.e., the buyer i.e., he produces evidence i.e., that the purchased slave also needs to prove that this defect existed with the seller at the time of sale because it is possible that it occurred with the buyer. But if the buyer produces evidence that the defect currently exists, the seller swears by agreement, and the buyer is not entitled to return. If it is established that the defect existed with the seller, the contract is rescinded between them because it is established in both states – with the buyer and with the seller. The gist is that proving the defect with only one of them is insufficient for return. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 27/2) (Fath) He provides proof i.e., the buyer produces evidence for his claim (Miskin) or his seller swears – with sukun on the ha and fatha with tashdid on the lam – if the buyer has no evidence that the defect existed with the seller at the time of sale and that it currently exists according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Because if the seller admitted it, it would be binding on him; if he denies, he swears. If he swears, he is absolved; if he refuses, the current existence of the defect is established, then he swears a second time that this defect did not exist with the seller. If he swears, he is absolved; if he refuses, the judge rescinds the contract between them. There is disagreement on Abu Hanifa's position: some say he swears because of his saying, 'The oath is on the denier' – and he is a denier regarding the defect in both states. Some say he does not swear, and this is the correct view because the oath is based on a valid claim, and a claim is only valid from a litigant, and the buyer only becomes a litigant after the defect is proven with him; and the requirement of evidence does not necessitate the requirement of an oath, as in the case of hadd penalties and the six matters – i.e., marriage, return to marriage, slavery, etc. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 27/2) (Fath) 'My witnesses are in Syria' etc.: By 'Syria' he means absolute absence. That is, if the buyer's witnesses are absent and he says, 'Give me time until my witnesses come,' the judge does not pay attention unless he requests a delay of three days. But the seller swears and is ordered to pay the price because delay harms the seller. If the seller refuses the oath, the defect is established because refusal is a proof for establishing the defect, unlike hadd penalties where refusal is not a proof. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 27/2) (Fath) His seller: because delay harms the seller and does not cause much harm to the buyer, since when he produces evidence, he returns the sold item and takes the price. (Ayni) Running away: i.e., the slave's running away that he bought and the seller denies. (Ayni) His seller does not swear: i.e., if the buyer claims running away by bringing the slave to the seller and saying, 'You sold me a runaway,' and the seller denies, and the buyer wants to make him swear, the seller is not made to swear until the buyer proves that the slave ran away while with the buyer. The gist is that every defect that requires exchange with the buyer must have its establishment with the buyer for the dispute to occur regarding its precedence and occurrence, like wetting the bed and theft. As for what does not require existence with the buyer, like fornication of a slave girl, he is made to swear initially in the absence of proof. (Fath, Bahr al-Raiq) If he proves: i.e., the buyer proves that the slave ran away while with him, then the judge makes the seller swear by Allah. Because the seller does not become a litigant until the buyer proves that the defect existed in the sold item while with the buyer, according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he swears even if not proven, as mentioned. (Ayni) It never ran away from you – and it is not known whether it occurred with the buyer or not. As for defects that do not occur like them, such as an extra or missing finger, the judge rules for return without making the seller swear because he is certain it existed with the seller. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 28/2) The gist is that defects are of types: hidden like running away and the knowledge of its ruling; apparent like being one-eyed, deaf, extra or missing finger – he rules for return without oath due to certainty, if the seller does not claim the buyer's consent. And what only doctors know, like liver pain, the statement of one just person suffices, but for proving it with the seller, the statement of two just persons is needed. And what only women know, like fusion or impotence, the statement of one trustworthy woman is accepted for its current existence. Then if it is after receipt, he does not return based on their statements, but rather the seller swears; if before receipt, it is the same according to Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf, he returns based on their statements without making the seller swear. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the rules for khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) from the Hanafi school. It opens by stating that a buyer who claims a defect must either produce proof (bayyinah) that the defect existed with the seller, or the seller takes an oath denying it. The commentary clarifies that the buyer must not only prove the present existence of the defect but also that it existed at the time of sale with the seller. If the buyer proves the defect's present existence, the seller swears it did not exist at sale. The author then discusses the case where the buyer's witnesses are absent: the seller swears and pays the price, but the buyer may be given a three-day delay. For defects like running away (ibaq), the buyer must first prove the defect occurred in his possession; only then is the seller made to swear, according to Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the seller swears even without such proof. The text then categorizes defects: hidden, apparent, those known only to doctors or women. For apparent defects (e.g., extra finger), the judge returns the item without an oath. For defects known only to women, the statement of one trustworthy woman is accepted for present existence, but after receipt, the seller still swears according to Muhammad; Abu Yusuf allows return without oath. Throughout, the authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah, with intra-Hanafi differences noted. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that the oath is on the seller in most cases, but for obvious defects, no oath is needed.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The buyer's option to return a purchased item due to a defect that existed at the time of sale.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females.
يمين (yamin): An oath taken by the defendant (seller) to deny the claim.
إباق (ibaq): The act of a slave running away.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the correct view (al-asahh) regarding the seller being made to swear in the ibaq case?
A) The seller swears immediately based on the hadith 'al-yaminu 'ala man ankara'.
B) The seller does not swear until the buyer proves the ibaq occurred in his possession.
C) The seller never swears in ibaq cases.
D) The judge decides based on the seller's character.

Key Takeaway
In khiyar al-'ayb, the seller's oath is only administered after the buyer establishes a valid claim by proving the defect's existence in his possession for defects that require it.

Categorization of Defects and Evidence Rules in Khiyar al-'Ayb
Classification of defects and evidence rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify defects into categories (apparent, hidden, doctor-known, women-known) and apply the appropriate evidence and oath rules for each, including the intra-Hanafi dispute on women-known defects after receipt.

Core Idea
The evidentiary burden and oath requirement depend on the type of defect.

This continuation of the khiyar al-'ayb passage categorizes defects and specifies who may provide evidence. Some defects are so obvious that the judge returns the item without an oath; others require expert testimony. A further dispute arises between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on whether the seller must swear after women provide testimony about intimate defects. We will walk through each category and its rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of defects
List the categories: (1) hidden defects like ibaq, (2) apparent defects like being one-eyed or extra finger, (3) defects known only to doctors (e.g., liver pain), and (4) defects known only to women (e.g., fusion or impotence). Note that the category determines the evidence required.
2
Apply the rule for apparent defects
For apparent defects that cannot occur after sale (e.g., missing finger), the judge returns the item without the seller's oath, because it is certain the defect existed with the seller.
Exception: if the seller claims the buyer consented to the defect, then an oath may be needed.
3
Analyze the evidence for doctor-known defects
For defects knowable only to doctors, the testimony of one just male suffices for the current existence, but proving it existed with the seller requires two just males.
4
Examine women-known defects and the dispute
For defects known only to women, one trustworthy woman's statement is accepted for the current existence. After receipt, Muhammad says the seller still swears; Abu Yusuf says the item is returned without the seller's oath.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter of the Option of Defect: But he must provide proof, or his seller swears. If he says, 'My witnesses are in Syria,' it is given to him if his seller swears. [Commentary: i.e., the buyer, i.e., the buyer pays the price. If he claims running away, his seller is not made to swear until the buyer proves that the slave ran away while with him. If he proves, the seller swears by Allah. i.e., the buyer i.e., he produces evidence i.e., that the purchased slave also needs to prove that this defect existed with the seller at the time of sale because it is possible that it occurred with the buyer. But if the buyer produces evidence that the defect currently exists, the seller swears by agreement, and the buyer is not entitled to return. If it is established that the defect existed with the seller, the contract is rescinded between them because it is established in both states – with the buyer and with the seller. The gist is that proving the defect with only one of them is insufficient for return. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 27/2) (Fath) He provides proof i.e., the buyer produces evidence for his claim (Miskin) or his seller swears – with sukun on the ha and fatha with tashdid on the lam – if the buyer has no evidence that the defect existed with the seller at the time of sale and that it currently exists according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Because if the seller admitted it, it would be binding on him; if he denies, he swears. If he swears, he is absolved; if he refuses, the current existence of the defect is established, then he swears a second time that this defect did not exist with the seller. If he swears, he is absolved; if he refuses, the judge rescinds the contract between them. There is disagreement on Abu Hanifa's position: some say he swears because of his saying, 'The oath is on the denier' – and he is a denier regarding the defect in both states. Some say he does not swear, and this is the correct view because the oath is based on a valid claim, and a claim is only valid from a litigant, and the buyer only becomes a litigant after the defect is proven with him; and the requirement of evidence does not necessitate the requirement of an oath, as in the case of hadd penalties and the six matters – i.e., marriage, return to marriage, slavery, etc. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 27/2) (Fath) 'My witnesses are in Syria' etc.: By 'Syria' he means absolute absence. That is, if the buyer's witnesses are absent and he says, 'Give me time until my witnesses come,' the judge does not pay attention unless he requests a delay of three days. But the seller swears and is ordered to pay the price because delay harms the seller. If the seller refuses the oath, the defect is established because refusal is a proof for establishing the defect, unlike hadd penalties where refusal is not a proof. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 27/2) (Fath) His seller: because delay harms the seller and does not cause much harm to the buyer, since when he produces evidence, he returns the sold item and takes the price. (Ayni) Running away: i.e., the slave's running away that he bought and the seller denies. (Ayni) His seller does not swear: i.e., if the buyer claims running away by bringing the slave to the seller and saying, 'You sold me a runaway,' and the seller denies, and the buyer wants to make him swear, the seller is not made to swear until the buyer proves that the slave ran away while with the buyer. The gist is that every defect that requires exchange with the buyer must have its establishment with the buyer for the dispute to occur regarding its precedence and occurrence, like wetting the bed and theft. As for what does not require existence with the buyer, like fornication of a slave girl, he is made to swear initially in the absence of proof. (Fath, Bahr al-Raiq) If he proves: i.e., the buyer proves that the slave ran away while with him, then the judge makes the seller swear by Allah. Because the seller does not become a litigant until the buyer proves that the defect existed in the sold item while with the buyer, according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he swears even if not proven, as mentioned. (Ayni) It never ran away from you – and it is not known whether it occurred with the buyer or not. As for defects that do not occur like them, such as an extra or missing finger, the judge rules for return without making the seller swear because he is certain it existed with the seller. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 28/2) The gist is that defects are of types: hidden like running away and the knowledge of its ruling; apparent like being one-eyed, deaf, extra or missing finger – he rules for return without oath due to certainty, if the seller does not claim the buyer's consent. And what only doctors know, like liver pain, the statement of one just person suffices, but for proving it with the seller, the statement of two just persons is needed. And what only women know, like fusion or impotence, the statement of one trustworthy woman is accepted for its current existence. Then if it is after receipt, he does not return based on their statements, but rather the seller swears; if before receipt, it is the same according to Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf, he returns based on their statements without making the seller swear. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the rules for khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) from the Hanafi school. It opens by stating that a buyer who claims a defect must either produce proof (bayyinah) that the defect existed with the seller, or the seller takes an oath denying it. The commentary clarifies that the buyer must not only prove the present existence of the defect but also that it existed at the time of sale with the seller. If the buyer proves the defect's present existence, the seller swears it did not exist at sale. The author then discusses the case where the buyer's witnesses are absent: the seller swears and pays the price, but the buyer may be given a three-day delay. For defects like running away (ibaq), the buyer must first prove the defect occurred in his possession; only then is the seller made to swear, according to Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the seller swears even without such proof. The text then categorizes defects: hidden, apparent, those known only to doctors or women. For apparent defects (e.g., extra finger), the judge returns the item without an oath. For defects known only to women, the statement of one trustworthy woman is accepted for present existence, but after receipt, the seller still swears according to Muhammad; Abu Yusuf allows return without oath. Throughout, the authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah, with intra-Hanafi differences noted. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that the oath is on the seller in most cases, but for obvious defects, no oath is needed.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The buyer's option to return a purchased item due to a defect that existed at the time of sale.
قسامة النساء (qasamat al-nisa'): Testimony of women, which is generally not accepted alone in financial cases, but is accepted for intimate defects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what evidence is needed to prove the current existence of a doctor-known defect like liver pain?
A) Two just male witnesses.
B) One just male witness.
C) One trustworthy woman.
D) The buyer's own declaration.

Key Takeaway
Defect type dictates the evidence and oath requirements; some defects allow return without any oath, while others require testimony and sometimes the seller's oath.

Ruling on Defect in One of Two Items in a Single Contract
Ruling on defect in one of two items in a single contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two items are bought together in one contract and one is found defective, and explain the disagreement between the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
In a single contract, the buyer cannot return only the defective item – he must return all or keep all.

This lesson addresses a common scenario in sales: a buyer purchases two items together in a single transaction. After receiving them, he discovers a defect in one. What are his options according to the Hanafi school? The text establishes a key principle that protects the integrity of the contract and distinguishes the majority view from that of Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle about disputes in sales
The passage begins by stating that in disputes about quantity or number of items, the word of the receiver (the buyer) is taken, supported by his oath. This sets the context for the specific case.
This principle is from the earlier part of the passage.
2
Define 'safqah wahidah' (single transaction)
The text clarifies that a single transaction (safqah wahidah) means one contract. When multiple items are sold together in one contract, they are treated as a single unit for the purpose of the option of defect.
This is crucial for understanding the ruling.
3
Apply the rule to a defect in one item after receipt
If after receiving both items the buyer discovers a defect in one, he has the option to return both or keep both. He cannot return only the defective one because that would split the contract.
This is the correct view (al-sahih).
4
Contrast with Abu Yusuf's opinion
Imam Abu Yusuf holds that if the defect is in the item already received, the buyer may return only that item, as the contract is considered complete regarding it. The majority rejects this.
The practical difference: under Abu Yusuf's view, the buyer has more flexibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول في قدر المقبوض للقابض أميناً كان أو ضميناً كالغاصب والمودع؛ ولأن البائع يدعي بعض الثمن في ذمته والمشتري ينكره، فالقول قول المنكر مع يمينه حتى إذا رد المشتري جارية بعيب أو عبداً بعد القبض، فقال البائع: كنت بعتك مع المبيع غيره، وقال المشتري: بعتني هذا المبيع وحده فالقول للمشتري، وكذا إذا اتفقا على مقدار المبيع واختلفا في مقدار المقبوض بأن قال المشتري بعد قبض المبيع موزوناً وجدته ناقصاً، فالقول للمشتري. (عيني، فتح) للقابض: فلو اختلفا بعد التقابض في عدد المبيع وهو واحد أم متعدد أو في مقدار المقبوض فالقول للمشتري؛ لأنه قابض. صفقة واحدة، وهي ضرب اليد على اليد في البيع، ثم جعلت عبارة عن العقد أي في عقد واحد. (عيني، مسكين) بأحدهما عيباً: لم يعلم به إلا بعد القبض فهو مخير إن شاء أخذ العبدين جميعا أو ردهما. (ط، عيني) أخذهما أو ردهما: جميعا سواء وجد بغير المقبوض عيبا أو بالآخر، وهو الصحيح وليس له أن يرد المعيب وحده؛ لأن في أخذ السليم تفريقا للصفقة قبل التمام ولو أعتق السليم أو باعه بعد قبضه لزمه الآخر، وعن أبي يوسف: أنه إذا وجد العيب بالمقبوض له أن يرده وحده؛ لأن الصفقة فيه تمت لتناهيها في حق المقبوض، وكذا إذا لم يقبضهما أخذهما أو نقص بيعهما. [رمز الحقائق: ۲۸/۲] (فتح)
And the saying is about the measure of what is received: it is for the receiver, whether he is trustworthy or guarantor like the usurper and the depositary. Because the seller claims some of the price as a debt and the buyer denies it, so the statement is that of the denier with his oath. So if the buyer returns a slave girl due to a defect or a slave after receipt, and the seller says, 'I sold you something else with the sold item,' and the buyer says, 'You sold me only this sold item,' then the statement is the buyer's. Similarly, if they agree on the quantity of the sold item and differ on the quantity received, such as the buyer saying after receiving a weighed sold item, 'I found it deficient,' then the statement is the buyer's. (Ayni, Fath) For the receiver: if they differ after exchange regarding the number of sold items – whether it is one or multiple – or regarding the quantity received, then the statement is the buyer's because he is the receiver. A single transaction (safqah wahidah) is the striking of hand upon hand in sale, then it was made an expression for the contract, i.e., in one contract. (Ayni, Miskin) A defect in one of them: he did not know of it except after receipt, so he has the choice: if he wishes, he takes both slaves together, or returns them both. (T, Ayni) He takes them both or returns them both: together, whether the defect is found in the one not received or in the other, and this is the correct view. He does not have the right to return only the defective one because taking the sound one would constitute splitting the transaction before completion. And if he emancipates the sound one or sells it after receipt, he is bound to keep the other. From Abu Yusuf: if the defect is found in the received one, he has the right to return it alone because the transaction is completed regarding it due to its finality in the right of the received item. Similarly, if he has not received both, he takes them both or reduces their sale price. [Ramz al-Haqaiq 28/2] (Fath)
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute about quantity or number of items in a sale. It begins by stating a general principle: in cases of disagreement between seller and buyer after the transaction, the word of the receiver (the buyer) is taken, supported by his oath. Examples include when the buyer claims he received less than what was agreed, or when the seller claims he sold additional items. The text then moves to the specific case of two items bought in a single contract (صفقة واحدة). If after receipt the buyer discovers a defect in one of them, he has the option to return both or keep both; he cannot return only the defective one, according to the correct view (al-sahih). This is because returning only one would split the contract. An exception is reported from Abu Yusuf: if the defect is in the item already received, the buyer may return that one alone, as the contract is considered complete regarding it. The passage also notes that if the buyer has not yet received both, he may still return both or demand a reduction in price. The authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah, with Abu Yusuf's view being a minority within the school.

Key Terms
صفقة واحدة (safqah wahidah): A single transaction where two or more items are sold together in one contract.
قابض (qabid): The person who has received (taken possession of) the sold item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi view (al-sahih), when a buyer purchases two items in one contract and later discovers a defect in one after receiving both, which of the following is correct?
A) He may return only the defective item.
B) He may return both items or keep both.
C) He must keep both and cannot return anything.
D) He may return the defective item only if it was not yet in his possession.

Key Takeaway
In a single contract for multiple items, the buyer cannot return only the defective item; he must return all or keep all.

Seller's Oath and Classification of Defects
Ruling on seller's oath and categorization of defects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify defects in a sale according to Hanafi fiqh and state when the seller must take an oath.

Core Idea
Hidden defects require seller's oath; apparent defects allow return without oath.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq explains how a judge administers the seller's oath regarding prior hidden defects and categorizes defects by their detectability. Understanding these categories is crucial for determining whether a buyer can return a defective item and whether the seller must swear an oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath procedure
The passage describes how the judge makes the seller swear: 'it did not run away with you at all' covering all past time before delivery. Note that 'qat' indicates complete negation.
The oath is wajib when the defect is hidden and could have occurred after sale.
2
Distinguish apparent vs hidden defects
Apparent defects (e.g., extra finger) could not have occurred after sale, so the judge is certain they existed before. Hidden defects (e.g., running away) require the seller's oath because their timing is uncertain.
Certainty (yaqin) cancels the need for oath.
3
Learn the four types of defects
Defects are classified as: (1) hidden (like ibaq), (2) apparent (like blindness), (3) known only to doctors (like liver pain), (4) known only to women (like ratq and 'aqr). Each has its own evidentiary rules.
For doctor-only defects, one just witness suffices; for women-only, one trustworthy woman suffices for current existence.
4
Note the ruling for minor slave's running away
If a minor slave ran away, that defect is not permanent; it ceases at puberty. So if the claim is about a minor, the seller swears only since puberty.
This shows how the oath is tailored to the specific defect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما أبق عندك قطّ والقول في قدر المقبوض للقابض، ولو اشترى عبدين صفقة وقبض أحدهما . شخص ووجد بأحدهما عيباً أخذهما أو ردّهما، ولو قبضهما . أي أحد العبدين ما أبق عندك أي كيفية تحليف الحاكم البائع أن يقول له: أن يحلف بالله ما أبق عندك قط، و"قط" لعموم السلب في الماضي يعني ما أبق قبل البيع ولا بعده قبل التسليم أي ما أبق عندك أيها البائع، وإن كان من حق الحالف أن يقول: بالله ما أبق عندي قط، والأحوط أن يحلف بالله ما أبق قط، أو بالله ما يستحق عليك الرد من الوجه الذي ذكره المشتري أو بالله لقد باع سلمه إليه وما به هذا العيب قط. ولو كان الدعوى في إباق العبد الكبير يحلف بالله ما أبق منذ بلغ مبلغ الرجال؛ لأن الإباق في الصغير يزول بالبلوغ، فلا يجب الرد، هذا في العيوب التي لا تظهر للقاضي، ولا يعرف أهي حادثة عند المشتري أم لا، وأما العيوب التي لا يحدث مثلها كالإصبع الزائدة والناقصة فإن القاضي يقضي بالرد من غير تحليف البائع؛ لتيقنه بوجوده عند البائع. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٨/٢] والحاصل أن العيوب أنواع خفي كإباق وعلم حكمه، وظاهر كعور وصمم وإصبع زائدة وناقصة، فيقضي بالرد بلا يمين للتيقن به إذا لم يدّع البائع رضا المشتري به ومالا يعرفه إلا الأطباء كوجع الكبد، فيكفي فيه قول عدل ولإثباته عند بائعه قول عدلين وما لا يعرفه إلا النساء كرتق وعقر فيقبل في قيامه للحال قول امرأة واحدة الثقة، ثم إن كان بعد القبض لا يرده بقولهن بل يحلف البائع وإن كان قبله فكذلك عند محمد، وعند أبي يوسف: يرد بقولهن من غير تحليف البائع (عيني، فتح)
It did not run away with you at all and the statement regarding the amount received is that of the recipient. If someone buys two slaves in one transaction and takes possession of one, and finds a defect in one of them, he may take both or return both. If he takes possession of both... 'It did not run away with you at all' means how the judge makes the seller swear: he says to him: swear by Allah that it did not run away with you at all. 'Qat' is for the generality of negation in the past, meaning it did not run away before the sale nor after before delivery, i.e., it did not run away with you, O seller. Although it is the right of the one swearing to say: by Allah it did not run away with me at all, the more precautionary is to swear: by Allah it did not run away at all, or by Allah you do not deserve the return from the aspect mentioned by the buyer, or by Allah he sold it to him sound and it did not have this defect at all. If the claim is about the running away of an adult slave, he swears: by Allah it did not run away since he reached the age of manhood, because running away in a child ceases with puberty, so return is not obligatory. This applies to defects that do not appear to the judge and he does not know whether they occurred with the buyer or not. As for defects whose like does not occur, such as an extra or missing finger, the judge rules for return without swearing the seller, due to certainty of its presence with the seller. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 28/2] The upshot is that defects are types: hidden, like running away and its ruling knowledge; and apparent, like one-eyed, deaf, extra or missing finger; he rules for return without oath for certainty, if the seller does not claim the buyer's consent. What only doctors know, like liver pain, a single just person's statement suffices for its proof. For what only women know, like adhesion and obstruction of the vagina, for its current existence the statement of one trustworthy woman is accepted. Then if after possession, he does not return by their statements; rather the seller swears. If before possession, it is similar according to Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf: he returns by their statements without swearing the seller. ('Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This is a ruling on the seller's oath regarding prior hidden defects and a classification of defects. The passage begins by explaining how the judge administers the oath to the seller: the seller must swear that the defect did not exist before delivery. The word 'qat' indicates negation covering all past time. The author distinguishes between defects that might have occurred after sale (hidden) and those that could not have occurred (apparent). For hidden defects, the seller must be sworn; for apparent defects, the judge rules for return without oath because certainty exists. Defects are categorized: hidden (e.g., running away), apparent (e.g., missing finger), those known only by doctors (requires one just witness), and those known only by women (e.g., rutaq - adhesion of vagina, qarq - obstruction of vagina). For female defects, a single trustworthy woman's testimony establishes the defect's existence. There is a dispute between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf: if the defect is discovered before possession, Muhammad requires the seller's oath, while Abu Yusuf allows return based on the women's testimony without swearing the seller.

Key Terms
إباق (ibāq): Running away of a slave from the master.
رتق (ratq): Adhesion of the vagina in a female slave.
عقر (ʿaqr): Obstruction or closure of the vagina.
قط (qat): Particle indicating totality of negation in the past.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following defects would require the seller to swear an oath before the buyer can return the item?
A) An extra finger
B) Blindness
C) Running away of a slave
D) Missing finger

Key Takeaway
The seller's oath is only needed for hidden defects; apparent defects are returned without oath due to certainty.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Female Defects Before Possession
Intra-Hanafi dispute on female defects before possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf regarding the return of a slave based on female defect testimony before possession.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf allows return without seller's oath for female defects before possession; Muhammad requires oath.

This passage continues the classification of defects, focusing on defects known only to women. After establishing that one trustworthy woman's testimony proves the defect's existence, the text discusses whether the seller must be sworn before return when the defect is discovered before taking possession. There is a known intra-Hanafi difference between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Recall the rule for defects known only to women
For defects like ratq and 'aqr, a single trustworthy woman's testimony establishes the defect's current existence.
This is a concession because men cannot inspect such defects.
2
Identify the timing condition
The passage distinguishes between after possession (post-qabd) and before possession (pre-qabd). After possession, the seller must be sworn despite the testimony.
Possession transfers risk to buyer.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad holds that even before possession, the seller must be sworn (same as after). Imam Abu Yusuf holds that before possession, the buyer can return based on the women's testimony without swearing the seller.
The source does not state a preferred view; both are transmitted.
4
Understand the basis of the difference
Abu Yusuf likely considers that before possession, the seller still has control and should have known the defect; Muhammad prioritizes the seller's right to deny.
This is a difference in the application of qawl al-munkir (denier's statement) vs. the weakness of the evidence.

The Buyer's Word on Quantity Received
Ruling on whom to believe when buyer and seller dispute the amount received

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that after delivery, the buyer's statement about the amount received is taken with his oath, and apply it to common sale disputes.

Core Idea
After delivery, the buyer is believed over the seller regarding what he received.

In sales transactions, disputes can arise after the goods have been delivered—especially about the quantity or count. The Hanafi school provides a clear evidentiary rule: the buyer's word is accepted, because he is the receiver (qābiḍ) and the seller is making a claim that the buyer denies. This lesson unpacks that principle and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed matter
Determine whether the dispute is about the amount of goods received after possession, not about the price or the original contract terms.
The rule only applies after exchange of possession (taqābuḍ).
2
State the ruling
The buyer's statement is taken with his oath. This is the principle al-qawl li-l-qābiḍ.
3
Understand the reasoning
The seller claims a debt (remaining price) on the buyer; the buyer denies it. The denier's statement is preferred with his oath.
Buyer is munkir (denier), seller is muddaʿī (claimant).
4
Apply to the examples
Examine the two scenarios: (1) returning a defective item where seller claims extra goods sold, and (2) weight deficiency after receipt. In both, the buyer's word is taken.
The text specifically covers 'number of the sold item' and 'quantity of the received item'.
5
Note the scope
The rule applies whether the buyer is a trustee (amīn) or guarantor (ḍamīn), like a borrower or depositee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول في قدر إلخ أميناً كان أو ضميناً كالغاصب والمودع؛ ولأن البائع يدعي بعض الثمن في ذمته والمشتري ينكره، فالقول قول المنكر مع يمينه حتى إذا رد المشتري جارية بعيب أو عبداً بعد القبض، فقال البائع: كنت بعتك مع المبيع غيره، وقال المشتري: بعتني هذا المبيع وحده فالقول للمشتري، وكذا إذا اتفقا على مقدار المبيع واختلفا في مقدار المقبوض بأن قال المشتري بعد قبض المبيع موزوناً وجدته ناقصاً، فالقول للمشتري. (عيني، فتح) للقابض: فلو اختلفا بعد التقابض في عدد المبيع وهو واحد أم متعدد أو في مقدار المقبوض فالقول للمشتري؛ لأنه قابض.
The statement regarding the amount is that of the recipient, whether he is a trustee or a guarantor like the usurper and depositee. Because the seller claims a portion of the price as a debt in his liability and the buyer denies it, so the statement is that of the denier with his oath. So if the buyer returns a slave girl due to a defect or a slave after possession, and the seller says: I sold you something else along with the sold item, and the buyer says: you sold me only this sold item, then the statement is of the buyer. Similarly if they agree on the quantity of the sold item but differ on the quantity of the received item, such as the buyer saying after receiving the sold item by weight: I found it deficient, then the statement is of the buyer. ('Ayni, Fath) For the recipient: if they differ after the exchange of possession regarding the number of the sold item—whether it is one or multiple—or regarding the quantity of the received item, then the statement is of the buyer because he is the recipient.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This is a ruling on evidentiary priority when buyer and seller dispute the amount of goods actually received. The principle is that the buyer's statement is taken because he is the receiver (qabid). This applies whether the buyer is a trustee or a guarantor (like a borrower or depositee). The reasoning given is that the seller claims a debt (remaining price) against the buyer, which the buyer denies; the denier's statement is preferred with his oath. Two examples: if after returning a defective slave, the seller claims he sold additional goods with it, the buyer's denial is believed. Second, if they agree on the total quantity sold but the buyer claims he received less, his word is taken because he is the one who actually received the goods. The phrase 'for the recipient' emphasizes that the buyer's statement prevails in disputes over count or quantity after exchange.

Key Terms
القول للقابض (al-qawl li-l-qābiḍ): The statement (in a dispute) is given to the one who has received delivery (the buyer).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If buyer and seller dispute whether the sold item was one slave or two after delivery, whose statement is believed?
A) The seller's, because he knows what he sold
B) The buyer's, because he is the receiver
C) Both must provide witnesses
D) The judge decides by qur'ah (lot)

Key Takeaway
After delivery, the buyer's statement about the amount received is taken with his oath, because he is the denier of the seller's claim.

Returning Defective Items in a Single Sale: The Rule of Ṣafqah
Ruling when one of two slaves purchased together has a defect after delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on returning defective items in a single transaction (ṣafqah) and explain the dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the return of a single defective slave when only one has been delivered.

Core Idea
In a single sale, you must return all or keep all—splitting the transaction before completion is prohibited.

When you buy two items together for one price, they form a single legal unit (ṣafqah). If you later discover a defect in one, you cannot simply return that one item because that would split the transaction prematurely. The Hanafi school's sound position requires you to return both or keep both. However, Imam Abu Yusuf offered a nuance: if the defective item was the one actually delivered, the transaction is complete for that item, and you may return it alone. This lesson unpacks that ruling and the reasoning behind the difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the general rule
What is the situation? The buyer purchases two slaves in one transaction (ṣafqah). After receiving one slave, a defect is discovered. The general rule: the buyer may take both or return both. He cannot return only the defective one.
2
Understand the legal rationale
Why can't he return only the defective one? Because taking the sound slave would split the transaction (tafrīq al-ṣafqah) before the transaction is complete (qabl al-tamām). The transaction is considered one bargain until both slaves are fully delivered and accepted.
3
Explore the exception: what if the buyer frees or sells the sound slave?
If the buyer after possession frees or sells the sound slave, then he must keep the defective one. This shows that separate action is not allowed and confirms the binding nature of the transaction.
4
Present the dissenting view of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf says: if the defect is in the slave that was actually delivered (the one taken possession of), the transaction is complete for that slave, so the buyer may return that one alone.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its practical consequence
The text explicitly says the sound view (ṣaḥīḥ) is that return of only one is not allowed. This is the operative ruling. In practice, the buyer must either keep both or return both. The difference matters for the buyer's rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اشترى عبدين صفقة وقبض أحدهما . شخص ووجد بأحدهما عيباً أخذهما أو ردّهما، ولو قبضهما . أي أحد العبدين بأحدهما عيباً: لم يعلم به إلا بعد القبض فهو مخير إن شاء أخذ العبدين جميعا أو ردهما. (ط، عيني) أخذهما أو ردهما: [جميعا سواء وجد بغير المقبوض عيبا أو بالآخر، وهو الصحيح وليس له أن يرد المعيب وحده؛ لأن في أخذ السليم تفريقا للصفقة قبل التمام ولو أعتق السليم أو باعه بعد قبضه لزمه الآخر، وعن أبي يوسف: أنه إذا وجد العيب بالمقبوض له أن يرده وحده؛ لأن الصفقة فيه تمت لتناهيها في حق المقبوض، وكذا إذا لم يقبضهما أخذهما أو نقص بيعهما. [رمز الحقائق: ۲۸/۲] (فتح) ثم وجد بأحدهما عيباً ردّ المعيب فقط. ولو وجد ببعض الكيلى أو الوزني عيبا ردّ المشتري كله أو أخذه.
If someone buys two slaves in one transaction and takes possession of one of them, and finds a defect in one of them, he may take both or return both. If he takes possession of both... 'with a defect in one of them': he did not know of it except after possession, so he has a choice: he may take both slaves together or return both. (Tahawi, 'Ayni) 'He may take both or return both': [together, whether the defect is in the one not taken possession of or in the other. This is the correct view. He cannot return the defective one alone because taking the sound one would be splitting the transaction before completion. If he frees the sound one or sells it after possession, the other becomes binding. From Abu Yusuf: if the defect is found in the one taken possession of, he may return it alone, because the transaction is completed with regard to the possessed one. Similarly if he does not take possession of both, he takes both or reduces their price. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 28/2] (Fath) Then if he finds a defect in one of them, he returns only the defective one. And if he finds a defect in a part of a measurable or weighable item, the buyer returns all or takes it.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This section presents the ruling when a buyer purchases two slaves in a single transaction (ṣafqah) and after delivery finds a defect in one. The general ruling is that the buyer must either keep both or return both; he cannot return only the defective one because that would split the transaction before it is complete (qabl al-tamām). The sound opinion ('ṣaḥīḥ') is that both must go together. However, Abu Yusuf has a different view: if the defect is found in the slave that was actually delivered, the buyer may return that one alone, because the transaction is complete for the delivered slave. The text also says that if the buyer frees or sells the sound slave after possession, he must keep the defective one. The comment 'then if he finds a defect in one of them, he returns only the defective one' seems contradictory but refers to a different scenario: after the transaction is completed (e.g., after both are delivered), if a defect is discovered, the defective one alone may be returned. However, the wording here is part of the ongoing discussion; the next sentence transitions to measurable goods.

Key Terms
صفقة (ṣafqah): A single transaction comprising multiple items.
تفريق الصفقة (tafrīq al-ṣafqah): Splitting a single bargain into separate parts.
قبل التمام (qabl al-tamām): Before completion, i.e., before the entire transaction is fully executed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases two slaves together for one price and receives only one. Later he finds a defect in the received slave. What are his options according to the sound Hanafi view?
A) He may return the defective slave alone
B) He may return both slaves or keep both
C) He may return the defective one and keep the other
D) He may demand a reduction in price for the defective one

Key Takeaway
In a single multi-item sale, the buyer cannot return only the defective item before the entire transaction is completed; he must return all or keep all, unless the dissenting view of Abu Yusuf is followed.

Defect in Measurable Goods: Return Whole or Keep Whole
Ruling on defect in part of measurable/weighable goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a defect is found in part of measurable/weighable goods and explain why partial return is not allowed.

Core Idea
A defect in part of a measurable lot forces you to return all or keep all—partial return is impossible.

In sales of goods measured by volume or weight, the law treats the entire lot as a single unit. If only part is defective, you cannot return just that part. This lesson unpacks the reasoning: why a grain of wheat has no individual value, and how the rule varies for separate containers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the goods
Determine if the sold item is kīlī (measurable by volume) or waznī (weighable). This classification triggers the special rule.
Examples: grain, rice, oil, milk.
2
Understand the ruling
The buyer must either return the entire lot or keep it. Partial return of only the defective part is not allowed.
The buyer has a choice, but the choice is between whole return or whole acceptance.
3
Reason for the rule
Measurable/weighable goods are considered as one unit in law and estimation. A single grain has no market value, so splitting the lot would harm the seller.
The ruling is based on the principle that such goods are fungible and their value lies in aggregation.
4
Exception for separate containers
If the goods are in two separate containers (e.g., two separate sacks), each container is treated as a separate unit, analogous to two slaves. The buyer may return only the defective container.
This exception is based on the analogy (qiyas) to the case of two slaves.
5
Distinguish from other goods
For non-measurable goods (e.g., cloth), partial return of the defective part is allowed because the item is not considered a single unit.
The thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference in application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وجد ببعض الكيلى أو الوزني عيبا ردّ المشتري كله أو أخذه، ولو استحق بعضه لم يخير في ردّ ما بقي ولو ثوبا خير، واللبس الكيلي أو الوزني المشتري المعيب رد المعيب فقط : [بحصة وحده لجواز التفريق بعد التمام. (ط)] وقال زفر: له ردهما، وليس له أن یرد وحده؛ لأن فيه تفريق الصفقة، والبائع يتضرر به؛ لأن العادة جرت بضم الجيد إلى الردي، فأشبه ما قبل القبض وخيار الشرط والرؤية قلنا إنه تفريق الصفقة بعد التمام؛ لأن الصفقة تتم بالقبض؛ لأن العيب لا يمنع تمام الصفقة، بخلاف خيار الشرط والرؤية؛ لأن الصفقة لا تتم معهما، وإن كان بعد القبض؛ لعدم تمام الرضا، وتضرر البائع من قبل تدليسه فلا يعتبر في حق المشتري. (عيني، فتح) ولو وجد فيما إذا كان المبيع كيليا أو وزنيا (مسكين) رد كله إلخ وليس له أن يردّ المعيب خاصة ويأخذ البعض، سواء كان الكل في وعاء واحد أو في أوعية، وسواء كان قبل القبض أو بعده؛ لأن المكيل والموزون إذا كان من جنس واحد كشيء واحد حكماً وتقديراً، وإن كان أشياء حقيقة؛ لأن المالية والتقوم في المكيلات والموزونات باعتبار الانضمام والاجتماع؛ إذ الحبة الواحدة ليست بمتقومة حتى لا يجوز بيعها، وقيل: هذا إذا كان الكل في وعاء واحد، وإذا كان في وعائين يكون بمنزلة العبدين حتى يرد الوعاء الذي فيه وجد العيب. (عيني، فتح)
If he finds a defect in part of a measurable or weighable item, the buyer returns all or takes it. And if part of it is usurped, he is not given the choice to return the remainder, except if it is a cloth, he is given choice. And wearing [after knowledge] is acceptance. The buyer [returns] only the defective [part] of a measurable or weighable item: [for its share alone, because splitting after completion is allowed. (Tahawi)] Zufar said: He may return both, and he may not return only the defective one, because that would split the transaction, and the seller is harmed by it because custom attaches good to bad, so it resembles before possession and option of condition and sight. We say: it is splitting after completion, because the transaction is completed with possession, since defect does not prevent completion, unlike the option of condition and sight, because the transaction is not complete with them, even if after possession due to lack of complete consent, and the seller's harm due to his own fraud is not considered against the buyer. ('Ayni, Fath) And if the sold item is measurable or weighable, he returns all etc. He may not return only the defective part and take the rest, whether all is in one container or in containers, and whether before or after possession, because the measurable and weighable if of one type are like one thing juridically and estimation, even if they are multiple things in reality, because value in measurables and weighables is based on aggregation and combination; since a single grain is not of value such that its sale is permissible. It is said: this is if all is in one container; if in two containers, it is like two slaves such that he returns the container where the defect is found. ('Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage deals with defects in measurable (kīlī) or weighable (waznī) goods when only part is defective. The general ruling is that the buyer must return the entire lot or keep it; he cannot return only the defective portion. The reason is that such goods are considered as one unit in law and estimation; a single grain has no market value, so partial return is not possible. However, there is a distinction: if the goods are in two separate containers, they are treated like two slaves, and the buyer may return only the container with the defect. The text also mentions Zufar's view on two slaves: he says the buyer must return both, not only the defective one, to avoid harming the seller. But the Hanafi response is that after possession, splitting is allowed because the transaction is complete, unlike before possession or with options. For measurable goods, the principle is the same: before or after possession, the whole lot must be returned unless they are in separate containers, analogizing to separate slaves.

Key Terms
كيلي (kīlī): Goods measured by volume (e.g., grain).
وزني (waznī): Goods measured by weight (e.g., gold, silver, wheat by weight).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases a sack of wheat (weighable) and after delivery finds that some of the wheat is rotten. What are his options?
A) Return only the rotten part
B) Return the entire sack or keep it
C) Demand a price reduction for the rotten part
D) He can do nothing because he accepted delivery

Key Takeaway
For measurable/weighable goods, a defect in part requires the buyer to return the entire lot or keep it; partial return is invalid unless the goods are in separate containers.
